The Red Page
If Christ Came?
If Christ came to England nowadays, what
would He do? According to J. C. Snaith,
whose novel. The Coming (Chatto and
Wiudus), should create a stir equal to that
of Mr. Britling, a modern Christ would
write a play. That, Snaith shows us in his
absorbing study of John Smith, “the village
idiot,” is the only way open to Him to reach
His modern audience. More, He would get
that play accepted by the leading N’York
manager and have it produced throughout
the world.
The Corning is brilliantly conceived and
written. Snaith is the chameleon of modern
English fiction: with each new r novel one
always wonders whom Snaith is going to be
next. He has been Galsworthy (Broke of
Covenden), Hewlett ( Araminta ), Compton
Mackenzie (The Principal Girl) and a lot
of others in turn; in The Sailor he improved
upon the nautical autobiographical fiction
modelled upon Masefield; in his first book,
Henry Nortlicote, lie was himself; and now
he is imitating that first minor masterpiece.
The Coming, an obvious synonym for Tlie
Advent, is simply set in rural England in
war-time. The narrow-minded Vicar is dis-
tressed by the village loafer, a man of 30,
in frail health, who when well enough to
work assists the village carpenter. This
John Smith is suspected of being a pro-
German, a pacifist, a faith-healer; and the
Vicar is convinced that Smith is also a
blasphemer and a person who should be im-
prisoned under the Defence of the Realm
Act. John’s mother is convinced that he
is the Messiah. So the Vicar considers that
the best thing to be done—and the kindest—
is to have John Smith certified as insane by
two doctors.
One character remarks: “If Jesus
appeared to-day, He would be treated as a
mischievous crank, bullied as a pacifist, and
perhaps be confined in a lunatic asylum.
Think of His reception by the yellow press.
He would find it the same devilish world he
left 2000 years ago.” But even the Vicar
thought that: “The Christ was overdue. If
civilisation, if humanity itself, was to con-
tinue, there must be Divine intervention. '
Organised and bestial hatreds were destroy-
ing the soul of the world. But an age that
had ceased to believe in miracles was now
crying out for a miracle to happen.” And
think of the tragedy if Christ came into a
world in which none should know Him?
The Vicar gains his end : John Smith is
certified insane. He goes to an admirably-
conducted private mental hospital—and he
dies there. This statement is deliberately
balej, since the reader will want to find out
for himself the amazing change that John
Smith wrought in that institution, and the
effect of this remarkable personality upon-
every individual who came in contact with
him. There are some most interesting per-
sonalities in the book. There is Murdwell,
the great inventor, who is perfecting a
development of radio-activity that will revo-
lutionise warfare, and do away with the need
of high explosives “It may become possible
to wipe out a city like London in about a
minute. It may become possible to exter-
minate organic life continent by continent.
... .A child could press a button and punch
a hole through the planet....” Murdwell
opens up delightful possibilities. With liis
committee of Allied scientists he hopes to
put the Central Empires permanently out of
business. The question is which side can
“get in” first; and if Germany harnesses up
the atomic energy first, “the first intimation
the world may have is that a small island
is missing in Europe.”
And there are the two theatrical managers.
English and American, through whose hands
John Smith’s inspired play must pass. “It
isn’t a commercial proposition,” said the
English manager, “and then f here’s tlie Cen-
sor. He’s very shy of religion as a rule;
and.... the parsons won’t like their pitch
being queered... .Yes, it’s a remarkable
play, but I’m afraid it’s pro-Boche. ...”
Dltimately the Lord Chancellor declines to
license the play unless the chief character
and two of the principal scenes are removed !
But unexpectedly the American manager
sees something in the play, though that
something isn’t money. He produces it in
his biggest New York theatre ; and immedi-
ately “a great world-religion bad found a
miraculous birth in the theatre.” It was
translated into every language, produced on
every stage—there was even the probability
of its presentation at Berlin —and the
author was awarded the Nobel Peace Prize!
Evidently John Smith knew his way about T
One wonders what would have happened if
it had come to Australia: a giggle from the
gallery?
The Coming will be discussed. It has not
the uncanny magic and the magnetism of
John Nortlicote; it is more leisurely done.
But it is a keenly interesting study of the
Christ personality, logical and human. And
its climax is not the death of this “village
idiot,” but the amazing scene in the asylum.
And throughout Snaith is reverent, though
in the final scene he just escapes bathos.
Strevjtli. another Billjim transport jour-
nal, provides the usual good fare of verse,
i fake-ads. and humor. A sample: First Pri-
j vate: “Don’t you think the Sisters seem more
reserved than when we were at the base
hospital ?” Second Private: “Sure, reserved
for the officers.”
War
Patrick Maegill is becoming staled to the
war—as who wouldn’t after three years of
it? His latest book, The Brown Brethepn
(Jenkins, per Dymock’s Library), is inter-
esting, but not so interesting as his earlier
works. The note of grimness lias become
weaker: Maegill is taking the war now as a
matter of routine. That is the way, no
doubt, for the soldier; but not for the writer.
Probably he is becoming a better soldier,
though—and his skill with a bayonet matters
more just now than his skill with the pen.
Tlie brown bretliern are a group of khaki
mates in the London Irish. Spudhole Bubb
is a delightful characterisation: the whole
British Army is Spudhole Bubb. And Mac-
gill sheds a new light on many a common*
place subject.
“Despite the distance which a regiment
may travel, tlie soldier is as circumscribed in
his area as the spoke of a limber wheel. The
space is confined, and Spudhole Bubb was no
less a prisoner on tlie march than he had
been in the guard-room. Always the same
mates in front, the same ruddy necks pressed
sturdily back, the same red-brick hands
swinging. across the khaki, the same en-
trenching tool handles waving backwards
and forwards, the same red-haired sergeant
with the tops of his ears pressing- tightly to
his head....” And here is a picture of
Christmas morning after the capture of a
mine-crater. “The rain-swept crater, the
crumbling clay, the fumes of gunpowder, the
dead bodies, tlie monotonous hum of ragtime
choruses, the shells bursting across the top.
the dirty rifles and the dirtier men who
endeavored to clean them.” That touch
about the ragtime choruses could never have
been invented. As for that strange world
apart, the trench: '“The inmates can only
claim for tlie most part a short existence ;
they have degrees of opulence and poverty,
but the former is far removed from those
who are legally heirs to it. And all tlie dwell-
ers in the trench commune share their pro-
perty in common. The word “ours” is on
all lips; save from a few relics of outside
civilisation there is nothing which a man
claims as “mine.” Food and drink and
clothing are “ours,” as also are the parcels
from home, though the men to whom they
are addressed have generally the privilege
of opening them. Money has lost all its
value: for the time being food is not sold
here, and all 'men have to work at the same
job, and they work well, for tlie safety of
their bodies depends upon the labor of their
hands. Again, in the earning times of
peace a soldier may depend upon the sweat
of others for his daily needs ; here in the
trenches he is a Socialist in the highest
sense of the much-abused word. The life of
the Commune is seldom monotonous, its un-
certainty makes it interesting, its novelty
never wanes. The trench has its history,
every dugout a legend, and the shell-riven
alleys of war are steeped in tradition.”
And Maegill can paint a picture with an
economy of strokes —a picture that remains.
As the platoon passed through a ruined
village, the only civilian in sight was a pale
thin woman of 40 who stood at the door of
a ruined estaminet.
“The men stared at the woman, saw her
bowed, head, her ragged clothes, her queer,
weedy form. In her eyes was a look such
as the men had seldom seen. The poor
creature reminded Bowdy of a. dog which he
once had seen prowling round a pond in
which its young had been drowned.
“ ‘Wot’s she doin’ standin’ out in the
street like that?’ said Bubb. ‘She’ll stop a
packet if she’s not careful.’
“ ‘Eyes right,’ came an order from an
officer in front, and the men turned their
eyes towards the woman at the door.
“ ‘Salutin’ ’er. I wonder wot for,’ said
Bubb.
“ ‘’Er four children were killed yesterday
by a shell,’ said somebody in the ranks.
“The woman raised her head and looked
stolidly at the soldiers. Her expression
did not change; perhaps feeling was dead
in her.”
In The Marne — and After (Cassell) Major
A. Corbett-Smith takes us back to an almost
forgotten epoch, ending his story with the
last battle of the “Old Contemptibles”—tlie
first battle of Ypres, when England’s regular
army sacrificed itself and saved the war.
After that momentous victory, so terrible
were the losses that practically the Regulars
may be said to have been wiped out as a
fighting force. The New Armies had to
carry on —and they’ve done it.
Those were the days before the realisation
of the meaning of Hun atrocities. Tlie war
was barely a month old : the British Army
was quite unprepared for the awful revela-
tions that met them everywhere as they
advanced after the Marne. Tlie author
speaks at first hand: he has seen the vic-
tims. When the first Hun prisoners began
to. drift past, Tommy said : “Rummy-lookin’
lot of blighters, aren’t they?” But his tone
of amused affection suddenly changed. He
knew the Hun then, and his opinion was
something like this : !”
Those were the days when the aeroplane
was a mere tentative experiment, when the
arrival of gifts from home was a surprise—
why should strangers at home send out cigar-
cues to soldiers? —when the possibility of
leave was untbought-of—officers who were
offered leave refused, preferring to stick
it out to the end; when everybody
believed the whole show would be over
and the Germans defeated by that first
Christmas: when the regular allowance
of tobacco to officers and men was a
totally unexpected extra. But those were
the days, too, when there were no motor-
ambulances, no entrenching tools—these bad
been chucked away on the retreat, no proper
trenches or dugouts; and, on our side, only,
no high-explosive, no big guns, and practi-
cally no shells for the smaller ones.
Major Corbett-Smith tells many interesting
things of the advance after the Marne: he
shows us the regular Tommy at his best; but
his hnest picture is the last stand of the Old
Army before Ypres. “At the very moment
when Germany was about to hurl the flower
of her army against the meagre British line,
when the enemy purposed to turn every
available gun of an overwhelming strength
upon the handful of men who dared to oppose
his will, there happened an. event as dramatic
and momentous, surely, as any in the war.
....On this day the order was issued that
every field gun in the force must be placed
on a daily allowance of 10 shells only ! In
.other words, a battery was allowed each clay
60 shells to fire when 700 were not sufficient.”
The author applauds the high moral courage
of French in issuing such an order at one of
the most critical moments of the world’s
history. Yet had the British batteries gone
on firing at tlie old rate for a fortnight they
would have had to cease fire altogether. The
plain fact was that there were no more shells,
either in France or in England. “It is well
that the world should hear of this, for surely
it throws another and a still stronger light
upon tlie heroism of the First Seven Divi-
sions during those fateful days. And will the
German staff ever dare to tell the German
people how their armies could not break
through even then? I think not.”
The first battle of Ypres is beginning now
to be recognised as the one of the most im-
portant of the whole war, since by the
defence of an exhausted British army against
a hostile force many times its number, North-
ern France and the Channel were saved. It
was toucli-and-go; yet that thin line held.
llow near that battle was to a defeat the
author graphically describes. After tlie
Headquarters of the Ist and 2nd Divisions,
the brain centres of the Corps, had been
shelled, and the C.O.’s put out of action and
six Staff officers killed, French and Haig
reached the spot. But there was nothing
more to be done. “You may nicture those
two men standing quietly by the great map on
the table of the battered chateau. . . .Not even
may any unit be withdrawn and thrust into
another corner of the line. If tlie breach is
made there can be no repairing. The sea-
coast and ports will be lost to us.” So the
two stood and waited for an hour and a
half. Were the men holding still? Then
came a message over the wires : the Ist and
2nd Divisions were attacking! Quickly
afterwards came messages of the 7th Divi-
sion attacking, of Glieluvelt taken, of the
German right being pushed back ! Was the
tide turning?
The -retaking of Glieluvelt set free some of
the cavalry, the Gth Brigade; and Haig
seized the chance and ordered it to advance.
The astonished brigade mounted and charged
hell-for-leather. “Clean through the wood
they go; nothing can stop that amazing
charge. Through the woods and out into the
open again. On. on!” At last they pull up
as they throw themselves into a gap in the
infantry line. By the late afternoon the
British had won back almost all of their
original positions, and before midnight “tlie
line as held in the morning had practically
been reoccupied.” And the astonishing nature
of that victory, won by a practically routed
army, is evidenced by French’s despatch. A
French cavalry brigade arrived just after the
tide had turned; and French records that
the services of the French -cavalry was dis-
pensed with! “It had been a square fight
between British and German, and the British
Commander-in-Chief was determined that
once the tide had turned the day should be to
his men alone.”
But tlie cost! The Old Regular Army had
fought its last fight, the greatest of all. Of
the 7th Division, which had sailed less than
a month before with 400 officers, only 44 re-
turned ; and of the 12.000 men only 2336
were left. And half of two cavalry divisions
were lost. But the fight was won.
S. G. Fielding writes a feeble prophecy of
Australia, a.d. 2000 (Angus and Robertson.
Sydney), in which he shows us the Federal
Capital of that date, and pictures the politi-
cal community divided into two parties, the
Materialist and the Spiritualistic—or the
Anti-Christian and the Christian. The
Materialist Prime Minister takes a refer-
endum upon the question that Christianity
should be abolished, and the Anti-Christians
win by a large majority. The speeches for
and against are quoted in full —and very dull
speeches they are. That’s about all. What-
ever S. G. Fielding may be, be is not a
prophet in his own country.
According to a writer m The Seven
Arts, a U.S. magazine. Australia possesses
a novelist of genius, hitherto unrecognised.
This is “Henry Ilandel Richardson,” whom
we learn with a tempered surprise is the
pen-name of an Australian woman. The
first novel by this author that this page
remembers meeting is The Fortunes of
Richard Mahony, published last year, re-
viewed 6/12/T7, in which this page re-
marked :
Not only does tlie author write 400 closely-printed
pages about his hero, but he has put into these pages
the minimum of action. And not only is the hero
dull, but most Of the other characters are duller.
Yet one reads on: the scene and the period are inter-
esting, and from the author's pedestrian descriptive
passages and studies of character the reader gets the
illusion of realit v and life. The theme of the story
is more than Richard Mahonv’s life; it is the growth
of Ballarat from a rude mining township to a modern
city.
And in this page’s review of the year it
was characterised as “valuable historical
work in a dull but honest volume.” This
novel is evidently the first part of a trilogy
to be called Australia Felix. But it was not
selected as the best novel of the Australian
year, two other novels being given prece-
dence. Roy Bridges’s The Immortal Dawn
and Gladys Taylor’s The Coo-ee Contingent.
On the title page of the novel two earlier
novels were mentioned, Maurice Quest
(11108) and The (letting of Wisdom (1010).
Apparently neither of these books have
drifted within this page’s ken. The Ameri-
can edition of the former is stated now to
be out of print. We learn that this novel
was met in England with unintelligent and
hostile criticism, though it was appreciated
by a few critics. . But Maurice Guest, a
failure in England’, had a great vogue on
the Continent, being translated into French,
German and Russian. The Seven Arts
critic declares it to be “one of the very great
novels.... one of the most remarkable first
novels ever written.” Apparently it has
no Australian reference, its background
being musical Leipzig of the 18110’s. Another
American critic discusses this novel as “one
of the few masterpieces of modern musical
fiction.”
The Getting of Wisdom is' set in a girls’
school in Australia. The Fortunes of
Richard Mahony received excellent but not
enthusiatic notices in a number of English
papers. And, judged solely by that novel,
since the other novels are apparently un-
procurable in Australia at this date, this
page * sees no need to revise its opinion of
Henry Handel Richardson. There is no
evidence of inspiration in Richard Mahony ,
but the thing is well done in its dull and
discursive way.
The Round Table’s quarterly review of
the Empire (Dec., 1917, 2s. 6d.) treats his-
torically of the present situation in India,
of America’s part in the war, "of Turkey,
Russia and Islam, and of the politics of the
Dominions. All that is said about Australia
is the Labor defeat and the strikes in Sydney
and in Victoria. Well, strikes and referenda
are about all that we specialise in just now.
The Book of Recipes. Formula and Processes
(Modern Printing Co., 246 Elizabeth-street, Melb.,
6d.), contains 375 scientific, chemical and household
recipes. According to the compiler the thrifty house-
wife will find the information herein of great value.
Why not make your own pimple lotion, perfume,
eyelash dye, .soap, pills, boot-polish, ink, anchovy
paste, bug-poison, putty, pickles, axle-grease and
bosom-developer at home ?
Edgar L. Stevens: A very beautiful fairy story
(R. P. 10/1 / *lB>, and one, I feel certain, will
become firmly fixed in the mind of every Australian,
young and old, who reads it, was marred by two
words. When Uncle Billjim said “My oath!” I felt
like kicking him. It spoilt the beautiful impression
of the wonderful old chap he must have been. It
may be a typical Australian expression, but in this
particular story was as out of place as the kitchen
pots would be on the drawing-room mantelpiece.
Had the story been for adults it would not have
mattered much, but because it was for the kid I
cannot but help entering a protest. Uncle Billjim
will become a hero in the eyes of the kid. And
would the writer of the story like a child of his,
say of four or five, to be exclaiming “My oath!”
on any and every occasion? There may not be much
meaning in the use of the words by adults, and
yet eveiy time that the sacred is made profane we
are lowering the standard of nobility of life. The
words we use, and the language we think in, do
mould, to a very great degree; our outlook on life.
The man who spends his time amid the greatest
writers and artists of the age, looks continuously
upon the visions of these great and noble souls,
cannot but fail to see something of that beauty in
the world about him. And this is a thousand times
more true with the children whose minds are not
yet developed. “My oath!” may be a meaningless
expletive, and yet anyone whose soul is attuned- to
the higher things in life cannot but fail to feel it
jarring upon his finer nature. I think if the author
will put himself in the place of a child reading the
story, taking in all the wonderful and fantastical
scenes and characters, he will feel the force of what
I have been endeavoring to express. I feel that the
author in his endeavor to make Uncle Billjim typi-
cally Australian stooped to use something that is
merely superficial to the true character, and in this
instance marred the painting of a masterpiece.

RAZOR.
The generality of men carry out their
shaving operations hurriedly, and with an
old-fashioned razor or with an inferior make
of Safety Razor it is often positive toil.
With a good Safety Razor —such as a
CLEMAK—however, shaving becomes a *
pleasure.
The great advantage possessed by a
CLEMAK over an ordinary razor is that no
stropping is required every time the razor
is used. With the 5/- CLEMAK are seven
blades—one for every day in the week—and
a few minutes at the week-end with a strop
will renew the cutting edges. The blades
are made of the best of steel. The CLEMAK
SAFETY RAZOR is so safe that shaving
may be done in a dim light—or in the dark,
if necessary.
Complete with Seven Bladen and
Stropping Handle.

It makes Muddy Water
Clear in a Few Hours.
The problem of purifying and clarifying Water without the aid of expensive filtering plants
is solved by “Klaro.” “ Klaro” is simple to use, inexpensive, and absolutely sure in its effects.
It is quite harmless, and has no deleterious effects whatever on water treated.
lib. of “Klaro” will clear 3000 gallons of Turbid Water.
One Level Tablespoonful will clear 150 gallons.
One Level Dessertspoonful will clear 75 gallons.
One Level Teaspoonful will clear 37 gallons.
Klaro makes contaminated water safe for drinking. It makes discolored water crystal clear and palatable.
>Simply throw “Klaro” into the Tank or Dam
and see that the water is well stirred. All sediment, mud or fine vegetable matter will be
precipitated to the bottom in from one to two hours.
Every station, farm and country residence should secure a supply of “ Klaro.” Banish the
danger of impure water to both human beings and stock. “Klaro” will not injure boilers if
used as above.
Klaro is sold by all storekeepers in lib., 31b. and 51b. boxes, or if not stocked in your town,
post us 35/- by cheque or postal note, and we will forward a small
case containing 151 b.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Joy of Life.
A dazzling dance the ripples dance,
The sunrays on them glancing;
With swaying grass and jigging leaves
And light o’ day entrancing.
Should Jack and Jill not take their fill
Of merry-footed dancing?
A time shall come when dust and heat
Shall make them tire and falter;
Who’d cry a halt to dancing feet
Deserves the hangman’s halter!
With dove and thrush in tree and bush
A-billing and a-cooing,
And butterflies upon the wing
Their light-o’-loves pursuing,
Should Jack and Jill not take their fill
Of merry-hearted wooing?
A time shall come when golden Youth
To greying Age shall alter;
Who’d cry a halt to love, in truth,
Deserves the hangman’s halter!
With minstrel birds from post and rail
Their liquid music flinging.
And every tree a gold-lit choir
With green cicadas ringing,
Should Jack and Jill not have their fill
Of merry-noted singing?
The voice, at morning, sweet and strong,
At set o r sun may falter;
Who’d cry a halt to joyous song
Deserves the hangman’s halter!
Then love ye one, and love ye all!
And shake each cobwebbed rafter
With thudding foot and rolling stave,
And let what will come after;
With Jack apd Jill, take ye your fill
Of love and life and laughter!
The golden smiles of girl and boy
Salt tears in time shall alter;
Who’d cry a halt to glorious joy
Deserves the hangman’s halter!
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
The Ship of Dawn.
A ship comes out of the east,
Qn a wind of promise blown—
Comes, golden out of the east,
From a realm unknown;
On a sea of the palest cream,
Like the hidden heart of rose,
Like the earliest rose of cream
That the summer shows.
With molten golden sails
Spread, wingwise, blindingly,
Near, and more near, she sails,
In her majesty;
Till the sombre bars of night
Flash warm with a sudden red,
And the flame-pierced black swan, Night,
Floats palely dead.
QUEENSLAND.
J. HOLLOWAY.
Paradise.
If ever beyond the ultimate wall of dream
Should lie a place of refuge sure for me,
A fair, safe place of stream and meads
agleam
With raptured flowers that gem eternity,
Ah, then I shall not mate with sorrow or
Weep out my soul through nights of long
distress;
You will be there with me, and I shall press
Your lips again—you, with God’s pity, for
My loneliness.
So shall we lie enclasped, and I shall kiss
Your eyes and brow (O lady full of
grace!) ;
Shall kiss the sweet limbs that encompass
me, x
And your dear mouth (how sweet a thing
it is !),
What time you, shuddering to me murmur-
ously,
Shall drive away the ghosts of old despair,
While your hands woo me and your exqui-
site hair
Is blown across my face.
N.S.W.
FRANK MORTON.
When Love Grows Stout.
His ideals were slim when she first smiled on
him,
And he noted her elegant figure;
She was slim, she was tall and she carried it
all
Like a ship newly fixed by a rigger.
So he took her for life, and an excellent wife
Was that sylph when she settled to love him;
And he judged every girl by his own precious
pearl
Whom he prized more than angels above him.
Now each year going by heard a new infant
cry
In the nest he had made for his honey,
And Ma’s figure, they say, broadened out in
a way
That her family thought was quite funny.
But the love of her life saw no change in his
wife,
Though he saw other mothers grow bigger.
To her matronly fat he was blind as a bat.
And he still bragged to friends of her figure.
What a crude thiner is Truth! When the
slimness of youth
lias departed, and all is confusion,
You are beaten and done and you’ll miss all
the fun
If vou’ve not got the gift of illusion.
Now that old lover sits throwing lover-like
fits
Round his lady who twice could enfold him.
And if some critic said: “She’s as broad as a
shed!” .. ..
It would take seven policemen to hold him.
N.S.W.

“SAFETY FIRST.”
You, Mr. Car Owner,'have a Tremendous Interest Herel
So Has Every Member of Your Family —
and Every Friend Who Rides in Your Car.
THE RESPONSIBILITY RESTS RIGHT ON YOU!
GOODRICH sends yon a Message
and Points Out a Protection—
SAFETY—
When the road is dry you won’t skid. But
Unfortunately the rain, the mud and the muck are often with us.
And with them on the job, when you make a sharp turn your Tyres
have nothing to grip; they skim over the ooze, and then comes that dizzy,
sickening slide around the corner; and if you don’t go smashing into the
kerb or piling into a ditcli you’re lucky.
The solution is obvious —STOP THE SKID!
But how ? ■ There’s the problem.
GOODRICH has solved it for you —with the GOODRICH SAFETY
TREAD.
Just take a look at those cross-bars on the tread and you’ll get the idea.
It’s simple—one row scrapes away the grease and mud and the next
has a clean, hard space to grip—and you bet it grips. Direction makes no
difference—the skid doesn’t even start.
There’s sense to the principle. It is based on scientific reasoning, and
each bar is there for a real purpose —not for ornamentation or difference.
Join the SAFETY FIRST MOVEMENT —equip with GOODRICH
SAFETY TREADS —ride care-free and safe.
THE GOODRICH SAFETY TREAD TYRE
The Cheapest Insurance You Ever Bought.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £1 6s. 6d. per annum you can have The
Bulletin sent regularly to you every week for a
year, including Special Christmas Number, to any
addkess in Australasia, post paid.

annum.

all Business Communications to be addressed to the

Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be
refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby expressly
reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where practic-
able, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column all
contributions submitted, whether in the form of Politi-
cal, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or
Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects, and
not exceeding two columns in length, or, say, 3000
words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs, tetters
or Neivspaper Clippings. All communications will be
regarded ,as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, sinless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction—i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn-—will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, FEBRUARY 21, 1918.
THE WASTE-AND-BORROW-MORE
GOVERNMENT.
Conclusive evidence that Senator Pearce
is utterly unfit to be Minister for Defence is
furnished by the fact that he is unable to
see that the Commission of Inquiry is not
throwing bouquets at him. The trouble about
Mr. Pearce is simply that he is a weak, dull
man without any business training. As an
early Minister for Defence he was declared
to be a success for the worst of all possible
reasons —the Tite Barnacles of his Depart-
ment said that he was. He did what he was
told to do, and the public was therefore
assured that he was a good fellow. He had
not been three months Minister after war
was in progress when it became clear that
the Department was already in a state of
chaos; and it got rapidly worse instead of
better. Overwhelming evidence of the awful
waste that was going on was forthcoming.
And then the Censorship, which was under
Pearce’s direction, got in its fine work: it
prevented the nublic being informed of the
scandal which was costing not thousands but
millions of pounds. Does the public realise
even now that, under the control of this
Minister, the Censorship was employed for
the purpose of suppressing adverse criticism
of the administration of the Department of
which it forms part? Positively so lately as
15 months ago, on the return of Hughes
from England, The Bulletin was barred
by Mr. Pearce’s Censorship from suggest-
ing that, having been in recent conference
with the military and other heads in Great
Britain, the Prime Minister should replace
Pearce as Minister for Defence! Such
wicked misuse was made of the Censorship
that this instrument for keeping the tax-
payers in the dark and Pearce in his office
prevented The * Bulletin applying to him
and his administration such a mild adjective
as “drifting.” What could be expected from
such an administration? No more and no
less than the Commission has reported: that
the Department has been the refuge of the
ignorant and the incompetent and that
millions have been wasted, most of it in
orders for unnecessary goods.
And bad as the revelations of the Commis-
sion are, worse could be told; and worse
uiis paper tried to tell the public, until it
became tired of trying. For the Commission
l.as only before it the stocks of non-perish-
able goods: the millions of Hounds’ worth of
unprotected clothing and boots, etc. What
it has not before it is the other hundreds of
thousands of pounds’ worth of clothing that
was burned—actually burned—and the
millions of pounds’ worth of food that was
wasted. Air. Pearce in his audacious reply
talks pi the difficulties of dealing with a very
big business suddenly created. The Bue-
eetin will, for its own part, make all allow-
ances for that. But the other waste —the
wicked waste of perishables—has been pro-
ceeding within the last few months, and
attempted protests against it have been cen-
sored. Meantime soldiers have been pro-
vided with food which has been either in-
sufficient or unfit for consumption. Some of
those soldiers have been invalids on their
way back to Australia. Others have been
men under training in Australia. • Senator
Pratten, a man who has been in the food-
stuffs trade most of his life, had not returned
to Australia a fortnight before —not know-
ing that he was supposed to be silent about
such matters—he supplied the House with evi-
dence on this point. And for the mismanage-
ment of its affairs even in the earlier days of
the war the Department is not to be excused.
It is not too much to say that scores of
business men of the very highest rank offered
their services. The Red Cross had only to
say that it wanted help, and business men
were ready to go to the uttermost ends of the
earth to serve it. There is hardly a front-
rank business man in Australia whose ser-
vices were not at the disposal of the Com-
monwealth at home. Men did indeed offer
more than themselves: some of them offered,
as well, their heads of departments. Their
services were “not required.” It suited the
Department better to work with its own staff.
It did not suit certain officers to have, about
them men who knew their business. Why?
There can be only one reason. One compre-
hensive word sums it all up—Boodle! And
poor, dull Pearce, who was completely in
the hands of the men around him. let it all
go on, and probably didn’t know what was
going on. Certainly this paper hasn’t the
faintest suspicion that the poor blunderer
ever made a penny-piece himself.
* * * *
The Commission has dealt with only the
business side of the Department, and thus
far with only one branch of that. When
the whole tale is told, if it ever could be
told, even the careless public might hold its
breath —the public which has been willing
that anything at all should be done so long
as the right political party did it. The pay
office has yet to be reported upon ; and the »
pay office was not only chaos but, in the
words of a detective officer who had been at
work there in the interests of an absent sol-
dier whose pay had been stolen, one State
office was “a den of thieves.” Into that
branch a previous Committee put good work,
doing what the Minister should have done if
he had known how. What that Committee
discovered about maladministration the pub-
lic was never pennitted to know. Nor will
this Commission know what is happening at
Headquarters on the other side, though
on the evidence of returned men there
is equal mismanagement and worse than
mismanagement there also. And on the mili-
tary side it will of course be silent. But
that money has been wasted on “training”
which is not training at all, and that re-
cruiting has been discouraged because of
grievances which have never been redressed,
is as certain as that money has been wasted
on the purely business side.
* * * *
A Board has now been appointed with
George Swinburne as chairman, to re-
organise and carry on the Department’s
business activities; and Brig.-Gen. Rama-
ciotti, a citizen-soldier who formerly did good
work under difficult conditions as N. S.
Wales State Commandant, has been appointed
Administrative Inspector-General. So far
good. But is nothing else to happen? Is
Ministerial responsibility to be a myth? Is
a Alinister's almost unparalleled bungling
and the wicked misuse of the Censorship
to cover that bungling to carry no
punishment? In days before the party
machine was perfected such a report
as that which was published last Saturday,
after being withheld from the public for
nearly four months, would have been accom-
panied naturally by the resignation, if. not
of the Ministry, at any rate of the Alinister
immediately concerned. If Ministers per-
mit Air. Pearce to remain in an , office for
which he has been proved so grossly unfit,
they must share the conseouences. The
public must not hesitate to sweep them away
at the first opportunity, nor waste any
available means to press that opportunity
forward.
SOSH.
The British Revolution rolls forward pon-
derously. Red caps and shouting in the
streets are not to -be on the progrannne, nor
is throat-cutting to play a part; but there
are to be permanent changes as tremendous
as the temporary changes caused by war-
work and military necessity. One of the
latest and most typical statements in this
connection, made by Dr. Addison, Minister
for Reconstruction, is that “house-building
■will no longer be a commercial proposition.”
Before the Kaiser’s outbreak many lands'
talked a lot about Socialism, and here and
there little exhibitions of the animal were
given by States and municipalities ; but Eng-
land, on the whole, struck pretty solidly to in-
dividualism, and it looked as though it would
take a long time to convince J. Bull that
there was anything of practical value in the
preaching of that political and indifktrial
brotherhood at which the Socialist propa-
gandist worked overtime. But the war
altered things. Military needs compelled the
nation to take over many activities from
Private Enterprise; and now even the Tory
opposition to larger co-operation seems to
have waned. There are, it is true, so many
brands of the article called Socialism that
the most contradictory political faiths wear
the same label. All of them, however, have
the one feature in common that they believe
in the substitution of public for private bene-
fit. In a general way, wherever the public
steps in and assumes control of any affair
formerly run for private gain, the movement
may be regarded as Socialistic. But the doc-
trinaire preacher of brotherhood generally
goes a lot further and sees a complete regi-
mentation of the nation or of all the nations,
in which every man and woman works under
the direct command of the State for the
equal benefit of all. Towards the fulfilment
of even this dream of the communist the big
war with its closer organisation for destruc-
tion has gone a long way. If a nation can
be organised for war because by such
organisation it gets the most power out of
itself to defeat the enemy, it seems to follow
directly and logically that a similar sort of
organisation will enable it to get the best
out of itself to feed and clothe and educate
its people and bring them the comfort and
security so often denied under the system of
competitive individualism. So civilisation—
the kindlier civilisation that means good food
for the children and the abolition of physical
misery—appears to be getting forward on
the old powder-cart; and the revolution is all
the time as quiet as a rabbit, and without
one hint of the tiger claws which for drama-
tic effect it should possess.
Of communistic Socialism among civilised
peoples there have been two examples in
history, and both have ended in failure,
though not necessarily for any fault of their
own. The first of these was the experiment
somewhere about 40 a.I). of the Christians
in and about Jerusalem. These new religion-
ists, with their doctrine of brotherly love,
decided to have all things in common and to
abolish all individual rights in property. The
details of the scheme are not known ; but it
is known that the new system -was inaugu-
rated with bickerings and discontent, and
that within a very few years the Judaean
Christians had as a community sunk into
such poverty that alms for their support had
to be collected as far afield as Ephesus and
Corinth. Of an altogether different kind was
the communism under the Inca power in Peru.
There the ruling Ten Thousand of the “Chil-
dren of the Sun” were more or less individu-
alists, though their duties to the State were
tolerably well defined and very well observed ;
but the millions of the common people in the
bisr empire were on a footing of absolute
equality of service and reward. Work was
apportioned for every individual and food
and clothing supplied from the public stores.
Over a thousand miles of territory there was
not one man, woman or child, outside the
Inca nobility, whose personal description,
food and clothing for the year, dwelling
arrangements and record of labor were not
‘known to the central administration. Such
a regimentation was, of course, secured under
a despotism; but. it was a kindly despotism
that made for the greatest good of the great-
est number. The inrush of the Spaniards,
the massacre that followed, and the plunder-
ing of the shrines and cities brought the sys-
tem to a sudden and inglorious end ; but the
possibilities of the thing had been proved to
a point, and its record remains. Yet the
Inca Government does not throw much light
on the problem of communistic effort to-day.
Such a mighty organisation as rendered that
wide-spread equality of prosperity possible
is difficult to imagine under democratic
rule; and the failure of the Early Christian
Socialists warns us against the very system
which under the Incas seemed to succeed.
The theory of the thins: may be perfectly
satisfactory. The moral force of the doc-
trine of “all for each and each for all” is
beyond question. But the machine by which
the idea could be put in State-wide practice
is a complicated one to build and a difficult
one to run. Leaving out the possibility of
miracles, the only way in which a commu-
nism could come into being would be under
an iron despotism that gave place to demo-
cratic rule only when its object was accom-
plished.
But keen as the street-corner orators of
Europe are to see the vast experiment, and
impatient as they are of half-measures
which seem to retard the movement, most of
the things which the most advanced Social-
ist of the most advanced group demands
could -sbe obtained by the laying down of
certain great, broad principles that woul,d
gradually turn the world upside down with-
out shaking any of the essentials of human
society out of place. In the long run a prin-
ciple is always more than a machine; and
its very nature is such that it will create
all the machinery it requires. State ad-
vances in a Socialistic direction, as seen in
Australasia, are not such as can arouse
much enthusiasm. In any case, a universal
system by which a man might become a
mere cog in an ever turning wheel is a
depressing thought; and most men would
rather,pay the penalty of occasional hunger
and frequent anxiety for their individual
freedom than agree to it. To preserve that
individual freedom while,, extending co-opera-
tive effort in every possible direction is
something which does not seem to be beyond
the bounds of human ingenuity. Simple
systems of profit-sharing between employer
and employed have given more incentive to
effort than the big, blundering attempts of
States to conduct business enterprises. It
is impossible to leap at a bound over all
the. barriers of thought raised by long cen-
turies of practice along particular lines.
The slave still works under the whip, even
though the whip be of necessity rather than
of 'leather; and nothing is more certain than
that he will loaf if the driving force be
removed, unless reward for service takes the
place of punishment, for its neglect. Let
the scheme of reward and betterment of con-
ditions for things really done lie at the base
of the new revolution. In that direction
is safety. Equality of opportunity is not
enough. Let there be added certainty of
success if the effort is maintained. Every
country in the world is capable of a develop-
ment that will lift all its people to a level
of comfort never dreamt of in the past.
With united effort there can be produced
enough to satisfy every need. If every
family were lifted to the degree of comfort
now represented by, say, £2OOO a year, the
fact that somebody was a millionaire would
really matter nothing. That millionaire
might be curious and interesting; hut he
could not diminish the food on the breakfast
table of another, nor eat more and better
food than the poorest citizen. Ilis clothes
and his furniture might be finer than some-
one else’s in a freak way; but the poorest
citizen’s would be so good that it could not
matter. So the world’s hope lies in build-
ing up rather than in knocking down; and
if after the great era of war comes the great
era of work, the shooting may have been
worth while. Socialism will advance; but
its strength lies in getting the whole force
of the world’s possible labor thrown into
the world’s task. Equality and equal dis-
tribution of poverty would be a poor ideal
to strive for.
SOME REFLECTIONS ABOUT
CHARITY.
A familiar verse of Scripture sets up three
virtues in a row for special admiration —
faith, hope and charity, and it adds that the
greatest of these is charity. Certainly it
is the most profitable, and writes most fre-
quently to the papers. It is never quite at
a loss for a peg to hang itself on, but it has
its good times and its bad ones, and some
years are fat while others are mere skin and
bone. There are even occasions when the
professional philanthropist has to fall back
on our well-worn colored brother, who is still
supposed .to be crying out for clothes and
instruction at Borrioboola-Gha, on the left
bank of the Niger. On the other hand there
are the extra-rich happenings of drought,
inundation, lack of work, disease and, above
all, war, when the business of being good to
the afflicted flourishes exceedingly. Just
now the injured soldier, the dead soldier’s
widow, the absent soldier’s wife and the
children thereof are useful properties. Ben-
evolence of the cheaper sort pastes its adver-
tisement on their door till space gives out.
A good deal of benevolence smells doubt-
fully and ought to be sniffed at by the police.
Leaving aside a few concerns which are
above suspicion—a Lord Mayor’s Fund, for
instance, which accounts in the most public
manner for all receipts and expenditures—
there is a lamentable tail to the business.
The tail looks so like pure adventure that
the naked eye can hardly tell the difference.
And there is a debatable land between the
head and the tail of the business. Even of
the reliable movements there are perhaps
too many. One big fund is quite as good
as ten small ones of the same aggregate
amount, and the fewer funds there are the
less dazed is the brain of the community.
Among the multitude of appeals the igno-
rant citizen may too often send his little bit
to some individual who is merely raising the
price of a piano or a suburban cottage, and
that person requires discouragement. In one
way, no doubt, 10 honest funds are better
than one of the same size. They supply
limelight to good people where otherwise
there wouldn’t be enough dazzle to go round,
and no one can begrudge the bubble adver-
tisement to a well-meaning fellow creature.
The real drawback is that they raise a dust
which covers many dubiosities. There is the
impassioned promoter who specialises in
some side-line, perhaps boots for the step-
daughters of non-commissioned officers who
were gassed on the road to Jericho. lie
issues no elaborate accounts, and his audi-
tor’s certificate is scarce. Very few people
seem to have heard of him before, but it is
vaguely understood that he has gladdened
many hearts and shifted many* burdens. Also
there is an impression that an influential
committee is behind him ; possibly it is so
far behind that it has passed out of sight.
Again there is the hazy affair which looks
highly respectable and is backed by influen-
tial names. Probably a large number of the
owners of the names have dropped the busi-
ness in the rush. Possibly a number can’t
remember having ever lent their names. One
robust person is usually the whole affair to
all intents and purposes, from the roof down
to the petty cash. This enterprise may be
beyond reproach, but it is untidy. Again
there is a class who collect among their
friends and acquaintances, also among their
friends’ acquaintances. There is no office,
no recognised name and no list of officials.
Any statement of accounts is presented by
mouth when the promoter happens to meet
an inquiring patron. And there is the beam-
ing girl collector at the door—-a handmaiden
who sometimes appears to disremeffiber even
the name of her fund and calls it indiscrim-
inately the Military Trunk or the War
Loan. Her essential assets are cheek, lack
of principle, a smiling countenance, a decent
costume and a collecting-book, or, by way of
giving her the benefit of the doubt, it may
be said that these are her essential assets
unless she is more reliable than she looks.
If her intentions are good she plainly re-
quires a strenuous mother to cane her and
put her to bed for conduct calculated to
bring the family into mockery. This brand
of philanthropist ought to be sft. 3in. in
height, aged anywhere between 17 and 24,
reasonably plump in the corset region, and
armed with a sweet voice, a soft complexion
and a wealth of golden hair. Her branch of
industry doesn’t even involve the cost of a
telephone. She doesn’t offer to give her
name, and if asked for it —well, by the time
some wearily inquisitive person begins to
search the papers to see how much the
vision collected, he Inis probably forgotten
whether she w r as Brown or Smith. She
may possibly be armed with credentials from
the church —perhaps from a parson who
doesn’t exist or a priest wflio never heard of
her.
* * * *
One weary hanger-on to the cause is in a
category by himself. He seeks no money, he
isn’t taking the right way to get limelight,
and if he has an eye on possible votes at
next election he isn’t taking the right way
to get them either. He is sometimes in Par-
liament and sometimes, otherwise.' Credulity
is his strong pointhe is full of available
enthusiasms and is liable to be switched on
to any issue at short notice. If he is taking
a turn at the sectarian row he is prepared
to swear, in his excitement, that he person-


ally saw the Pope, one morning about four
o’clock, setting fire to the wooden tenemen
of a staunch Protestant at Collingwoo .
This well-meaning party is apt to take up
the cause of alleged military widows wi
are starving on 245. or £3 10s. per week,
and are also treated with gross incivility.
A haggard department spends a lot ot
trouble in proving that some of them don t
exist and that the rest are receiving £- lis.
lid. per week or something like that. J-jns
time-wasting pnilanthropist is corroborated
in his wild career by a colleague of a differ-
ent kind —one who has no delusions, and
who wouldn’t turn a hair if the Pope burned
down all Collingwood and himself to boot.
The corroborative nuisance is simply against
the Government on general principles and
against anybody in authority. Both lie and
his brother who saw the Pope committing
arson have a certain vogue; they do a little
to stimulate collections and smooth the path
of the collector by showing the urgent need
for more money. Sometimes weird and pos-
sibly quite unreliable reports are circulated
re military ladies who collect the regular
official stipend and are also on three funds
at once; but these arouse little interest. It
isn’t philanthropic to crush the poor and
the oppressed, even if they are on three
funds at once; and rumors of the sort
wouldn’t help along the collection business.
If they were widely spread and investigated
they would furnish grumpy folk with a pre-
text for saying that the administration of
the funds requires looking into, and that
perhaps the old limelighters had better re-
sign and let new people take a turn.
No reasonable person would demand that
charity should be abolished. It is about, as
henest as the average—more so than politics
and quite as much so as the liquor trade.
It supplies employment, amusement, adver-
tisement, jobs, haloes, pocket-money,
healthy exercise for fraudulent and other
collectors, and it also relieves a lot of dis-
tress. Many a woman struck by undeserved
misfortune has got a new outfit out of the
donations of the charitable, and the person
who raised the money for her has got a new
outfit also. No healthy-minded individual
with cash to spare objects to being taken in
occasionally by the holiest of human emo-
tions. But he does object bitterly to the
slipshod character of some of the devices
he meets with, and gives way to anger at
being taken for a complete ass. He says
that “Samuel Brownjoiinson, honorary
manager,” doesn’t suggest a real individual ;
that 29,726, G.P.0., Bankstown, is an un-
convincing address, and that hardly any-
thing is made public save that Samuel
wants money. It might perhaps be a good
idea to enact that every charitable movement
should be registered, that it should have a
fixed address, a license granted by the Gov-
ernment statistician, and a regular state-
ment of accounts accompanied by vouchers,
these to be supplied periodically to the
same official. The statement might be annual
or semi-annual in the case of the concern
which is above suspicion, as Cesar’s wife
ought to have been, but wasn’t, and monthly
in that of the affair which has got its per-
mit by the skin of its teeth and which is
half suspected of devising a midnight flit-
ting. This might be rough on some slipshod
Christian flappers who meant well but had
never heard of bookkeeping by double entry;
yet in every branch of industry the unfit are
liable to elimination. Probably the slip-
shod flapper would be allowed the option of
a fine for her first offence.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Troubles of Another Wordy
Welshman.
The Lloyd George Government is getting
into broken water, and unless something in
the nature of a miracle happens John Bull
will soon find himself with a large-sized poli-
tical disturbance on his premises. The Gov-
ernment has all along been an experiment.
But it has done big things; and had the
war gone according to schedule the combina-
tion might have survived until the end. The
war has, unfortunately, not gone that way.
Sharp divisions of opinion have therefore
arisen in Britain both as to the conduct of
operations in the field and as to the terms upon
which peace might be made. These, running
for the most part along the lines of party
cleavage, have damaged the foundation on
which the Ministerial outfit rested. And, as
was inevitable from the nature of the busi-
ness in hand, the general administration of
the Government has provoked many enmities.
Now, with the vast changes that have taken
place in the military situation and the in-
creasing strain put upon Britain, the
place of the Government naturally enough
becomes uncertain. The Lansdowne letter
of a few weeks ago was ominous. The
changed tone of Lloyd George’s recent
address to Berlin had a meaning that was
unmistakable. The resignation of Carson
the other day was explained in terms having
little if any point. The critical attitude of
the Liberal and Labor sections in the House
of Commons to the Prime Minister has been
more definite lately than ever. It is impossi-
ble at this distance to judare the merits of
last week’s uproar about the War Council
at A r ersailles. The issues are so heavily
obscured as to make the cabled accounts of
the controversy almost incomprehensible.
The probability is that even in the House of
Commons itself all the cards are not on the
table, and that the debate of the other day,
in which Lloyd George got a very unfriendly
hearing, was merely the camouflage covering
considerations of more vital significance than
those* touched upon. One possible construc-
tion to place upon the faint echoes of fact
contained in the news lately received from
London is that the peace movement is more
alive than it was six months ago. To the
Vatican is attributed the remark that “the
seeds are sprouting.” There are many in-
dications of this. The collapse of the Lloyd
George Government could be looked on as
confirmatory evidence of something of the
kind being in progress. The foundering of
uussia is a fact of enormous significance.
The recent speeches of Woodrow Wilson,
Count Czernin, Lloyd George and even of
the Prussian Chancellor are all suggestive of
peace hems' at least as much thought about
as slaughter. And as the Lloyd George
Ministry is solely a Win-the-War coalition,
one of the first signs of there being need to
consider something else might be seen in a
readjustment of the Ministry on new lines.
Meanwhile if anything went wrong on the
Western Front Lloyd George would prob-
ably go overboard inside half an hour.
That Blessed Word Organisation.
At Melbourne Athenaeum Prime Minister
Hughes has delivered two columns of impas-
sioned circumlocution about what should be
done to keep up this country’s end of the log
when the war of arms is over and the raising
of the blockades enables the war of trade to
resume with renewed energy. He said that
the Ministry had considered the scheme
which he outlined three months ago in Syd-
ney, and was prepared to put it into operation
at once. So alleged this breathless yet lin-
gering shipbuilder who hasn’t managed to
build one vessel in four years. His hazy
scheme is to get into motion so suddenly that
no one will see its heels for dust. Tho
matter, it seems, is one for organisation,
and this is how the organisation is to
organise:—
(1) The unit of the scheme will be the association
representing each industry—
when the industry isn’t on strike or being
eaten up by cheaper and better organised
and older established foreign rivals —
primary and secondary. This will be composed of all
the producers or manufacturers of an industry form-
ing themselves into an association.
How they . will come together, and keep
together, and what they will do when they
are together, and what power they will have
to do anything the great circumlocutionist
sayeth not.
(2) There will be a general council of commerce
and industry composed of representatives from the
various associations.
Nothing said about its authority. Appar-
ently it is simply to talk itself to death and
then attend its own burial.
(3) There will be a Department of Commerce —
Which will provide some billets —
and a Minister for Commerce and Industry—
which will mean another billet.
A first-class business man will be appointed as
permanent head of the department—
at a good salary.
There will be joined with him other experts—
at more good salaries —
representing primary and secondary industries.
Thus there will be presumably a primary
expert to represent the surplus wheat which
isn’t sent away in ships and a secondary
expert to represent the ships which don’t
carry away l the wheat. And apparently none
of these persons will have any concrete
authority whatsoever.
(4) The Science and Industry Bureau placed on a
permanent basis and thoroughly staffed —
seems like quite a lot of paid billets here —
and equipped—
equipped doubtless with all the upholstery
of the season —
will be an integral part of the organisation, and
be at the disposal of the Department of Commerce
and Industry—
but as it isn’t shown that either will have
any specific power or duty, this suggests a
wraith disposing of a shadow —
and of the various industrial associations. (5) Trade
representatives will be appointed in the principal
oversea markets.
And that should mean at least 20 billets at
£lOOO a year each —perhaps 50 billets. And
then there will be staffs and office expenses
for the trade representatives. Yet oversea
markets liat'e shown themselves ready to buy
every old thing we have to sell without the
interference of highly-paid commissioners at
Damascus or Timbuctoo, provided we could
deliver the goods. Therefore why this find-
ing of pleasant jobs for politicians and their
friends? It is a costly business to have a
Government which knows no remedy for
anything save the appointment of still
another lot of experts, secretaries, commis-
sioners, experts, clerks, office-boys, office-
cleaners, office cats, night-watchmen, door-
openers, door-bangers, travellers, agents,
advisers, surveyors, appraisers and the like,
with expensive premises for each gang, and a
powerless, high cockalorum in the principal
room, and carpets, desks, stationery, fire-
buckets, libraries full of works on bee-culture
and ancient history, also free railway passes,
coal-scuttles and instructions to get to work
and organise something or anything or
nothing like the very Devil, and then report
to a special Board. Whereupon the Chief
Paid Buzzer of the special Board expresses
his deep satisfaction, and the Stipendiary
Bumble-bee approves of what has been done,
and the matter is filed by the Tape Office and
the Sealing-wax Department. And in the
fulness of years and the long process of cir-
cumnavigation everything suddenly remains
as it was.
Arbitration in the AVorker’s Kitchen.
There is one clause in Beeby’s Arbitration
Amendment Bill now being wrangled over in
N. S. Wales Assembly which, owing to the
promise it gives of a laugh, almost reconciles
The Bulletin to the whole measure. This
is the section which provides:—
The Court shall in the month of December in each
year declare what shall be the living wage for the
succeeding year for adult male workers and female
workers respectively, and no industrial agreement
shall be entered into and no award made for wages
lower than such living wage.
No mention is made of wages fixed by pre-
vious awards or of agreements automatically
advancing to keep pace with the living wage
if it crawls up during their currency. Pre-
sumably this is left out since such a provi-
sion would be incomplete without a comple-
mentary section providing for an automatic
fall in wages if the living wage underwent
reduction, and that, of course, would be the
very devil. Nor can this paper discover any-
thing in the clause to make it incumbent
upon employers who are not working under
awards to pay the living wage laid down by
the Court before having its Christmas dinner.
Neither does the most minute search reveal
any indication of what the term “lifing
wage” is supposed to mean—whether there
is to be one datum line drawn by the Court
for all workers, or whether a living wage is
to be determined for each particular indus-
try. And if anyone connected with the
Legislature is able to find direction, formula,
hint or indication in the Bill concerning the
basis on which wages are to be fixed at 5,10.
50 or 90 per cent, above the living wage in
different industries, such person has keener
eyesight than this writer. As much because
of what is not in the clause as of what it
actually contains The Bulletin is moved to
propose three cheers; for it has long yearned
to see a Court at work fixing a hard-and-fast
living wage, which the man who works shall
consider to be the foundation of his share in
the earnings of industry—has yearned to see
the problem brought from the region of
blither to the realm of practice. One or two
attempts have been made by Arbitration
Courts to define what a living wage implies.
“That which will enable a man to keep a
wife and three children in reasonable com-
fort” is the nearest approach to a compre-
hensible definition yet recorded ; and “reason-
aole comfort” may mean £2 a week or £2O.
The truth is that as soon as you try to
interpret the term “living wage,” the stan-
dard of living is as hard to keep out of the
controversy as an eel out of the wet. This
paper has, for its own part, never been able
to understand what the cost of living has got
to do with wages. To pin men and women
down to a fixed point of income and say,
“That is a fair thing because you can live on
it,” seems to The Bulletin to be a step
towards bringing a wage-paying commercial-
ism nearer the point of slavery than ever.
The wages of a man should be governed by
the value of his labor. The margin by which
it varies from this is the measure of the pro-
ducer’s exploitation, whether his work be
manual or mental, practical or theoretical.
So far as it may prevent gross forms of
sweating, determination of the “living wage”
may be temporarily useful, in a small way.
So far as a means of helping the general
body of active wage-earners in their double
struggle against fat-headed politicians and
hungry middlemen, it is worth next to
nothing. In fact, it may be worth less.
Still,, the snectacle of the Court grappling
with the job contemplated bv Beeby will be
a joyous circumstance in days which may
otherwise be oeastly solemn.

How to Pay a Dog anil Bark Yourself.
“We have only to imagine a great public agita-
tion arising, with meetings being held everywhere to
enforce and advertise a deep public conviction, and
the pressure upon the Government would become
irresistible.” —S. M. Herald, on St. Valentine’s Day
re Prime Minister Hughes's local shipbuilding drkam.
That kind of support is little less than
brutal. It implies that the Government is
merely making a hollow pretence of an
intention to build ships, and will only yield
to “irresistible pressure.” Everybody yields
to “irresistible pressure,” for nobody can do
otherwise, but it is unkind to hint that the
Australian Cabinet is hanging on by the
skin of its teeth to prevent an essential
local industry being started and our millions
of tons of decaying victuals being sent to the
hungry folks in England, and will only get
to real work when it finds itself shoved more
than half-way over the precipice, at the
bottom of which is oblivion and loss of
salary. Why should the nation pay a dog
and bark itself*? Why should it have to
leave its work that it may attend meetings
and pass resolutions and walk in processions,
and wave banners, and post up bills, all to
save its greatest agricultural industry from
being ruined to the advantage of Germany?
It can get loans, unpayable railways,
Norton-csmitii agreements, useless trade
commissioners, new departments of all sorts,
motor-cars for Ministers, luxurious trips to
England for the same and a thousand other
things without putting on all this “pressure.”
Even a new Zoo, not legally authorised by
the Works Committee, drops like the gentle
dew from Heaven without any huge national
demand, and the Sydney /,oo cost as much
as would have been required to build five
good wheat and rabbit carriers for service
on the sea. No irresistible shcve was needed
when the Prime Minister bought a few ships
in England and ordered a few in America —
but" then that was a matter of encouraging
and paying for other people’s industry, and
when it is a case of feeding the great im-
porting craze no pressure is required. In
fact, the Government couldn’t be held back
if it was tied to a kedge anchor. How much
German influence has been at work and how
much German money has been spent to
throw unaccountable obstacles in the path
of the job by which Australia could have
given more help to the Allies than by all
other jobs nut together no one can say. Per-
haps bought-and-paid-for obstacles weren’t
wanted. The Dictator of Australia could
manage without them. He is undiplomatic
except in matters of Parliamentary manage-
ment, and his capacity for blundering into one
difficulty after another with the unions has
been amazing. He is sluggish, and when it
is a matter of holding consultations and
conferences to clear up difficulties it is a
pathetically long time between drinks. He
is timid, and apparently the responsibility
of starting a cheap and simple new business
in which definite results must be promptly
shown or ignominious failure put on record,
reduces him to a cold perspiration. Likewise
he is an importer by instinct-same as the
K. M. Herald. So the easy and not expen-
sive business of running up cargo ships has
been talked about till it looms in the public
eye like something ruinous and gigantic—
something like a dangerous lunatic to be
approached with caution or an unchained
gorilla which roams around at night eating
infants.
“It All Comes Back to the Tariff.”
From Australia’s pioneer ironmaster, Wm.
Sandford -
Referring to the paragraph under the above head-
ing in The Bulletin (14/2/’18), I have been
through the United States from New York to San
Francisco, stopping at the principal cities, and
endorse Major Hay’s words: “The United States is one
of the most advanced countries in the world.” And
out of my experience I repeat The Bulletin's com-
ment: “It all comes from the tariff.” Had Aus-
tralia adopted the United States policy of High Pro-
tection when The Bulletin first advocated it, she
would not be in the backward state that she is in
to-day. Take, for instance, the shipbuilding in-
dustry. Had Australia been part of the United
States the Parramatta River would be to-day crowded
with ships in course of construction, and built from
our home-made plates and sections.
Before me is the speech made by our great man
David Buchanan, at the Victoria Theatre, in Sydney,
on March 27, 1880. In concluding it he said:
“Never let this great fact be absent from your minds
-—open ports mean work for the stranger and for-
eigner, and poverty and idleness for yourselves,
accompanied by stagnation and national decay.” It
would be easy to show that the policy of Australia
ever since Buchanan spoke has been to look after
the stranger and foreigner, and allow Australians to
go unprovided with work. Take the Year Book of
Australia (No. 10), just published: you will find it
therein stated that the import? from countries outside
of Australia for the year 1915-10 amounted to
£77,521,142. The imports for the six years ending
1916 amounted to the enormous sum of three hundred
and sixty-seven million pounds—nearly all partly
manufactured and manufactured goods. That shows
what a big market our home market is.
The proportion of imports into Australia from
U.S.A. in 1886 was 6.11 p.c. ; this was increased to
32,35 p.c. in 1915-16. But the proportion from the
United Kingdom had decreased from 73.37 p.c. in
1886 to 51.26 p.c. in 1915-16. The decrease from
Great Britain and the increase from America are
entirely owing to the advantages given to America by
its tariff.
We know how various industries have suffered in
this country for want of a scientific tariff since
David Buchanan spoke. But. what will it be when
Japan and the United States, with their scientific
plant and brains, come into full competition with
the unprotected workmen of Australia?
Building Industries by the Hot-air
Process.
Dear Bulletin, — One more drove of
public unaccountables is the Primary
Industries Development League of Syd-
ney with a president, two treasureis,
live vice-presidents, and a varied and
numerous council. It includes a special
councillor to promote meat, one to push on
wool, another to gladden- wine with a leg
up, ditto as regards fruit, cow produce, fish
and forests, and of course a man to give
wheat a real hard shove. Now there are
L 1.000.000 bags of wheat stacked up at N.
S. Wales railway stations and sidings, and
nearly 9,000,000 bags have been on hand
over a year. Unless the* most ancient sec-
tion of this mountain of grain can be shipped
to England before it festers any worse, it
might almost as well be burned. So as
vessels are not available, Arthur, M.L.A.,
suggested that the League might include
shipbuilding among its objects, but was
ruled out on the ground that the blessed
combination didn’t deal with political matters.
Why the ship should be political while its
contents are not, who can tell? Perhaps the
explanation is that any well-meaning old
party can sit around in a meeting and
“encourage” fruit or fish or trees. That is
mere theorv and windy platitudes. But when
a society starts to encourage shipbuilding
it must needs build at least a punt sooner or
later as an excuse for its existence, and that
might make the institution tired, even
breathless. Afore ships are urgent, while
more wheat isn’t; in fact, for lack of means
of carriage grain may soon be more common
than back yards and dust, and more super-
fluous than bigamy. But apparently the
half-pint of brains required to deal with the
country’s biggest want is missing. The
buyer in England is very hungry for want
of wheat, and the grower here is hungry
because the wretched and precarious dole of
4s. per bushel isn’t much to live upon. So a
crowd of city humorists get together in a
city office to fill the stomachs of both parties
with approval, moral support and applause!
They find a lame dog in creat distress and
they give it three cheers. If the thing wasn’t
so well-meaning and pathetic one might
imagine it to be a premeditated scoff at those
who are in misfortune. Incidentally, anyone
may scoff for life on payment of £lO 10s.. and
the office is located in O’Connell-street, Syd-
ney.—Henry Horsecollar.
Price Fixing as It Is but Shouldn’t Be.
From a man who ought to know: —
The contributor (B. 7/2, 1 ”18) who advocates the
adoption by the State of the proprietary-article idea
and the marketing of Government flour, beer, sugar,
corsets, etc., as a cure for the high cost of living,
denounces the present “rule-of-thumb price-fixing”
and pours derision on the “well-meaning amateurs
who run the show.” But what is being done is not
price-fixing. It is a mixture of bluff and laziness;
a sham, a delusion and a piece of leg-pulling that
costs quite £20,000 a year and is about as useful
as a ha’penny with a hole in it. Like the ship-
building “scheme” and the Bendigo pledge it is a
confidence-trick. The pity is that its calculated
uselessness will help to damn future efforts to hold
the balance fairly between buyer and seller, and
furnish hardshell Tories with additional evidence of
the lunacy of interference with the sacred law of
supply and demand.
The first serious attempt to regulate prices was
the appointment by the Holman Labor Government
of the Necessary Commodities Commission in N. S.
Wales. It did good work, but its success was ham-
pered by the artificial State boundaries which limited
the operation of its regulations. Business, on the
other hand, has no concern with these lines on the
map. The Commonwealth came on the scene in
March, 1916, when the price of flour and bread was
fixed under the War Precautions Act, and a Prices
Board (of politicians) appointed. Later, Commis-
sioners were appointed in each State ,to work under
the Board. In August the Cabinet, alarmed at the
forward policy of the Board, took the reins out of
its hands and vested full control in Assistant-Minister
Russell, who is also chairman of the Wheat and
Shipping Boards, and probably the laziest man in
Australian politics. Thereupon the Board resigned.
Ever since the stars and the politicians in their
courses and discourses have fought against any seri-
ous attempt to keep prices off the mountain-tops.
The first Conscription Referendum and the resultant
split gave Ministers other things to think about, and
during those strenuous months Russell was too busy
making speeches, offering to enlist, and finessing
with the P.L.L. to do the work he was paid to do,
with the result that the machine stopped. Then
came the haggling that preceded the Hughes-Cook

was established nearly three years ago. Thousands of farmers and pastoralists have already co-operated, the Company is rapidly growing, and the
future is decidedly promising.
Every Farmer and Pastoralist in Australia Should Support this Cooperative Insurance Co.
