fusion. The Libs, made it quite plain that the
abandonment of real price-fixing was a condition of
coalition. This was agreed to, and just so soon as
the plums were distributed, Hughes wired notice of
dismissal to the Commissioners in the States. Tnis
was in March, 1917. The Ready- Watson disclosures
precipitated a dissolution, and the knife was stayed ;
so that the Commissioners still hold their jobs,
although the sentence of death has never been re-
voked. So the farce goes on. The food riots in Mel-
bourne last year stirred Hughes into action, and he
set the Inter-State Commission on its legs to inquire
into the prices of a number of foodstuffs and com-
modities. Mark you, he did not ask the Commission
to report why the expensive organisation which
already existed to fix prices had failed, because that
would have meant the exposure of his own bungling
and maladministration. The Commission is still
inquiring.
To tell the whole of this sad story would take a
week’s Bulletin, but the public ass, who pays for
it all, may be given a few thistles to chew:—
(1) The Fusion Government has deliberately
adopted a “go-slow” policy with regard to price-
fixing.
(2) Letters and recommendations from the State
Prices Commissioners to the Minister are not even
acknowledged.
(3) In very many cases recommendations are never
considered. In other instances their gazettal is
delayed for months.
(4) No attempt is made to enforce the few prices
fixed. Everyone is chancing prosecution. Traders
know they may disregard the proclamations with im-
punity ; no organisation exists to detect breaches,
and no move has been made to secure the services of
the police for this work. Even should a Commis-
sioner wish to prosecute offenders he is not allowed
to do so, but must refer all cases to Melbourne.
(5) In February of last year a Conference of Com-
missioners held in Melbourne drew up a memoran-
dum to Minister Russell, asking him to end the
unsatisfactory conditions then, and now, existing.
The memorandum was accompanied by some dozen
recommendations giving their suggestions in detail.
The Minister has not yet even acknowledged receipt.
The remedy for all this is not the expenditure of
some millions in the establishment of State corset-
parlors and boot-factories which, presumably, would
be run by the brothers and first-cousins of “well-
meaning amateurs.” It is the establishment of a
system of responsible government—by Parliament, if
you like, but certainly not by War Precautions
Regulations. If political 1.W.W.-ism carried penalties
and defaulting politicians received the same sentence
in “years’ hard” as a defaulting clerk, we might get
along a lot better and a darned sight quicker.
When There Isn’t an Army to Fight.
When Hannibal brought his astonishing
army of hard-fighting mercenaries down from
the Alps and smashed the flower of the
Itoman armies, the big city of his enemies
lay before him. The Carthaginian could
possibly have walked in arid finished things
in one red week ; but he knew the city, knew
the sort of people it contained, and decided
to leave it alone. The Roman in the field
might be broken; the Roman citizen with
his short, ugly sword in his hand and his
back to his own front-door was another propo-
sition. Something of the same kind appears
to be happening in the case of the Kaiser
and the Russian revolution. That the Ger-
man could go ahead and smash things to his
heart’s content seems probable; but he hesi-
tates and enters into a series of long palavers
with the enemy. Trotsky’s remark that a
nation of workmen with rifles in their hands
is a serious proposition has evidently got
him thinking. While there is an opposing
army, that army can be definitely licked.
With luck it can be more than licked; it is
possible to break it up in panic-stricken frag-
ments which may run screaming across the
landscape, carrying the terror of the ap-
proaching victor wherever they go. Armed
individuals are another affair altogether; and
it is possible that the German military
authorities see a stupendous task before them
if they attempt to round up a scattered and
angry revolution which refuses to be licked
and has forgotten the meaning of victory.
The rounding-up of the few Boers left in the
field at the close of the South African calam-
ity kept the British staff awake all night and
every night; and if the Kaiser struck the
same sort of mobile opposition he would
apparently require about 10,000,000 soldiers
to really capture Russia. The Bolshevik
may be mostly talk or mainly whiskers, but
evidently Wilhelm doesn’t like the look of
him.

The New Loan.
The Commonwealth is sending up its plate
for a sixth helping of war loan ; and if the
money is not got that way, it will have to be
found in another. There are two innovations
in the prospectus: (1) a proposal by which
money may be lent either at 41 per cent, free
of taxation or 5 per cent, with liability to
Commonwealth tax; and (2) a conversion
scheme by which with certain limitations 4-j
per cent, stocks may be converted into the
new o per cents. So far as this is meant to
meet the objection to the removal of a vast
sum of capital from the ambit of taxation.
there is nothing in it. The effect will be to
put the greater part of the new loan in the
same, position regarding taxation as the
previous issues, which, in the judgment of
this paper, is a grave mistake, and certain
to be the cause of violent disputation later
on. Boiled down to essentials, the hesitating
step taken bv the Commonwealth amounts to
an offer of higher interest to small investors
and a perpetuation of the system which gives
the big tax-free investor a substantially
greater net return from the smaller rate.
The higher the income the greater the in-
ducement. to sternly resist accepting 5 per
the case of an investor of
x-0.000. At 5 per cent, the return would be
f 1.250 less the present tax, ftiG 10s. 10d., or
£1383 3s. 2d. net. At 41 per cent, the vield
would be £1125. In that case there seems
to be an advantage of £5B 16s. lOd. in paying
income tax.: but a rise of Id. in the tax
would blot it out and an increase of 2d. turn
the balance the other way. That the tax
will increase 2d. and more is as certain as
the multiplication table. Now, take an in-
vestor with £lOO,OOO asking himself which he
will condescend to purchase, bonds at 41
tfree) or 5 (taxable). The income at 5 per
cent, will be £5OOO, less tax £760 18s. Id.:
net return £4230 Is. lid. At 4J free there
would be £4500 to appropriate. Not much
room for doubt there! Then take the case of
a £1,000,000 investor. The taxable bonds
would show net earnings of £37.905; the
untaxed income from 41 would be £45,000.
The amended conditions thus make things a
little better for the small man; but they
leave things far too advantageous for the big
investoi.
The Minor and Major Riots in Europe.
Pending information about the coming Ger-
man push, copious details, with the innards
carefully removed, have been available lately
about a rumpus in England concerning the
War Council at Versailles and changes at
the War Office. Nobody at this distance can
do more than guess at the meaning of it all:
but one important fact is that Robertson,
the Chief of Staff, has retired from his job
after a misunderstanding or its equivalent
with Lloyd Geobge. There is an allegation
that the proceedings are “resented by the
army,” which is nonsense; for who could
ascertain in 24 hours the opinion of the
army? There is the further statement that
the retirement created a sensation in Lon-
don. This is probably true, but it was cer-
tainly not an unexpected sensation. Rob-
ertson’s retirement, if not actually expected,
had for a long while been spoken of as pro-
bable, just as Jelliooe’s had been. Appar-
ently Robertson objected to the Allied Coun-
cil of Versailles and stood for “the free
hand” and “no interference,” and all the
rest of it. On the face of it the inauguration
of a common strategy among the Allies seems
tc have been over-long delayed. It
is certain, too, that the d'emands of
highly-placed soldiers for “free hands”
can easily reach a point where the
choice is between disaster and something
else. There are limitations to “free hands.”
The conduct of a national war is first and
foremost the business of the governing
authority, whether it be a Ministry, a mon-
arch or a committee of Bolsheviks. To that
authority the military arm must always be
responsible, anu always has been—except
where the military arm becomes a dictator,
as in Germany. Whether it would have been
better to follow the Robertson system,
whatever it may be, than to go on the lines
now favored by the Allied Governments,
whatever they may be, is a point to be
decided by later events and the illumination
of past happenings; but it is certain that a
military autocracy must be resisted. Apart
from that, there cannot be much reason to
doubt that the time was pretty nearly ripe
for a change, a reflection which is not made
look any less reasonable by contemplation of
Robertson’s attitude. Unquestionably if the
most intelligent private soldier in the army
disapproved of a stupid superior’s plans,
he would find it a serious offence to lay
down his arms in the face of the enemy.
Plow Russia is getting along nowadays is a
problem as vast as the ultimate fate of the
universe. The other morning, if the cables
are to be believed, the Bear was out of the
proceedings and getting ready to take what
was coming in a humble, contrite spirit.
Next day he had decided to remobilise him-
self fall in several millions upon the
enemy. There was news early in the week
that Germany was hanging out flags of joy
at the consummation of peace. Before this
had time to soak in came information that
the Kaiser’s armies *wsre getting ready to
resume hurting Ivan. Every explanation
made of Russia and the Bolsheviks seems to
call for further explanation, and when this
is forthcoming nothing appears clear except
that it is all wrong. Next week The Bul-
letin is going to try to throw some light
into the murk by printing the nearest
approach possible to an official statement of
the Bolshevik position—the contribution of
Peter Simonoff, nominated as Consul-
General for Australia by the Bolshevik
Government.
A note from near the inside: —
The Holman Ministry last week got nearer to
being blown up by an internal explosion than any
other Government that ever lived to tell the yarn.
The shell was attached to a time-fuse, and Holman
had about the liveliest 24 hours of his life stamping
the fire out. All along there had been a loud aroma
from the wheat-silo jobs, and when Beeby discovered
that there had been omitted from the contract
entered into with Teesdale Smith certain safeguards
protecting the State against loss or excessive pay-
ments for additions, things began to happen. Beeby
wanted to know why, and handed his resignation
to Holman, with an intimation that he could accept
it at once or get the contract made straight, just as
ho pleased. David Storey, another member of the
Ministry, also went up, and a Cabinet meeting of
great vehemence supervened. The end of it all was
that Holman almost within a day had obtained from
Smith an undertaking to sign a new contract and
drop the other one down a well. Whereupon what
the S. M. Herald called an “incident” passed into
oblivion —for the time being. But it shook the
Ministry to the bluchers and acted upon its party
following in the Assembly, already as dejected as
wet hens over the- Brown coal business, very much
like an extra-strong emetic. The silence that fol-
lowed could be heard a mile away ; but anyone who
happened to be a little closer could hear a chorus
of curses in 15 dialects. Why the missing clauses
were left out and why, being left out, the contractor
affably agreed to enter into a contract less favorable
to himself than the one already signed has not been
explained—and most likely never will be. The posi-
tion of the “Nationalists” outside Parliament now
is that when anyone mentions the word contract
everybody rushes to open the window. And the
affection of the regular, dyed-in-the-wool Lib. for the
new lodgers brought in on the llolman-Wade deal
is a moving circumstance, in which humor struggles
hard with pathos.
“Fairfield” in support of “Phthisis” (B.
31/1/’lB and 14/2/T8), re the Mont Park
(Vie.) military sanatorium: —
Lt.-Colonel Tiiwaites must be blind ; if not, he is
being hoodwinked. The statement made by
“Phthisis" was mild in view of the actual facts—
facts known to every adult within the neighborhood
of Mont Park. The Progress Associations have made
representations to the military authorities in regard
to the unrestricted travelling in trains to and from
Mont Park of phthisis patients, all to no purpose.
There have been officers who tried hard to discipline
patients for their own good, and for so doing have
become unpopular and have been quietly removed.
It is quite an open boast at Mont Park: “We will
scon shift you from your possy if you start the regu-
lations stunt.” I have personally visited Mont Park
with organised amusement parties and I have first-
hand knowledge that the facts as stated by
“Phthisis" are only too true. A day—particularly
a pay-day—spent in Heidelberg (the nearest town)
will confirm (ill that has been said and justify one
in saying more.
This is supported by five other contributors.
In spite of the official statement which The
Bulletin was required to publish as an
addendum to the statement made by
“Phthisis” —himself, The Bulletin may
be permitted to say, a man with a fine record
of service—it is obvious that a thorough
inquiry is called for.
Better a week of Sydney than a cyclone
of Mackay.
The art of governing by party has been
pushed to its logical limit in South Aus.
The resignation of Styles made a vacancy
in the Coalition Ministry of Liberals and
Nationals. As he belonged to the latter, it
was agreed that they should appoint his
successor. Thereon W. H. Harvey was selected
by the National members of Parliament, and
“the decision of the meeting was immedi-
ately conveyed to the Premier,” who accepted
him as a colleague as a matter of course.
Harvey has less than three years’ Parlia
mentary experience, but has been a solid
Labor worker for a long time. He becomes
Minister of Education, with Forestry.

honorable members for Parramatta and Balaklava,
and both believe that their efforts on behalf of the
Empire could be doubled with the reward of the
Prime Minister’s salary. Hughes, who is secretly
wearied of the pair, will have to take one or other
to England, though he would like to go alone, not
only to mortgage the reduced limelight that can be
spared for Australia, but also to keep Cook and Watt
so busy watching one another that neither would have
time to enhance his own prestige. That arrangement
would be admirable from Hughes’s point of view, but
it wouldn’t suit Joe Cook, who is willing to take
the trip even at the risk of letting Willie Watt be
Acting Prime Minister. Hughes would prefer Cook
to Watt as a fellow-traveller, for Watt has a rich
bravura speech and disconcerting ability, whereas
Cook, despite a fraudulent air of authority, is aboui
as entertaining as a sexton. Yet talcing Joseph
means leaving Watt, and that is not a fascinating
prospect for a fearfully-shaky Prime Minister who
understands Watt’s objection to a modest place.
There is a further complication in the fact that
Irvine was promised a berth with the delegation that
was expected to leave for England last year. That
promise has not been revoked ; so if Irvine suddenly
mentions that Australia’s voice would be incomplete
without his silvery tenor, the position would be very
interesting indeed. As a compagnon de voyage Cook
is bad enough ; Watt would be worse ; but the pre-
sence in London of the one man who had stood
forthright for Conscription, regardless of personal or
party interests, would make Hughes look like a
Pierrot at the Imperial Council table.
Up till the other day all advertisements,
proclamations and so on bearing the signa-
ture of the Commonwealth Treasurer finished
up with “John Forrest.” Now he signs
“Forrest.” If John gets any more attention
from George V., The Bulletin expects to
notice him going under an alias like a bishop
and announcing himself as Bunbury.
One Bell, of Fairfield, uprose lately at a
Nepean (N.S.YV.) patriotic meeting and said
that he would give half of what he possessed
to improve the lot of soldiers and their
families if the Government would make
everybody else do likewise. Well, this
writer won’t let anybody get ahead of him
in the good cause. He will give everything
l e possesses, including his clothes and boots,
to help on the war, provided the Govern-
ment makes everybody else do the same.
“The Pariah”: Add Bapinba to “D.F.’s”
list of Victorian towns whose names had a
queer origin (B. 7/2/TS). When the Bal-
larat-Colac railway was opened, the railway
bosses told the Weering people that they
would have to choose another name, as there
might be confusion with Weerite, which is
also in the Western District. The locals
invented “a euphonious aboriginal name” by
taking the first syllables of the names of the
three men who represented the riding—Ba-
pin-ba.
Excluding the month’s regime when the
officers of the old Rum Corps ran the show,
Davidson, who arrived in Sydney this week,
is the 26tli Governor appointed to N. S.
Wales since 1788. Of 25 Cocked Hats who
have come and gone Macquarie lasted the
longest—ll years. Fitzroy hung on for nine
years, Gipps for eight, Robinson and Rawson
seven ; Darling, Denison, Young and Loftus.
who kept the poultry, each six. Bligh fin-
ished up in 17 months, and Beauchamp of
the Birth Stain reckoned the time was ripe
to stop taking the cheque a fortnight before
a couple of years had passed.
Fate seemed to be giving things an ironi-
cal twist the other day over in Maoriland
when the newspapers announced the death
by drowning of Harry Bedford, Professor of
Economics and History, at Otago University.
During most of his short, useful life Bed-
ford had persistently preached the virtues of
cold water. He was the son of a journey-
man tailor, and as a kid was given a trial
at the old man’s trade, but threw that up
and sampled farming and blacksmithing, did
the same to them and then turned to law.
He was a brilliant student, and took high
honors at the University Colleges in Auck-
land and Dunedin. Also he . was a youth of
unbounded loquacity, with an unextinguish-
able yearning for politics and the capture of
Richard Seddon’s scalp. At 21 he had a shot
at the Caversham seat in a by-election, but
was defeated by Tom Sidey. In the next
year (1902) he stood at a general election
as an Independent for Dunedin City, run-
ning against the Seddonian ticket of
Millar, Arnold and Barclay, and lo! he
emerged at the top of the. poll with the
biggest vote cast for any candidate in the
Dominion. When the pink little man with a
rudimentary moustache took his seat in the
Reps, he looked like an escaped schoolboy.
He talked like a phonograph for three years,
and then was snuffed out. The rest was
mostly the practice of law and university
work and fulminations against beer. He was
only 41 at the finish.
Salvation Chaplain-Major W. McKenzie,
M.C., got back to Sydney last week after
three years of war, and the soldiers who
knew his extraordinary record made
papers crackle with his praises. A fighting,
singing Scot of enormous vitality, enthusiasm
and pluck, he won the Cross on Gallipoli,
and, what was even better, the affection of
all the Billjims he led and comforted so
naturally and easily. The men of the First
Brigade—what is left of them—swear he
went into the glorious Lone Pine charge
armed with an entrenching tool, and another
story tells he w, on a transport returning
from Gallipoli to Egypt with a thousand
brooding soldiers, an enormous voice roared
the old challenge, “Are we downhearted?”
“No!” yelled the Billjims, and, looking
round, saw it was Mac. “Then let us sing!”
he shouted, and soon “Australia Will Be
There” was rolling defiantly across the sea.

“The Easiest Way” will point its moral at
Melbourne Royal for another week at least.
Its morals are a good bit off, but its moral is
a warning to young women of a prevariea-
tive turn of mind. Laura applies to her pal
Elbe for advice when she is suffering from
the effects of fibs, and Elfie exhorts her to
keep on fibbing, with the result that she gets
badly left at the finish. Muriel Starr makes
spasmodic efforts to realise the possibilities
of the character, but her dressing is a con-
stant contradiction of the sort of girl Frank
Harvey alleges her to be. According to
Harvey, she has ever been the mould of neat-
ness and the glass of style. As a matter of
fact, she wears an untidy air, and her amaz-
ing morning gown in the third act seems cal-
culated to make any well-regulated “protec-
tor” get up and protest. No lawfully-married
man would consent to sit at breakfast with
such a weird, untimely garment, nor would
any tasteful profligate fail to weep if he
strolled into his lost girl’s lodgings and found
her wearing Laura’s skirt and blouse.
* * * *
In this play one perceives the deter-
mination of the ’Murikan machine drama-
tist to “can the old stuff” and intro-
duce a new line of business. The
telephone is conspicuously omitted. No-
body sits at a desk to converse with some
far-away person concerning matters of imme-
diate importance to the plot. A while ago it
was the rule for all the leading characters,
and sometimes the servants, fo talk things of
the first importance into a telephone-receiver
and impart valuable information to the aucli
ence by means of replies to a supposititious
person at the other end. The whole drama
radiated from the ’phone, as it were.
Nothing of this in “The Easiest Way.” Still,
there is a lot of conversing with persons off
the stage. In Act I both Frank Harvey
and Muriel Starr exchange words with a
Voice —the property of the never-apparent
lady who is said to be a demon for playing
bridge. In Act II the Voice of the Invisible
Lodging-house Keeper utters words that
have a direct bearing on the plot; for the
heroine’s relapse into unvirtuousness is
clearly hastened by the bawling landlady
who wants her rent. And in Act 111
naughty Elfie discloses her system of witch-
ery whilst talking in loud tones to Laura,
who is changing her dress in the next room.
The audience is tickled by the news thus
conveyed that Elfie thinks of returning to the
stage because her elderly protector has re-
sumed his old “front-row habit,” and must
oe given the gratification of pointing out his
expensive chorus-lady to his cronies, and say-
ing “That belongs to me." It isn’t probable
that even that sort of Elfie would proclaim
her policy so loudly in a place where others
beside the negro servants might overhear
her; but the dramatist scores his point, which
is to give a conversational air to what is
practically a monologue by the only charac-
ter on the stage—and this without resorting
to the old telephone dodge. The methods of
smart American playmakers seem to breathe
the national desire to have “no flies on it.”
** * *
Popular prices are a recommendation for
a musical show that has some sweetly-melo-
dious moments, and “The Rajah of Shiva-
pore,” at Melbourne Princess’s, is progress-
ing favorably. The shortcomings in the per-
formance and the fictitiousness of the plot
strike one as before, but the music improves
on acquaintance. It is strongest towards the
end. The duet and chorus number (“My
Beloved”) which immediately precedes
“Sword of Mine” is a charming accompani-
ment to Miss Myra Morrison’s dance, but
here the want of “padding” to the opera’s
meagre form is most apparent. The same
young ladies come tripping in with their
little drums who have only a few minutes
earlier been packed off to the harem of the
Rajah, that gloomy potentate who delivers
the author’s Gilbertian jokelets without the
least sense of their humorous intention. The
Rajah, the Ranee and the Emperor, disguised
as a bleating blind beggar, are a painful trio
of exalted personages. Their claim to class
distinction ought to be dismissed with costs.
His nibs with the black beard, however, has
been relieved of the job of exchanging
whacks on the shield with the tall, tuneful
hero whom Alice Bennetto mistakes for a
divinity. A nimble gladiator is now laid on
to knock Lohengrin out, and their mutual
shield-thumping is a quick and noisy episode.
Last Bulletin, by the way, followed the
printer in a strangely-erroneous spelling of
the hero impersonator’s name. He was billed
as Tristram Greene instead of John Quinlan,
so the management informs this conscien-
tious journal. It seems a pity to part with
the old delusion. Tristram Greene sounded
like a name that might become almost
immortal in comic opera. But under the
name of John Quinlan, who played the part
in Sydney, the hero can sing just as satisfac-
torily. And he will talk more gracefully
when he has mastered the gentle art ol
diction.
“Aladdin” is approaching its last lap at
Melbourne King’s. Six more nights after
Friday and Dame Bailey will cease to re-
cover the wonderful lamp in a wonderful
way. The he-lady’s method of getting it
back from the magician is to call him “dear”
and “spounce upon him.” All things con-
sidered, it is a truly womanly way. No good
to call the family cash-producer “brute” and
snort at him. Call him “dear” and spounce
upon him.
“Whittington,” at Melbourne Majesty’s,
announces nothing more definite than last
nights. The only noticeable change of late
is Constance Cayley's new song, instead of
the lamentable ditty known as “Fishing.”
The loss of that utter foolishness would have
been the panto’s gain even though Con-
stance’s new item hadn’t been a lively affair,
supported by the kiddies.

body has seen Ada in vaudeville, and rejoiced
over her, for she is one of the few magnetic
artists who make the audience feel excep-
tionally glad. But everybody has not yet
seen her in “Winnie Brooke,” hence the
need to hurry up and grjib fleeting opportu-
nity by the tail.

ment, but it has the disadvantage of having
too much Vivian. The young actress belongs

young artist who impersonates Lovely
Woman and shows an evident pleasure in
wearing her uncertain garments and acting
as if life were a birthday party. This Julian
is another Apostate, and should be sentenced
to 32 months in a manicure parlor.

“The Garden of Eden”—the new special
turn dropped into the “Samples” revue at
Sydney Tivoli —is a clever animation of the
decorative kind of drawings that appear
from time to time in Vanity Fair and
similar papers. It is carried out boldly and
brilliantly in black and white, and the elabo-
rate dresses of the girl dancers fit in with
the eccentric background. The freak note
is well sustained during the dance and
pantomime, and there are a series of tab-
leaux. Jean Keith is the solo dancer, and
Nellie Leach, in white coat and black bell-
tcppor, is concerned in the pictures. The
varied show is moving easily now and busi-
ness is booming.

Anderson’s “Beanstalk” pantomime, at
Adelaide Tivoli, is having a third week,
which in that town means an unusually sob'd
success. At the Majestic are Clevalli with
his dog, Leeds and Posner, Tilton and West,
and a good all-round show, strongest on the
musical side. In pictures, Olive Thomas
has a Salome dance at West’s, and Theda
Bara is more vampirish than ever at the
Royal in “A Fool There Was”—Hugh
Buckler’s old play filmed. Wondergraph is
particularly strong, with the heroic House
Peters in some brisk fighting, and Mary
Pick ford taking off the boots of the Kaiser
in “The Little American,” which remarkable
play may have been a useful agent in get-
ting her countrymen to realise that it was
time to leave off being neutral and become
human.
Prime Minister Hughes still announces
that if he is an obstacle in the way of union he
will gladly throw up his job. But on Satur-
day he couldn’t resign by reason of the heat,
and it would be irreligious to retire on Sun-
day, and the spell of rain on Monday pre-
vented him going out, and then on Tuesday
there was a high wind and dust. Once,
years ago, Hughes, then Attorney-General,
was in a mixed sort of place, and was liable
to abdicate at any moment, but his cow fell
sick and diverted his attention. The poor,
retiring man has no luck.
Wilson’s Pocket Directory to Sydney and
suburban streets and lanes is in its ltith
year. A feature of it is the 90 maps which
finger-post the way to the most inaccessible
corners. It’s as handy as a policeman.
The German still talks of his partnership
with Gott. And no doubt he believes in it.
What else could he doV Thus far 14,000-
odd non-combatants have been killed as the
direct result of submarines and air and other
raids; and all the slaughter has been on the
one side. The German naturally regards
one-sidedness as the rule of Gott. He gazes
fondly at the war map and is impressed by
the apparent impotence of the Allies to
retaliate in kind; for he naturally reckons
that if Britain could drop 10,000 bombs on
Cologne she would. Ilindenburg’s promises
of heavier and unprecedented punishment of
non-combatants serves to further buck him
up. “Yen ve haf kilt fifty tousand vimmen
und children ve shall segure a goot Sherman
peace,” says the Ilun who is flourishing in
bizness under British rule —in, say, Ger-
man New Guinea, or Sydney.
Put in the fewest possible words the wise
men of Henty and Billabong (N.S.W.) offer
to lend the Government some money provided
it is • spent on an unpromising local rail-
way, and the community at large stands the
loss on working, and. they get 5 per cent,
interest on their loan. Plenty of other
people have put money into the war and
other loans without stipulating for a rail-
way, but they are not wise men from Henty
and Billabong. Brains are grown in those
parts.
Now that Agar Wynne, Vic.’s new Minis-
ter for Railways, disappointingly finds that
“the Railways Commissioners are unable to
suggest economies amounting to more than
£25.000,” he should nose around and see
what he himself can propose. For instance,
there is the department’s affable habit of
granting a free annual holiday-pass over the
railways to its employees and their wives.
Every one of these back-stair payments
ought to be abolished; and until they are
abolished the public will never know what
any particular service costs. It will only
know that, in the aggregate, the expenditure
has a horrible habit of being larger than
was expected. This country’s perks are be-
coming more intolerable than some other
countries’ tips.
“Jonah”: The old Dunmore, which re-
turned to Sydney one day lately in a
frabjious condition after a heavy bump from
John Brown’s tug Champion, seems to have
a hoodoo. First she collided with the
Ivelloe, off Botany. Good-bye, Kelloe; but
the other barge staggered into port. Then
she ran over a naval pinnace in Port Jack-
son, drowning 14 bluejackets. Next she
nosed the Captain Cook, the N. S. Wales
examining steamer in war-time, and was
beached on South Head. Although as slow
as a funeral she is the champion butter-in of
the Pacific.
As it was taught to an old lawyer:—
These are the facts of the action with regard to
the coal monopoly which the A. A. Co. attempted to
establish in N. S. Wales. In the ’forties James
Brown and Alexander Brown, the founders of the
present firm of J. and A. Brown, became entitled
to 60 acres of coal-bearing land near Maitland, the
grant of which contained the usual reservation of all
coal to the Crown. Notwithstanding this embargo,
the Brown brothers “did dig and carry away large
quantities of coal and other minerals from the said
land,” wherefore the Attorney-General of the day
fell upon them with a writ of intrusion, claiming
the coal under the said 60 acres to be lawfully in
the Queen’s possession, and that the Browns had in-
truded into such possession. The case having
been decided with damages Is. against the Browns,
they appealed, and in 1847 the appeal was heard
before the Full Court.
The chief contention by the brothers was that the
reservation was, under a Statute of James 1., a
monopoly, since the Crown, by granting the mines of
coal to one person and denying tnem to others,
might throw the trade in the article into one chan-
nel, to the exclusion of every other person. The
Court decided for the Crown, holding that there was
nothing in the intrinsic operation of the reservation
to create a monopoly, and, although it was true
that by matters extrinsic the reservation might
become an ingredient in the creation of a monopoly,
still that possibility formed no ground for adjudging
a provision void which in itself had no indication
of such consequences.
Looking at the matter calmly, after the lapse of
over half a century, it does seem that the Browns
were doubly armed in that they had their quarrel
just; for surely the arrangement between the Crown
and the A. A. Co. justified the Browns in considering
such to savor of a monopoly.
In the same year (1847) the A. A. Co. appears
to have voluntarily given up its monopoly of the
coal-n,ining; but it was not until 1850 that a Royal
Proclamation appeared here stating that her Majesty
had been graciously pleased to direct that all reser-
vation of coal should be abandoned and that the
grantees of the land in the colony then and hence-
forth should be at liberty to dig and carry away
the coal under their lands. Thus ended the early
coal monopoly.


Beauty, Ladies, Beauty!
Beauty is everywhere a right welcome gyest.
Beauty can afford to laugh at all distinctions.
As culture begets taste, taste begets beauty. Even
as music soothes the soul, so beauty soothes the
heart. ' •
The thought of bea.uty lasts long after other things
have been forgotten.
Is beauty vain because it will fade? Then are
earth’s green robes and Heaven’s light vain.
The beautiful is like sunshine to the world.
A beautiful woman is a paradise to the eyes.
Personal beauty wins almost without anything else.
We must carry the beautiful with us or we find it not.

SOCIETY
MINCHINBURY
“Y.Z.”: M.y friend Wylie, motoring in
Gippsland with his wife and two children,
had the misfortune to drop one of the young-
sters overboard. The boy was a bit knocked
about, and Wylie dashed for the nearest
township and interviewed the first man he
met by the way. “Is there a good doctor in
this place?’’ he asked. “No, thank God!”
was the reply. Wylie subsequently learned
that he had hit upon the undertaker.
THE POINT OF VIEW.
“Wasn’t it disgusting, those two hoys fight-
ing in the vestibule last night!”
“ Depends, darling, whether they were fight-
ing about me or you.”
“303’’: In connection with the Japs’claim
to the Carolines, which they have been
occupying since the war started, one of
their professors announces the discovery
that his people used to hold possession in
the long ago. The cable refers to “traces”
he has found. As likely as not he has been
exploring the ruins of the mysterious forts
and other ancient buildings which are among
the little-known wonders of the Pacific.
Thousands of years back some now unknown
peopie, skilled in building fortifications, and
able to shift by sea huge masses of stone,
raised on those lovely atolls stone walls and
bastions of basalt 40 feet high. Though
overgrown with jungle, the ramparts to-
day could resist modern field-guns, and there
are one or two cellars that would be safe
even from howitzers firing h.e. shell. The
present natives of the islands know nothing
about the origin of the ruins and have no
traditions about them, so far as I was ever
able to discover; and this seems to indicate
that they were the work of other people,
who had to protect themselves against a
race as strong pernaps as themselves. It all
opens up intensely interesting speculations,
and is going to give a lot of work for the
historian some day.
“Dr. Nil” asks a few questions:—
Miss Kttie Rout, hon. sec. of the Maoriland Sol-
diers’ Medical Club at Hornchurch (Eng.), says (B.
7/2/’ 18) : “If it were possible for me to run a
properly-conducted licensed house for our troops I
would do it. It is not possible to do that at
present. In the nature of things we M.D. workers
can give only social values. The ‘other women’ give
more. I think it is my duty to see that this latter
‘giving’ is done under reasonably safe conditions.”
I would like to ask:—
(i.) Does Miss Rout really mean that she would
wish to run an aseptic “licensed house?”
(ii.) What women would she procure for this pur-
pose ?
(iii.) Does Miss Rout think it proper for women to
lend themselves to this purpose?
(iv.) Does she think it right to procure healthy
girls to minister to the sexual needs (?) of soldiers,
when “the nature of things” debars her from doing
it herself?
Also (v.) does she think that it is always the
women that infect the men, and not vice versa ?
And (vi.) why does she not take on a job that
she is willing and anxious that other healthy women
should do, and take the risk of disease herself ?
This does not exhaust the subject. For instance,
ask racing trainers whether they allow stallions to
“visit” mares during their racing careers.

“Bill Bowyang” (Palestine) : When any-
thing ails him otlr Gippo cobber who is
throwing down a railway in the direction of
Jerusalem is the most doleful sight in the
wide world. He turns up on sick-parade
with a face as long as the time between
pays—a face never rippled bv even the ghost
of a smile. His complexion is changed from
a good chocolate to a withered yellow. There
is a look in his eye like a lizard’s just after
it has lost its tail. The face is enshrouded
in a blanket wound closely round the melan-
choly head and allowed to hang down over
the shoulders. Arms
and legs are bare,
and he shivers with
cold even though the
day be a scorcher.
He does not speak,
even in Arabic. The
weight of his afflic-
tion, whatever it may
be, presses too closely
on him for mere
speech. He moans a
little, groans a little,
presses his hands to
his stomach and looks
unutterable anguish ;
then he sits down a
picture of helpless
hopelessness. If he
is really ill he gets
sent down the lines;
if it is only a trivial
ailment he is suffer-
ing from he gets
imshied. Always a
few dozen have to be
imshied after a
Taube has paid us a
visit. The Gippos do
not like Taubes, and
on the crash of a
bomb they remember
the manifold ills
that flesh is heir
to. “Taubitis,” says
Pills, and perhaps
gives the patient
some liquorice water;
but the real remedy
is the sight of one of our own machines
soaring heavenwards.
“Bill Bowyang” digs trenches in Canaan:
In the days when war was a game we played at
Liverpool camp, under the direction of men who
never smelt it, we first pegged out the line the
trench was to take. It was a nice line, with charm-
in;? curly traverses neatly pegged with slips of wood.
Then we cut the sods, like gardeners, and dug the
trench. It was all the same width, and if anyone by
ill luck knocked a corner off a traverse, he was
strafed. Finally, after making a dug-out for each
platoon commander, we put up wire. It was
pretty wire, and beautifully thick. All the poles
were upright, all the wires regular, and festooned
from pole to pole in graceful lines. We used to go
down every morning to dust the landscape and tidy
THE SOURCE OF DANGER.
“Yeth, bithnith keepth up, but it'll all go to Sol when I’m dead, and I’m vorried about
Sol. He can’t play cardth.”
“Well, that’s all right. Why worry?”
"Yeth, but he playth cardth”
the wire, until the Brigadier came to inspect them.
I grin as I look back on those trenches now after
months in Gallipoli, the deserts and Palestine. Over
here trenches begin anywhere, go through anything
and end nowhere. They ramble on, all sorts of
shapes, through wheat-fields and orange-groves, round
mosques, through villages, across waterless wadys,
anywhere and everywhere. There is nowhere a trench
may not go. They are all shapes and sizes, all
depths and widths; they are riveted with every
imaginable material, from plaited palm-branches and
sandbags to stone from neighboring ruins. They curl
serenely on through sand, buried pottery, shale and
soil, changing every few yards. And (God bless
the text-books !) the enemy can’t do any better. The
other night I followed my C.O. to Abdul’s aban-
doned trenches near Gaza. They looked like a
municipal tip overtaken by a landslide. All schools
of wiring were presented. On the left were the
remains of the German 1914 and 1915 styles. There
was a very fine example of the 1916 manner. Finally
there were some of the very latest “concertina” and
“gooseberry” fashions. A “gooseberry” is a round,
hollow ball of wire. A “concertina” is a coil which
pulls out all right behind the lines where it is
made, but on the flat ground in Palestine generally
refuses to pull out more than half-way. Then when
everyone is well tangled up in it, a Very light goes
up, and you drop fiat, and afterwards spend a pleas-
ant couple of hours getting yourself out.
Early-closing in Victoria is providing a
harvest for the wily Chow in Little Bourke-
street. By day Ah Fat sells the banana and
the ginger and by night the bottled booze.
Night after night the lanes in the Chow area
are filled with beer-fighters who squat in
doorways and on the exit steps of the
theatres and guzzle into the morning. On
the trail of the beer-eaters comes the lady
who has slipped, and when the sun has
pushed the gloom out of the lanes and by-
ways along comes the small boy with a sack
to reap the harvest of dead-marines.
“Trallwm”: Like so many others of its
ilk the Victorian Education Act knocks off
just before it reaches the end it’s out for.
Every week truancy officers have their batch
of parents up for neglecting to send children
to school—perhaps for the 20th time of
fining. In some cases the parents are no doubt
to blame, but in others the children are un-
controllable. These the Department has no
means of handling. Where the parents are
willing that the outlaw shall be put under
discipline, the Department must go cap-in-
hand to the Salvoes, or the Methodies, or
the Brothers of St. Joseph or St. Somebody
Else and ask that the child be taken into
their private fold—a fold for the most part
that has no machinery for controlling a
really wayward boy. In fact, they can’t
even hold one. There was a queer case in
Yarraville the other day—a boy sent to
Surry Hills in charge of about 17st. of
policeman was waiting on Yarraville plat-
form to meet the bobby on his return from
taking the kid to the home! The boy had
escaped at- once and sneaked a ride home on
the same train. Thus it is that often quite
innocent parents are landed with almost
weekly fines, while neither the State nor the
child gets any benefit from the operation of
the law ; The youngsters, for their part, keep
the Children’s Courts busy ; and once con-
victed of continued crime the State provides
for the junior criminal the juvenile peniten-
tiary. But to insist on a child becoming a
criminal before the Government will take
any strong measures to reform him savors
more of Dean Swift than modern thought.
“Ato” meets tragedy :
The theatres were out. The streets were alive
with streams of people rushing' for trains and trams.
Suddenly I bumped into a tall man standing stiff
as a post in the middle of the path.
Beg pardon,” he said, without turning his head.
He woie the badge of a returned warrior and stood
as upright as though on parade.
Anything wrong?” I asked, touching his arm.
tt ,B 8 a H right, mate,” he answered cheerfully.
Ive lost my mother in the crowd. Don’t you
worry. She’ll soon spot me if I stand still.”
He spoke without turning his head, and I knew
that behind the smoked glasses there was no sight.
“Don’t worry, mate. Mum will find me.”
And two minutes before I had helped three other
men to lift into an ambulance-waggon a poorly-
clad old woman that a mol r-car had mangled.
I glanced up at the serene, smiling, expectant face
of the big blind soldier.
“It’s all right. Mum will find me.”
Then, like the coward I am, I ran away.
THE PEACE OF GOTT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
EDMUND FISHER.
“Cod now sets before the Germans the great task
of bringing peace to the world. To this end the
violent powers of Heaven must assist us.”
— Kaiser’s latest speech.
Great Gott, be with thy chosen Hun
When driven like dumb cattle;
We know Thy will is truly done
When Max is urged to battle;
His captain’s curse is Heaven’s law,
Likewise the smack on Max’s jaw,
And (though it seemeth bestial)
The colonel’s spit in Max’s face
Is but a hint of heav’nly grace,
A sort of sign celestial.
The Kaiser and the Lord are one:
Jehovah is assisting
When Max is chained to a gun.
Or belted for resisting.
The sergeant biffs him into shape,
And curbs his impulse to escape
If he would fain surrender.
. On every holy Hun—eh, what?—
The gracious and all-seeing Gott
Rains down His blessings tender.
Great Gott, let not thy servant Max
Get Bolshevikian nations ;
Keep with him when his morale’s lax
And stifle his emotions.
If he be sadly sore or sick
Uplift him with a kindly kick
(Thou know’st how well he stands it) ;
Bathe every Max in seas of blood
And bury him in crimsoned mud —
The Peace of Gott demands it.

“Tas.”: An entry for the hard-luck stakes.
During the recent floods up our (Rockhamp-
ton) way, a man named Linden hoisted his
dog, Matilda and himself into" a tree at
Yaamba and was prisoned there for four
nights and three days before a rescue party
arrived. Linden and his belongings were cot
safely into the boat, but making down the
river the craft struck a log and overturned.
One of the men swam after the boat and
managed to bail it out with his hat. Then
he set out to rescue the other rescuer and
Linden; but though the former was found
safely perched in a tree, no trace at all
could be seen of the swaggie*

“Garnet” : No use telling our lot that there
isn’t anything in the superstition that it’s
unlucky for a third man to light a cig. at
a match that two cobbers have used already.
We were in the Palace Gardens (front line)
and there was an issue of fags—two packets
a man and a box of matches between two,
which meant that matches had to be made
to go as far as possible. Two boys had used a
match, and a third, who was hungry for a
smoke, was just poking his face out towards
the light, when his cobber playfully pushed
him away.; and at the very moment that he
was hitting the ground, a nosecap planted
itself where lie had been standing. If he had
been allowed to have his draw as third man
it would have been lights out for him.
(B.S. —I suppose I did it sometime. There’s
a leg of mine in England.)

“Dave B.”: Heard of the sixth-century
Mosaic our boys found in the Turkish trench
at Shelal, north-west of Beersheba? I was
at the digging-out. One Billjim dug away
the thin covering crust of earth with a
bayonet while his mate cleaned up the
Graeco-Syriac illustrations with a sandbag.
The Lion was reported all clear at 9 a.m
The Stork shone at 9.45. The day was a
corker, and the digger's interest was flag-
ging when the polisher, who specialised in
tombs and bones, sooled him on with the re-
mark : “Keep her jigging, Bill; we’ll get one
of Norman Lindsay’s bears directly !”
BUSINESS BRISK.
“ Er — aren't you the girl I kissed last night on the promenade ?”
“About what lime was it?”
Broken Hill (N.S.W.) is varying its usual
pastime of worrying the Law by giving the
doctors a turn. A mysterious disease —
marked “x” for identification —has the local
faculty thinking. Two deaths occurred in
the local hospital from the unclassified hor-
ror last week. One victim was a miner of
54, who had lived on the Hill since the ’SO’s.
He fell sick on Sunday, and was arrested on
a charge of insanity on Tuesday. Observa-
tion proved that he wasn’t mac! but deadly
ill. He died on Thursday. The doctors
have a dark suspicion that “x” isn’t the
newcomer he pretends to be —that, in fact,
he was responsible for many cases which
last year were ticketed septicemia or menin-
gitis. Meantime they are after him with a
leg-rope and branding-iron.
“1449”: As Billjim was leaving the mess-
table the officer of the day, accompanied by
the sergeant, was going the rounds. Jim-
bill, with a plate in one hand and a mug in
the other, was passing without the usual
salute, and was halted. “Don’t you know
when to salute?” said Saltpetre the Great.
Billjim uidn’t bat an eye. Solemnly he
crowded his utensils upon the sergeant,
wagged his wing smartly, hooked his grub
again and went his gait. Such things hap-
pen even here in Bagdad.
“T.F.M.”: As to the date of the first
settlement at Mallacoota Inlet (Vic.),
“D.F.” (B. 24/l/’IS) says it is almost cer-
tainly older than the Henty establishment
at Portland Bay. It isn’t. When the
Hentys went to Portland, in November,
1834, there was no other white settlement
on the coast between Westralia and Twofold
Bay. The Twofold Bay settlement is senior
to Portland by only three' months. I am
aware that a tradition exists that Captain
Stevenson, the pioneer of Mallacoota, went
there in 1828; but, like a good many others,
this yarn has no substantial foundation. The
present-day decendants of Stevenson, who
still reside in the locality, aver that their
ancestor quantified at Mallacoota, on what
is still known as Captain’s Point, in 1842.
He had previously been a whaling skipper
in the employ of Ben Boyd at Twofold Bay,
and Ben didn’t reach Australia till 1840.
“Kari”: I know Darwin and its China-
town, and I am wondering what is the use
of an Administrator who permits a white Aus-
tralian baby-boy to be transferred right
under his eyes to a Chinaman to be brought
up in China-town. Has Gilruth amongst
the multiplicity of ordinances no machinery
whereby a destitute white child can be
cared for? Surely a little of
the money annually slung into
the Territory could not be put
to a better use than to give a
little Australian a chance!
This is not the first case of
the kind. I recollect that a
few years ago a woman in the
N.T. was deserted by a worth-
less husband. She had a large
young family, a couple of whom
were able to scratch along for
themselves and did it very well.
Amongst them was a harem-
scarem youngster who was en-
ticed into China-town and har-
bored there by the Pongs, who
gave him ginger and cigarettes
and sam-shu and opium and
other nice things. As the ad-
ministration did nothing to put
that kid straight, the burly
R.C. padre, a Frenchman, who
claimed the family in his flock,
walked into China-town one
morning with a stick in his
hand and beat the wild colo-
nial boy out of an evil-smelling
joint. Then taking the kid by
the ear he walked him down
to the old jetty, and, putting him on
board a boat, shanghaied him over to the
mission station at Bathurst Island, whe.re
the youngster’s brother was. Later on the
good padre sent the two boys to a school in
Sydney, where they are doing well.

From a wireless Billjim in Bagdad:—
We had a rather interesting trip down to the
ruins of Babylon the other day. There’s nothing
much to see on the way—nothing to break thi,
monotony of bare desert except an occasional sand-
hill or two, each supposed to be the remains of some
palace, village or city of a date before the Flood.
The Germans have done a lot of excavating at Baby-
lon and got away with valuable relics. Nebuchad-
nezzar’s place is the biggest ruin brought to light
so far, and even what’s left gives some idea of the
one-time splendor. The bricks are wonderfully pre-
served and the old picture-writing is still very clear.
There are also some rather well-preserved animals
wrought in the brick walls, and one very good lion
carved in solid Milestone —the first rock of any kind
I have seen in this country. The lion has lost his
head. The story goes that some years ago a
Frenchman came over to do an excavating job, and
to startle the Arabs proceeded to draw forth money
from the lion’s mouth. This set the Arabs thinking,
so back they came in the night and knocked old
Leo’s head off to get the balance of the cash.
’The more one sees of this country the more one
wonders why there isn’t a tree or two. There are,
of course, millions of palms, and along the river
and irrigation canals the Arabs grow marvellous
crops of grain. I'm beginning to read a new meaning
into that verse of Omar’s. The book of verse is easy ;
there’s plenty of sour wine; a congenial “thou”
mightn’t be hard to find; and wilderness —there’s
miles of it! But where old Omar got his “bough”
from beats me. Abdul must have wondered too, so
lie’s planted a grove of Australian gums right here in
Bagdad.
G. 11. Knibbs, Stipendiary Investigator of
Idols, now breaks the mossy superstition
that Nature tries to even-up battlefield wast-
age by producing an increased proportion of
male infants during war-time. lie finds that
the present birth ratio is about 106 males to
100 females. Taking France as a criterion,
no increase of male births was observable
from 1865 to 1876. During the first four
years of that worrisome period the excess of
male over female births averaged only 24 in
every 10,000. It dropped to 23-} during ’7O
and ’71 —the actual war era—and again
averaged 2-i from ’72 to ’76. As regards the
Commonwealth young Billjim hasn’t arrived
more frequently than usual during the past
four years.

“Glen ll.’’: In England a precedent was
sot when a wealthy soldier who shot a rotter
with whom his wife had become infatuated
was acquitted to an accompaniment of
cheers. Now we learn by cable of a
soldier who had returned home and
shot his giddy wife being bound over in the
sum of £5 to come up for judgment when
called upon, a fatuous judge observing that
the sentence was “consonant with reasoned
public opinion.” That “reasoned public
opinion” does not view these matters as
lightly as do some judges was shown by the
disapproval expressed by a large section of
the more responsible press at the acquittal of
the accused soldier in the first of the two
cases mentioned. Owing to the present
popularity of gentlemen in khaki, right-
thinking journals, which ordinarily are great
barrackers for the lash and the gallows, will
not raise a squeak of protest when a warrior
guilty of a murder, for which some senti-
mental motive can be discovered, is dis-
charged. This sort of thing won’t do T.
Atkins any good. The last thing the civilian
soldier can afford to see weakened is the

CIGARETTES
By the irony of fate old Abdul the
Damned acquired inflammation of the lungs
and had a nice, dozy, don’t-care end. For
more than 40 years the absurd, bloodthirsty
creature lived in nervous dread of death by
assassination, and then, this month, he pegged
out in ji state of not-wanting-to-be worried.
Death is an eccentric potentate, with no
sense of poetic justice.
ALL BETS OFF.
JOHNNY: "The dad’s awfully keen on me en-
listing. He wants to bet me that if I
do the war will be over before I get
there.”
JENNIE: “Why don’t you be a sport and
take him up?”
JOHNNY : “I would, but the poor old chap’s
so awfully unlucky, y’know. He’s never
won a bet in his life.”
The appointment of Attorney-General
Herdman to the Supreme Court of Maoriland
in succession to John Denniston was fixed
up the other day, and Herdman now wears
the wig. There is at least one point of
identity between the records of these two men.
dissimilar in every other respect. Before
taking to law both were bank clerks, Dennis-
ton in the Union and lierdman in the
National. Denniston was 44 when he went
to the bench. He stayed there 29 years, and
though during that time he was often criti-
cised for leniency towards criminal offenders,
lie was never once charged with severity.
lierdman is 49. Most of the other Maori-
land judges are old enough to look on him as
kid. Stout the Chief is 74. Chapman (19,
Edwards 68, Hosking (>6, Stringer 62, Cooper
68, Sim 67. In another year the law will
remind Stout that it is time t--> take a
pension.
Transit’: Bigjohn of Bunbury was one
of the most reliable of the old-time surveyors
of the Groner State. The field-notes of his
surveys, effected with primitive instruments
and heavy link chains, are remarkably clear
and accurate. Of late years when the boom
in land settlement led to the employment of
hundreds of surveyors equipped with modern
instruments and light steel tapes, the result
of the linking up of the old surveys proved
disastrous to the professional reputations of
some of John
Forrest’s con-
temporaries. His
own stood the
test. As one
who was on the
job with the best
of instruments I
found that sur-
veyor Forrest’s
traverses of
tracks an d
rivers were
thoroughly re-
liable and could
be fitted in with
the most recent
work.

Billjims Pitts and Choate lately escaped to
London from a German prison camp. As a
preliminary, Choate, who had been sentenced
to two years for nothing or other, learned to
speak Hunnish. Then he raised some cash
by raffling an old fiddle. For disguise they
faked something that passed muster as
ordinary workmen’s duds, and they grew
moustaches as fierce as the Kaiser’s.
Finally, they commandeered some tucker,
dodged the sentries and left. For the
behoof of the natives Choate talked col-
loquial German to his mate, who, being a
surly fellow, replied only by nods. Their
biggest bluff happened at the Dutch frontier,
where they brazenly asked a German sentry
the way to the railway station. Said Fritz :
*^ r ho are you?” “Belgian workmen,” lied
Choate. “Where are you going?” “Home!”
quoth truthful James. And they went.
Died in Melbourne last week good old
Nicholas Caire, an enthusiastic landscape
photographer whose work stared Yic. Rail-
way travellers in the face for many years.
Caire, an octogenarian when he died, used to
take views of beauty spots for the Railway
Dept. He knew all the giant trees in Gipps-
land, and could talk interestingly of their
age, height, dimensions and family history.
In his photographic way he was an explorer,
and during his sober and discreet life trav-
elled thousands of miles of rough country
with his trusty camera.
One of the Sydney’s returned boys was
asked liow he felt when his vessel went into
action against the Emden. “As if I had
swallowed an alarm-clock and it was iust
ready to go off,” he said.

McNamara, father of Flight-Commander F.
11. McNamara, Y.C. ; wheat inspector, autho-
rity on Australian flora, and former Presi-
dent of the Australian Natives’ Assoc. He
passed out at Castlemaine (Vic.) a month
or two after his son came home invalided,
with a record of having flown down under

A useful citizen was G. R. Marks, just
dead in Adelaide at close on 80. lie was
with Doultons in London, and came to a
similar concern at Brunswick (Vic.), but
shortly afterwards started the Ballarat pot-
tery on his own account; then, nearly 40
years ago, he moved on to Adelaide just in
time for the start of deep drainage, and
(successfully backed his opinion—much
doubted at the time—that the very best pipes
could be made from the local day. His pot-
tery at suburban Brompton was a model.
It wasn’t long after Billjim’s arrival on
the \\ esteru Front that it struck Sergeant
Raiinall Carlisle, formerly manager of an
Australian concert co.. that all fight and no
play was no good to Bill, and in quick time
lie had the Anzac Concert Party giving two
shows nightly behind the lines-—the first at
5.d0 and the second at 7.15. Sometimes a
“special” was put on at 9.30 for the men
just out. of the trenches. One six-weeks’
takings—officers, one franc; others, 4 franc
totalled £9OO, cash going to purchase hot
coffee, bovril, etc., for the chaps in the firing-
line. The original cast has, of course, been
changed from time to time, sometimes for
unavoidable reasons, as for instance when
Anderson (pianist) and Brittlebank were
blown to bits in their dugout. Frank Dono-
va.n (Allan and Co., Melbourne) succeeded
Anderson at the piano. At latest accounts
the Anzac Coves”— which is the party’s
other name—was touring Britain in the
same old cause.
The Wares, of Adelaide, having been 50
years in occupation of the Exchange Hotel,
have just had a beano. The Exchange was
one ot the first buildings erected in the citv,
and was given its present name by George
Coppin when he had it about 1850. The
ate C. J. Ware went in in 1868, his widow
later carried on for about 26 years, and for
the last 20 the place has been owned by the
sons—A.W.,. George and “Boxer.” A.W.,
who was Mayor of Adelaide during King
George s visit, has been living in Queensland
for some years, but is now back in S.A.
again. There was a big gathering to cele-
brate the jubilee in the old place, which still
keeps the low roofing, narrow oassages and
sudden steps of an old-fashioned English inn,
and holds a collection of pictures of Coppin
and other celebrities of his day.
Of the Patersons, of Haberfield (Sydney),
nine brothers are fighting. There is a tenth,
who wanted to go, too, but couldn’t get past
the doctor.
The death of legislator Tare Parata gave
occasion for obsequies in Maoriland which
were the unqualified success the jolly brown
man himself would have desired. They re-
called the tangi of another noted Maori, when
Tare held high place among the living. On
the day of the funeral the undertaker died
suddenly. “You wouldn’t have believed wlmt
a shock it was to us,” said Tare afterwards:
! 1<: threw quite a gloom over the proceed-
ings.”

George Lewis, for many years’ sec. of the
N. -S. Wales Mill Employees’ Union, who
recently left the Trades Hall to run a mill
on his own account, has now taken on the
job of sec. to the Flour Mill Owners’ Defence
Assoc. He is not the first brainy union
official to change over; for company he has
Harry Mitchell, once of the Federated
Engine Drivers, but now Industrial Officer
with the Broken Hill Prop.; and assistant-
sec. Morgan, of the Southern Colliery Pro-
prietors, who used to be prominent in the
South Coast lodges some years ago.

“The Snake”: When Kerensky whirled
into the limelight with his “blood and iron”
and “merciless punishment” speeches, I sus-
pected that he was qualifying for a season
with Bilanderson. Later he was photo-
graphed with his staff—upstage Kerensky,
wearing an English officer’s uniform, his
hand in his tunic and shoulders drooped a la
the Little Corporal. Now comes this picture:
In conversation, Kerensky laid stress on his physi-
cal weakness. He postured. At Moscow he in-
sisted on having an armchair different from the chairs
of his colleagues, and kept his military and naval
secretaries, two young and good-looking officers,
standing motionless and erect behind his chair. He
had a craze for phrases such as “I am your supreme
leader.”
In Petrograd he got the advt. he craved for
by his whim of sleeping in the Imperial
beds. So the laurel-bays prepared for him
turn to dockleaves. I am convinced that he
is really a Welshman.
Ex-M.H.R. John K. McDougall, the
Labor poet of the Wannon, has written an
Elegy to Peace (Labor Calk, 7/2/T8). He
found his divinity in Summer’s chosen
ways, with purple heath around her and
scented flow’rs ablaze,” somewhere in a West-
ern valley where “the Wannon sings to her.”
It seems that, “beside a sleeping river at
dusk her footsteps roam,” while she dozes
in the daytime among the bulloak and mallee.
It is a placid region, down Maroona way,
where McDougall grows his wheat and drives
amiably around in his farm-buggy. Yet it
used to be very much like that in parts of
Belgium, and writing non-combatant poetry
won’t really do anything to assure Peace an
abiding tenure in her present lodgings. The
mistake these peace poets make is that they
always compose their verses in their own lan-
guage for home circulation instead of in the
other fellows’ language for consumption next
door.

Probably ■ out of every war that ever was
fought somebody made money; but usually
he was either a big thief who sold army sup-
plies or a little thief who robbed the sick
and the wounded and the dead, just as they
are being disgracefully robbed to-day. The
world has, however, developed credit amaz-
ingly ; and in this war the nations are taking
full advantage of that development. That
has made all the difference in the world to
the people : it lias turned war-time actually
into a boom-time : it has put silk stockings
on thousands of legs that never wore them
before, and will probably never wear them
again; it has littered womankind with gaudy
jewellery; and it has more than doubled the
profits of this highly respectable old eo. In
consequence of the development of credit
before-mentioned and the boom in wages,
aggravated by submarines, prices of most
commodities have been far higher than
usual; which, of course, has meant more
insurance premium. But not only have the
values been higher, but in marine business
especially (and this co. does a big marine
business) the rates have almost taken the
breath away. Of course there have been
losses; but if the underwriters discovered
that they were not making enough to cover
the losses, they are so well organised that
the rates would be raised within 24 hours.
Add also the heavy premiums got on the old
hulks that have been hauled out of the
world’s Rotten Rows and sent to sea. And
add to all this the very important considera-
tion that trade has been so extraordinarily
good that the moral hazard has been practi-
cally negligible-—in plain English, it hasn’t
paid anybody to either burn his premises and
stock or send his ship to sea to sink —and
this co.’s remarkable figures for the past
year are understandable.
This next table shows even more strik-
ingly how the figures have bounded ahead :

Included with the exes, for the past year is
£40,000 appropriated for taxation; and that
is apparently in addition to the taxes actu-
ally paid during the year (£37,9351. In 1913
it cost £178,613 in commissions, salaries and
other exes, (apart from rates, taxes and fire-
brigade contriuutions, etc.) to get £702,968
of business. Last year it cost only £205,789
to get £1,147,704 —£27,176 more in exes, to
get £444,736 extra premiums. That’s where
the profit has come from, because the loss
rate has kept at just about the average.
But it has all got to be paid for. The
world doesn’t get rich by printing lOIJ's and
going on a spree with the proceeds. Things
like that only make it a very sad world
when the lOU’s have to be cashed. In this
case, unfortunately, we are cashing one
another’s lOU’s. That is to say, we shall
ourselves have to do the paying. And not
only will this co. have to cash up more or
less, but the paying-un will then stir our old
friend Moral Hazard, and ships and other
property which have lately seemed unsink-
able and incombustible will gradually become
quite different. Thus two things have to be
noted: (1) The very big increase of business
during the uast year or two may be disre-
garded ; (2) the very handsome accumula-
tiou of profit consists of the proceeds of
IOl”s which have to be paid presently. The
whole question is. Who will do the paying?
It is no use saying that the New Zealand
Ins. Co. will have to pay its share, and that
its net revenue may be no better with the
extra money than it was before without it.
We don’t know yet who will pay. It is
pretty certain that nine patriots out of 10
won’t pay a penny that they can dodge.

feature of the table is the fine increase in

practically everything that has come in dur-

gone into British and Maoriland War Loan.
Interest earnings were only about £5OO short
of the dividend ; in other words, all but about
£5OO of the fine underwriting profit has been
banked for the further protection of policy-

This is another co. that will probably be
affected presently by the new Gas Bill. So 1
far as it has gone it looks as though 8 p.c.
will be adopted as the standard rate, and
any co. which is not making the standard
rate is entitled to go to a Judge and have the
selling price of gas put up if it can show
that costs have increased in the last
five years. Premier Holman told the
Assembly that Ministers had been ad-
vised that 8 p.c. was a fair rate.
Who advised them? Will whoever advised
the Government answer this question: “If
this Bill is passed and provision is made for
increasing the price of gas so that the Aus-
tralian Gas Light Co. can pay 8 p.c., and if
there is a new issue of shares which will get
the benefit of that provision, at what price
do you think they will stand in the market?”
An 8 n.c. dividend, guaranteed to this extent,
that the whole of any increases in wages and
the cost of materials may be passed on to the
consumer, will, this paper is satisfied, mean
a 20 to 25 p.c. premium. The gas cos. have
rather severely tried the patience of the
community, and in asking now for this 8 p.c.
standard—it is presumably their request —
they are once more seeking from the public
more than a fair thing. Palmer’s “Stock
Exchange Guide’' for December showed that
at that date Australian Gas Light Co.’s
shares were selling at a price which gave a
return of 51 p.c. The investor thought that
was a fair thing, not 8 p.c.
This Newcastle Co.'s present capital is
£28(5,789, of which, as the table shows, no
less than £105,925 was transferred from re-
serves when the Gas Bill was passed. In
that most ridiculous measure—of which the
just-returned Carmichael, by the way, was
in charge—it was actually proposed that a
co. might pay 10 p.c.! Heaven only knows
how they expected it to be done by a concern
which had just watered its capital to equal
the total value of the undertaking: but that
was the proposal. And practically it is the
same Legislature which is dealing with the
matter now. The fact is, it is, in the lump,
so utterly unversed in the financial questions
involved that any interested person, if he is
smart enough, can pull its leg indefinitely.
It may be that there ai'e men in the Parlia-
ment who are there for the purpose.
This co. is paying, though (judged by the
accounts! it is not earning, 7 p.c. on the old
capital and £4 18s. p.c. on- the new. This is
not a fair thing cither; and the co. is entitled
to relief. A co. ought not to be compelled by
law to sell its goods at a price which pro-
duces less than 5 p.c. on any share capital;
and its case for relief is all the more
unanswerable because the law itself has in-
creased the cost of manufacture since the
selling price was fixed. The only tilings that
really admit of argument are (1) the stan-
dard rate of dividend and (2) the proportion
of extra costs that a co. should be entitled
to pass on. The Bill proposes to let the co.
pass on the lot. It shouldn't. If it is
allowed to do that the cos. may regard every
demand for an increased price as a matter of
no consequence to them ; and that shouldn’t
be. It should pay every co. to make in
connection with every transaction the best
bargain it can possibly drive.

There s a little difficulty about some of those
figures. Something over three years ago the
co. issued a first lot of debentures and got
permission to send out a second lot a year or
so back, the two issues making £1,000,000;
and these have to be redeemed out of a so-
called sinking fund. In the foregoing table
the amount used for this purpose is not
deducted from the profits; nor, of course, is
account taken of the redeemed debentures
put back into Profit and Loss. In other
words, the transactions in connection with
the repayment of this debenture debt are
ignored. The profits are also shown without
deducting the arbitrary amounts written off
against depreciation.
The co. was hit by strikes during the half-
year not only at the mine, but at both the
smelting works. General-manager Delprat
puts it with delightful directness:—
Iron and steel works.—Productive work was again
interrupted tics half-year through a general strike,
which lasted about three months. This time our
men had no grievances, hut ceased work in sympathy
with men employed elsewhere who considered they
had.
Broken Hill.—Work was interrupted for about
eight weeks owing to a strike brought about by the
A.M.A. in sympathy with the men on strike in
Sydney, and a considerable number of men left the
district, leaving us shorthanded when operations were
resumed.
However, the war came to the rescue; and,
though only 55,316 tons ore were mined at
Broken Hill, the co. found such a fine market
for its products and especially for the output
of the steel works at Newcastle, that, barring
the previous half, you have to go back more
than 10 years to find anything so good.
But even under normal conditions the mine
is only small potatoes nowadays compared
with the steel business. Of a total expendi-
ture last half-year of £779,454 on working
account £542,609 is charged to “Iron and
steel,” and there are probably o'ther items to
go into the heap—a portion, no doubt, of the
£46.187 paid for fuel and fluxes, timber and
water, and of the £50,100 spent on freight,
insurance and charges on products. And the
co. is only beginning its career as an iron-
master. Of £436,948 spent on construction
and charged to capital account last year no
less than £430,744 was spent on the new in-
dustry, the total outlay on which has now
exceeded £2,566,000, exclusive of the expendi-
ture on the Iron Knob tramway (S.A.),
over which the iron-ore supplies are drawn.
You never know your luck. The Broken Hill
Prop, mine had paid just over £10,000,000 in
dividends, and probably one way and another
five times that much in wages—most of it in
Australia, too, because the co. did all its
smelting here right from the start, and had
a refinery up soon after the furnaces were
blown in. The approaching exhaustion of a
mine like that didn’t seem anything to smile
aoout; yet, with a man of imagination and a
brilliant organiser like G. D. Delprat at the
head of things and a solid Board and a co.
with vast resources behind him, it was one
of the best things that have happened to Aus-
tralia. A gloomier man might have groaned
about labor troubles and stood back. Del-
prat said, in effect, as he has said all along:
“Well, it’s my business to beat labor
troubles”; and when he can’t do it by smil-
ing he does it some other way. it was
characteristic of his methods that at Broken
Hill,, when, he wanted some music for his
tennis parties (and perhaps when he didn’t),
the miners’ hand had a job. “Look here,” he
said long asm, “there’s hardly a day of my
life that I don’t want to sell somebody some-
thing for more than he will give me, or buy
something from somebody at a price
that he won’t sell it for; and when I
want to buy labor I’ll expect to now, and
then find the man wants more. There s no
reason to call him bad names for that, is
there?” Which explains the almost-cheerful
remark quoted above, a ter reporting that tin;
steel works were shut down for “about three
months”: —
This time our men had no grievances, but ceased
work in sympathy with men employed elsewhere
who considered they had.
Already there are at Newcastle a blast fur-
nace, for the production of pig-iron, and a
second is under construction; seven open-
hearth furnaces, which had last half an out-
put of 52,878 tons steel; a blooming mill,
which rolled 57,065 tons of ingots, with new
equipment for producing shipbuilding plates ;
a rail mill, which rolled 23,308 tons rails
and 11,076 tons structural steel, the rails up
to 1001 b. per yarn and the structural steel up
to 1201 b. per yard ; an 18in. merchant mill
for light rails, etc., and 12in. and Bin. mills,
to prouuce rods down to three-eighths of an
inch —with, of course, steel and iron foundries
and a coke works of 78 ovens. Now. to pro-
vide fuel for the new blast furnace and other
additions, 54 more ovens are to be put in,
which should make the lot equal to about
300,000 tons of coke a year; and the co.
won’t be living up to its reputation if before
long it isn’t producing something more there
besides coke, tar and sulphate of ammonia.
The only other plant under construction at
present is one in which Cocky will be inter-
ested—a continuous rod mill from which
fencing and other wires may be drawn.
So far as the old silver-lead-zinc mine goes
tlie co. could probably work it out, if it
wanted to, in four or five years at most.
Exploration during the last few years has
given negative results, and the co, is already
poking about for ore : it lately broke into the
old 190 b fire area. In addition to the mine
it has large dumps of zincy tailings. These
are retreated in the lead mill and after being
deleaded are sent on to the flotation plant.
The latter handled last half 92,907 tons tail-
ings and produced 21,960 tons zinc concen-
trates, a proportion of which were no doubt
sent on to the Associated Smelters’ spelter
plant under the bright arrangement which
the co. made when it sold the plant—that
the small zinc section should give priority to
this co.’s concentrates while the war lasted.
And besides the mine and the dumps and the
iron and steel works and almost inexhaustible
’supplies at Iron Knob, the co. has £821.514
of more or less liquid not reckon-
ing the outstanding debentures (£901,700) as
a liability. And, almost as valuable as any
other asset, a magnificent organisation and
the faith to go ahead without first asking
anybody whether he will strike or not. There
are now 1,181,006 shares.

Profits last half-year were nearly £BO,OOO
less than in the previous six months; but
owing to the strike, which lasted 11 weeks,
only 69,758 tons ore were raised as against
121,433 tons. The profit per ton of ore was
thus really a bit better —£2 Bs. Sd. as against
£2 Is. This improvement is traceable to
everything except lower costs. Values were
slightly higher in all metals; and better
markets, especially for silver, have also been
a factor. Metal recoveries were slightly
higher, too. The South has, of course, its
own lead mill for the treatment of crudes.
This recovered as a smelting concentrate
73.7 p.c. of the lead and 52.2 p.c. of the
silver. There remained in this concentrate
8.4 p.c. of the zinc. But it was a very good
smelting product, for all that, assaying only
7.1 p.c. of zinc to 67.5 p.c. lead and 22.70 z.
silver.
This lead mill makes four products:—
(1) The lead concentrate just described (16.3 p.c.
of the total tonnage) ;
(2) Zinc tailings (57.4 p.c. of the tonnage), and
containing 13.1 p.c. of the lead, 27.6 p.c. of the
silver and 72.1 p.c. of the zinc;
(3) Quartz tailings (9.9 p.c. of the tonnage),
with only 0.9 p.c. of the lead, 1.8 p.c. of the silver
and 4.4 p.c. of the zinc;
(4) Slimes (16.4 p.c. of the tonnage), containing
12.3 p.c. of the lead, 18.4 p.c. of the silver and
15.1 p.c. of the zinc.
The quartz tailings are not retreated ; they
are sent below for filling. The zinc tailings
are delivered as made to the De Bavay Co.,
which treats them on a profit-sharing basis.

there is still a very poor market for zinc
concentrates, there is an enormous quantity
of this product in Australia unsold ; and the
South Co. may still get a considerable sum
on account of tailings not yet finally settled
for What the sum will be will depend upon
