The two most admirable features in the
recent metallurgical practice of the B. 11.
South are (1) the small quantity of metal
left in the nuartz tailings; (2) the excellent
recoveries by the selective flotation slime
plant. This plant, which was developed just
before the war started, consists of two sec-
tions—the lead and the zinc. The entire
slime product of the half-year was ; fed to the
lead section, which extracted no less than
51.7 p.c. of the lead and 84.8 p.c. of the
silver, and left in only 8.9 p.c of the zinc.
The zinc in this concentrate is only 7.7 p.c.,
so that it is almost as clean a product as the
concentrate from the crudes (7.1 p.c. zinc).
This deleading process leaves in the residue
91.1 p.c. of the zinc. “Experimental work on the
full-size plant in the zinc section has demon-
strated,” manager Wainwright says, “that
an excellent grade of zinc concentrates can
be obtained at reasonable cost with good
metal recoveries. Owing, however, to the
very limited market for slime-zinc concen-
trates at present, it has been decided to util-
ise this portion of the plant for treating the
old slime dump and obtaining a lead concen-
trate which can be readily disposed of.” Of
these old slimes there are 458,848 tons. It
is impossible to tell from the accounts what
profit this new slime process has added. The
two lots of lead concentrate compare as
follows
Lead, Silver, Zinc,
p.c. oz. p.c.
From crudes . . 67.5 22.7 7.1
From slimes .. 62.1 45.6 7.7
It looks as though the slime concentrate
should be worth more than the crude concen-
trate. Now, the profit all round, after de-
ducting £25,373 received for interest, and an
estimated £25,000 for zinc tailings, was
£9 6s. per ton of concentrate; and it takes
seven tons of slime to make one of slime-
lead concentrate.

14s. 6d. in dividends per ton of ore mined;
and the ore in reserve is estimated at
3,500,000 tons. If this ore in the mine pays
the same dividend per ton as the mine has
averaged during the seven years, sharehold-
ers will get £2,537,500, equal to something
over £l2 10s. per share; but at the old aver-
age rate of extraction the last dividend of
this series won’t be paid for 11 years.

to get with the German cut out. German
control of the metal business was a upas
tree; but Billy Hughes’s friends in the Aus-
tralian metal ring were very well content to
lie under the tree and pluck dividends from
it.

Melbourne-owned Elsmore (N.S.W.) lost
£293 last half, No. 1 plant having to be re-
moved and No. 2 getting into unpayable
ground. No. 1 is now in pound ground, and
there is years’ work ahead; but values have
not been proved. No. 2 plant now faces a
bit of shallow ground worth 1-ilb. With any-
thing like present prices the co. ought soon
to be kicking out divs. again.
Cope’s Creek Central, Tinglia (N.S.W.),
which, after being almost three years off the
dividend list, handed out Is. to shareholders
a couple of weeks ago, has unexpectedly
come to light with a further Is. div. Nos.
2 and 3 plants are in good ground on Skele-
ton Creek, and with concentrates realising
from £lBO to £l9O a ton the co. is having a
fine run. There are £30,000 shares paid to
6s. (id. * #
Manager Giblin puts the cost of installing
new pumps at Edna Central at £7500, which
is to be raised by calling up 2s. 6d. on the
60,000 shares. A month later and then the
“little river” flowing above the dyke from
the Central to the Edna May may come
under control. Deeps so far is immune,
but Stuckey is too wise to poke out behind
the dyke with either bore or drive. Con-
solidated now bewails water and heavy
ground. With the big stopes opened the
market has been expecting trouble of the
kind, just as every dealer feels it a sacred
duty to go home with a small bear in Deeps
on the off-chance that the long-talked-of burst
may happen along like a thief in the night.
The latest yarn is that the pitch of the
Central new reef is carrying the formation
so fast into the Deeps that Stuckey may
have a second string to his fiddle, so bear-
venturing may not be such a soft snap, after
all.

Commodore, Meekatharra (W.A.), recon-
structs on a 3d. call basis. Where Kearns,
the co.’s old co-partner in the Macquarne,
will come in isn’t obvious. As the cash pay-
ment of £lOOO for his half-share is the only
div. disbursed to anyone out of the old co., he
may not fuss over the promised paid-up
interest. The old Board goes into harness
in the new Commodore, though Brothel
Trade seems to have left. What gives Com-
modore scrip a fillip is the following-up next
door of the Ingliston reef to within a tew
feet of the Commodore boundary. A hun-
dred or two spent in crosscutting will show
if this little stranger meanders across the
line. If so the old claim has a kick left.
* * * *
Ida H. (W.A.) has been raised from the
dead. For many years this show paid regu-
lar dividends, and for its size was reckoned
one of the best English-owned mines in the
State. Then war-time costs rendered profit-
able mining impossible, and word came from
London to close down. Latterly tributors
have been taking a hand, and sufficient ore
is now in sight to enable regular gold pro-
duction to be continued for some time to
come.
* * * *
Great Fingall (W.A.) is to be let on
tribute. Work in the lower levels has ceased
and tributors will endeavor to keep the mine
going by taking out the best ore left in the
top levels. Some 70 men will be employed
instead of about 200. As Day Dawn township
has been living on the one big mine, there
will soon be another almost-deserted village.
Mount Cutlibert’s latest is to get Turner
and Brownhill to underwrite some 34,000
shares at 13s. Shareholders can get a cut
in at the rate of 14 for every 100 held.
Markejt prices stick at about 125., so it looks
as if there isn’t much in the concession for
shareholders. At 13s. the co. gets in about
£22,000. If alO p.c. com. is allowed, the co.
would have £20.000 wherewith to reduce
debts. This would go to deferred creditors,
which in August stood at £59,000. Profits
since then have eased that amount to
£44,000. With this £20,000 off and a decent
two or three months’ run, Cutlibert will get
its balance-sheet into better trim than ever
before. What narks is the constant bicker-
ing with labor. This is put down to the
I.W.W. Is there no other cause?
Mungana, near Chillagoe (Q.), is to be
resurrected once more, the Lady Jane and
Girofla being reopened with the assistance
of a Government grant. Whether the Gov-
ernment intends to have first call on the
ore mined for its State smelters enterprise
is not known. Old hands swear by the quan-
tity of ore left in these mines. The Griffiths
mine is being worked by Cleeland and party.
They have unearthed some fine slugs of
native copper, one of which turned the
scale at 1 ton ,scwt. They are raising high-
grade ore now.
The Flying Pig, Thornborough (Q.). is
to be given a trial by a local syndicate. This
once-famous Hodgkinson mine has large
dumps of ore saiu to be worth loz., besides
huge quantities of stone in the hill worth
down to half an ounce. Having no develop-
ment expenses and with a mine ready to
break stone from the syndicate reckons it
can make a good thing of the show. It is
putting in its own mill and cyanide plant.
Out of £3244 worth of tin Lode Hill (Q.)
snicked in £1350 of profits in the half-year to
December last. No. 1 face is worked out, and
now the nozzle is laid on to Knowlton’s lease,
but there isn’t enough labor to work more
than one shift.
* * * *
r. J. Iloldenson, boss of the Victoria
liyoro Electric Co., holding rights on the
Iviewa and other Victorian rivers, smites
the late Peacock Government hard. He says
he urged that crowd to view the generation
of power from the rivers as a national mat-
ter. Failing that he demanded that his co.
should be let loose on the streams. This is
all right; but what folly to form cos. and
spend cash if a title is not assured ! That
seems to be the weak spot. Still, that
doesn’t dispose of the wickedness of locking
up. potential wealth such as is stored in
rivers and brown coal while problems like
those of repatriation have to be faced.

E. C. Dyason is as voluminous in his
monthly Bendigo Amalgamated report as
Billy Hughes is on the metal question. But
if Dyason must spread himself, why not
adopt the Yankee newspaper’s story-telling
dodge of introducing the yarn with a short
summary par. ? Here is a lead
Constellation shaft, Pyke reef: results rotten.
Tributors given a go. Londonderry shaft, Pandora
stone no good; stopped; now dodging after the
Garden Gully reef extension. Garden Gully main
shaft, better prospects ; Carlisle shaft, breaking 40ft.
of quid stone —
and end with the terse little sentence:
“Month’s profits, .” Then he can let
himself go as he does over the new reef
gotten at Extended Hustler’s at 840 ft., or
toot out the wonders of the shoot drill and
do a. crosshead splash over any special patch
of good fortune. Dyason’s earth-hunger
hasn’t been satisfied yet, as he is grabbing-
up ground on such lines as the Napoleon,
Nell Gwynne, Carshalton, Thistle and a
dozen others. J. Bromley was the pegger and
Dyason is to be the financier. Is another
emissary to go to the IJ.S.A. '!

Tongkah Compound, Siam, ended the half-
year to September 30 last with a net profit of
£15,622, and shareholders received three six-
pennies, totalling £11,250, on their fully-paid
6s. Bd. shares. At the balance the co. had
cash totalling £13,685 and concentrates on
hand worth £1829. A further dividend and
bonus amounting to Is. was paid in Decem-
ber. During the six months 337,904 cub.
yards were treated, so net profits worked out
at just under Is. per yard. The latest re-
turn for a run of 606 hours was 27 tons of
oxide from 60,612 ■ cub. yards. Another 61
acres of ground were purchased last year:
but further acquisitions will be necessary to
prolong the co.’s life beyond three years.
There are worse mining specs, than that
being launched as the Wallace-Chiltern Val-
ley (Vic.) by A. G. D. Bernaechi. His
game is to open up the Chiltern lead within
the Rutherglen Viticultural College reserve
and take a shot at the Indigo lead. This
tributary gutter was splendid in the upper
reaches, and the main Chiltern lead carried
good gold on both sides of the reserve. Wal-
lace Consols shaft is available, and the gutter
is dry, 20 years of pumping having sucked
most of the water out of it. To get the mine
going would have been simple in the heyday
of old John Cock, but alluvial is such a poor
game just now that it takes a lot to get in
the £5OOO wanted to reach the wash. Of
60,000 shares 15,000 go as paids to A. Hen-
derson of the Chiltern Valley Co., and 25,000
at ss. are offered to the public. Henderson
also gets £l3OO cash after registration; so
the loading isn’t heavy.
Victoria has a little curse coming home to
roost on All Fools’ Day, It is an old 5 p.c.
loan of £500,000; and present holders can in
exchange get either debentures or inscribed
stock, 41 p.c. interest, with three to five
years’ currency. No cash is being asked, for
by the Treasurer: he has already provided
himself with enough to meet any of the
maturing securities which are not converted.

The Thermo-Electric Ore Reduction Co.,
which has pitched camp at Wolfram (Q.),
has taken over the Mt. Carbine property and
mill from the Irvinebank Co.: £40,000 is
said to have been the price. Mount Carbine
is the biggest known thing in wolfram in
Australia, and will yield big quantities of
ore with a little development work. Mining
is cheap, too, as no timber or filling is
required. Mount Carbine and Koorboora
were the only two good things that the
Irvinebank Co. had left of its mining ven-
tures.

Follow the lead of experienced men. Alumino-
ferric is used by the N.S.W. Public Works Department,
Sydney Metropolitan, Newcastle and Hunter River
Water Boards, Ardlethan Mines, graziers, etc., for
precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, dams, etc. One pound clarifies 2000/3000
gallons of the most turbid water within three hours.
It is exceedingly cheap, and harmless for either
drinking or boiler water, and is used throughout
Australia. Water is a valuable asset in summer
weather for drinking, concentrating or boiler pur-
poses. Why not use Aluminoferric and have it
clear and crystalline? Sole Agents: Wm. Adams & Co.,
Ltd,, Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane, Perth. — Advt.
The latest copper-smelting plant to be erected in
Australia is that of the Mount Cuthbcrt Company
in North Queensland. It consists of buildings, power-
plant with boilers, generating sets and blowers,
blast furnace, copper converters and converter re-
fining plant, blister gathering and cleaning shed,
also steel bins for ore and coke. Wherever possible
the plant is electrically driven. The whole of the
plant was supplied by the Austral Otis Engineering
Co. of Melbourne, who have made numerous smelting
plants working in Australia at present.— Advt. [5

Australian and Other Patents. Obtainable through

The dream ships of William Morris Hughes, though
now not in the least likely to eventuate, have in one
direction at least borne good fruit in the establish-
ment of an entirely new Australian industry on a
large scale. Upon the idea of auxiliary schooners
being mooted. Nelson and Robertson, the Australian
agents for the “Skandia” crude oil engines, sprang
off the mark and completed arrangements with
Thompson, of Castlemaine, for the complete manu-
facture of these engines, upon the same design and
of the same materials throughout as the famous
Swedish engine. The Federal Government promptly
placed an order for a large marine set, and at
present orders on hand for larger-powered engines,
marine and industrial, total over four figures. The
engines turned out so far are declared by experts to
be even superior to the imported article, and it is
to be hoped that the enterprise exhibited by the firms
interested will meet with the success it deserves.
—Advt.

Sydney, February 18, 1918.
My Dear Abini, —
A thoughtless shipping company landed
the Davidsons on Sunday—a day before the
proposed official discovery of Australia at
Farm Cove. The Cabinet hurriedly decided
that it wouldn’t unland his Ex. just that he
might make a second invasion. Very sensi-
ble was the decision to let his Ex. get
through the hand-shakes at Government
House.
The city gets the first word when a new
Governor takes the oaths of office. Monday
morning’s ceremony at Government House
thus brought the Mayor, in his official
bearskins, to the fore. As civic parent Joyn-
ton Smith welcomed the just-arrived Cocked
Hat, and landed a few aldermen and the
Town Clerk at the sub-regal feet.
The Hall seethed with dignitaries. First
came the wigs—the Bar in its plainer horse-
hair curls with little tails behind.
The bigwigs, in their Charles 11. coif-
fures and judicial gowns, floated for-
ward beyond the red rope which hedged
in the mighty. Ministers of the
Crown, an Archbishop (the bald one), and
wives of Ministers lined the staircase;
the larger flotsam bobbed up and down like
corks around the State table. Minister
Reeby’s glasses flashed in the offing, and Chief
Secretary Fuller took a modest place on the
lee side of the documentarv evidence.
His Ex., a large kindly man with the
homely warmth of manner of one who has
lived amongst homely people, read his typed
statement; then seizing a jibbing pen he
signed his name to it. Deputy Cullen, now
plain Chief Justice again, disentangled his
fingers from his long honiton-lace ruffles, and
appended his signature. Official-Secretary
Budge read aloud the King’s Proclamation.
General Finn and hon. A.D.C. d’Apice
guarded the Davidson coat-tails.
On the stairs behind was Ladv Davidson;
and the miniature painters will soon long to
reproduce the delicate, refined face, with a
graceful top of grey hair and the pale pink
bloom of Canadian winters in the soft
cheeks. She had the Cullen dame on one
side and mrs. Holman at her left elbow.
After the new Gov. had kissed the Book
and (I suppose) sworn to tell the trut’, the
whole trut’ and nothing but the trut’, Pre-
mier Holman got his w T ord in. Easy and
graceful, it was the speech of the forenoon ,
and to address the lords and-commons cheer-
ily before lunch is a frosty task. After a
few vice-regal remarks —Sir Walter David-
son looks through plain, old-fashioned specs,
-—the meeting took to its heels. For the
circus was to begin outside, and the funeral
and other coaches usually hired for these
occasions were waiting to make a procession
round the city.
Attorney-General Dave Hall and his lady
go to Japan for a trip presently. It has been
a dreadfully explosive time lately. The
Ministry has threatened to blow up at any
moment. Somehow the two lots won’t mix
comfortably. Down south Mr. Hughes is
warning the Libs, that he won’t stand any
squeezing, and he’ll go back to the Labs, if
somebody doesn’t look out. And the Labs,
are saying they’re blessed (or something) if
he will!
Having performed the “dual duties” of
Acting-Gov. and Chief Justice, Cullen, C.J.,
has been offered another two months’ holi-
day. Of course it may not be accepted. The
dual pay may have been solace enough.
“I am singing in New York to-morrow at
11 a.m. —God help me! Yet it is pleasant
to earn £5OO before lunch.” Thus Melba,
writing in the midst of her 25th anniversary
of song in the U.S.A.
There’s an Assistant Minister for Defence.
How do I know? Because he opened Syd-
ney’s new Red Cross kitchen. It’s cheek-by-
jowl with the big warehouse area, and its
scones puff out on one corner of York-street.
His name’s Groom, and having said those
things required of him he had an opportunity
to test the cooking. Mrs. Holdship is in
charge. Having been well brought up she
didn’t mention the Sixth Division to him.
The Shellbacks had their annual outing on
Saturday, with genial old Capt. Green to
name the toast. Surgeon Scot Skirving, not
long back from the Front, was one of the
speakers. He’s an ex-seaman. And—-do
you know? —a novelist, too. Years ago he
was guilty of “Love and Longitude.” But
lie’s done lots of better things since.
I rom an Australian girl in France:—
'V e started our Y.M.C.A. in a marquee, and as it
rains all the .day every day, we used to stand for 12
hours in gum boots serving tea and cakes to the
boys. We lived a mile away down a steep hill, and
the gum boots and the walk through slush nearly
killed me. You see, we had all to be inoculated for
typhoid, and it pulled me down so much that I got
'thi’, and the horrid thing wouldn’t go. Life is drier
now, as we are in wooden huts with dry floors and
can change our shoes; also the Y.M.C.A. has
taken a villa belonging to a French countess for the
women-workers, and as it’s quite close to the camp
that everlasting walk at 10.30 is a horror of the past.
Me have no life outside our job—just one day a
fortnight off to have tea in a real town and get our
liair waved. But life is so full that we have hardly
time to mend our stockings. We wear holland over-
alls over our frocks, and our dress money goes
in heavy field boots (very expensive), wool undies,
ditto sweaters and good rubber hats which don’t spoil
in the eternal rain. The frumpish things cost twice
as much as the muslin frocks I used to wear in old
sunny Sydney. But one thing that the war has torn
from me I don’t miss—my umbrella !
Our local nobility are sorting out their
offspring to pick likely candidates for the
task of playing ladies and other games with
the infant Davidsons. “How to llomp in
Upper Circles’’—-I wonder if there’s a text-
book on the subject.
Assistant-Treasurer ,T. C. L. Fitzpatrick’s
daughter Dorothy has accepted the usual
token from her young man, Quartermaster-
Sergeant Bennett, of Woolwich.
“Australia Jane’s” line from Brisbane :
The Grange Hill convalescent home has been
opened by his Ex. Mrs. George Forrest is the hon.
matron, and six V.A.D. girls are to manage the
housekeeping and cooking. A library and billiard
table, a lounge and a piano are provided for
Biiljim’s use. Grange Hill is the old Raff home,
and has been handed over rent free.
A few months ago a little band of workers under
Mrs. Lockhart Gibson set out to give the Soldiers’
Comforts Fund a lift by making jam. So far over
5000 bottles have been sold and the fund has been
benefiting to the extent of £25 a month. A
bottle-shortage, however, is handicapping the fruit-
boilers ; and if Brisbane has any spare jars lying
about it is implored to send them to the rooms in
Adelaide-street.
Mrs. Ted Antill, the War Chest’s tireless
hon. superintendent, has been snatching odd
days on the Mountains. Mrs. Morris, a sister,
and General Christian, a cousin, have fore-
gathered in the Leura cottage. In spite of
mushroomv weather, the invalided artillery-
man has been getting up his strength, and
can poke round the golf-links once or twice
in a day.
Mrs. Bertie Willsallen and her sister, Airs.
Sheldon, had a miserable homecoming in a
recently-arrived American boat. The former
brought back her late husband’s body to be
buried in the vault on the big sheep-run out
Gunnedah way. The pastoralist and one-
time polo-olayer was told here that his case
was hopeless. Then came tales of the won-
ders achieved by a U.S.A. specialist. He
tried his luck and died.
At the age of 12
Percy Leason turned
an old piano-case
into a studio. Then
he settled down to
work. Only in the
evenings, of course.
Father had a farm
in the Victorian
Mallee, and there
was plenty of work
to do. As the studio
—it was behind the
stable—was regarded
without enthusiasm
by the family, the
artist had to solve
the lighting problem
himself. This he did
by scraping the pith
out of reeds, soaking
the fragile tapers in fat, and setting them
alight. A guttering, smellful flare was the
result; but it sufficed. There were odd days
of stolen joy when the young draughtsman
went out into the bush to lie under the trees
and try to sketch the wild birds of the
Mallee.
After school-time —school was at Kaniva
—came the cold prose of apprenticeship tj
a sign-painter and house-decorator—which
was supposed to satisfy his inclination for
art, while giving a prospect of tucker. But
that didn’t prove a success. Then Leaso.l
got to Melbourne—tried litho work, and put
his soul (pro tern.) into jam-labels. From
this billet he was abruptly pushed by his
employer, when his term was up, as a youth
who had no business instincts. A job in a
drapery store, in which he was responsible
for the price labels, came to an equally un-
timely end through a wicked habit he had of
mutilating the window cards with his draw-
ings. Then followed commercial work for
a crust, with evenings spent at the National
Gallery in art-classes under MeCubbin —a
brief episode, but all the regular teaching
that has come his way.
A young artist with the gifts of humor
and imagination, and almost incredible per-
sistence in overcoming obstacles, Lea son
came to Sydney about a year ago. Before
leaving Victoria his decorative three-color
drawing’s in “Here is Faery” gave him his
first whiff of publicity, lie is doing better
still in the black-and-white sketches for a
new volume of selected poems by Henry
Lawson. Angus and Robertson, the publish-
ers, wisely chose an artist who knows the
bush to illustrate these songs <»f the great
spaces. Shy as a mallee-hen. or even as
Henry Lawson himself, he finally faced
Judith Fletcher’s camera. The little photo
goes with this.
Lieut. Lebby Hordern is back from the
r ront on six months’ leave.
Another homing pigeon. Captain Car-
michael, looks a sun-and-wina-tanned warrior.
Leichhardt ( his strictly Labor constituency)
will probably overlook the A.D.C. he acquired
as shipmate of the new Governor. Jack
Virgo, the general sec. of the Y.M.C.A., was
also aboard. He is on one of his usual boun-
dary-ruler’s rounds.
War alters many things, and Lent is to
be no longer an “off” season as far as It.C.
weddings are concerned. A recent decree
from Rome brushes aside the old edict, and
wipes out the “black fasts” from the
Church calendar, so that even the best-
behaved religionists may indulge in butter
and its greasy relations on the days those
substances were hitherto barred.
Jack Garry, of Garryowen, Binalong, for-
ward among the forwards of inter-State foot-
le" ? ew y ears a §'°> has been measured for
khaln. He owns a very bright wife (she
was Queenie MacCauley, of Mosman) and
two kiddies, also a B.E. of Sydney Univer-
sity, and for the latter reason has booked
with our Engineers for an indefinite season
abroad—and at home, too, judged by the
experience of some busy mining men' who,
after enlisting, have been kept hanging
around Australia for nearly 12 months.
A Sydney woman whose son is attached to
a sugar plantation in Fiji had one thrill
during her visit to the sooty island. When
she found it was the custom to dispense
with locked and barred doors and windows
at night, the strongest testimonials to the
docility of the very numerous nigger failed
to reassure her, so she took the precaution
to sprinkle a packet of tacks on the floor
before turning in. She was awakened by a
crash and a shriek that also roused the
household; and when everybody rushed in
expecting any old sort of tragedy, they
found the Black Brudder who took round the
early cup of tea busy grubbing ironmongery
out of his spacious foot. The burglar-alarm
was barred for the rest of her stay.
From a Summer Hill girl at present in
Kansas, U.S.A.-
Now that the boys are going from here the “ser-
vice flag’’ is seen everywhere. It's oblong and red,
hanging downward, with white centre and a blue
star for every enlisted member of the family, firm,
etc. In the small houses a card with “A man from
this house is serving his country” on it is placed in
tlie window. In the larger houses, and outside
offices, public buildings, colleges, etc., the flag is
flown. Harvard unfurled hers the ether day with
over 700 stars, and Kansas Uni. will fly one with
many hundred stars shortly. Small service flags
are seen at the rear windows of big limousines and
small cars alike, and tinv flags are being worn l>v
the women on their coats. Such an idea would
surely be welcomed in Australia, where so many
have given their loved ones, and it should put to
shame those who are still sheltering their eligibles.
The brooch “flags,” by the way, have reached
Australia, and some American women here
are already wearing them.
Surgeon Alex. MacCormick, his wife and
two daughters are unpacking after coming
safely through the perils of tin fish.
A- E. Hughes has bought the late “Wattie”
Holdship’s red-roofed bungalow near the goff-
linlts at Leura—where, by the way, aquatic
sports would have been the only possible out-
door frolic during recent icy winds and rain-
storm. Yet the local Mountain visitor turns
out regularly to get the air. And Fido. too !
The spectacle of a consequential little dog in
a scarlet coat, hand-led by adoring humans,
is a feature of the landscape.
Captain F. S. Betts is lion. sec. of this
week’s Light Horse gym. at Menangle. A
camel race—the riders to be pressmen—and
a melon melee help the show.
The Alexandra Hospital for sick kiddies
gets another cot as the result of Walter
i horman s students’ recital a few weeks
ago.
The recent fashion-display reveals
Esquimau fur coats and immense muffs with
the beasties’ heads and tails all over the
pelt. The heat of the lady who gets inside
these Arctic wraps will be about 200° in
the shade. The furrier is apparently as busy
as ever, and the black fox is losing his skin
with all the old pre-war frequency.
From the evening
long ago in Mel-
bourne when as a
mere lad he played
the flute as a soloist,
making his metro-
politan musical debut
by chance with
Melba, no one has
been so closely asso-
ciated with her
affairs as John
Lenimontj. lie is not
only her devoted
chum, but the man-
ager to whom in Aus-
tralia the Dominant
Voice leaves her busi-
ness concerns —abso-
lutely. As the faith-
ful watcher of the
many irons the singer has in the lire, John
has lately allowed his flute to slip into
second place. Yet the Victorian is admitted
everywhere to he one of the world’s greatest
flautists. A world-tour with Amy Sherwin
years ago; then round the globe with the
great English contralto, Janet Patey ; later
with the famous Irish bass Foley (on concert
programmes Signor Foli) ; flute recitals in
London, thanks to Melba’s introductions;
appearances at the English soirees, the
fashion of that day, at which the Skylark
sang; five tours with the inimitable Adelina
Patti—-to these add various concert trips
with Melba through TJ.S.A. and Canada, and
you’ll see that Leuimong has had an experi-
ence of world travel not beaten by many
Australians.
When Lemmone returned to his native
land he was in concert management for a
while. He brought Mark Hambourg on his
second tour. Also he was responsible for
Paderewski’s visit, and .smoothed the lion’s
mane with consummate tact —even Madame
Paderewski answered to the silken cord in
John’s deft hand. Here is his smiling face
(May Moore is responsible for the photo.),
the reflex of a buoyant Australian tempera-
ment.
The solitary quill—very often from the
coat of the lady who lays the breakfast egg—
is an autumn fixture. More or less, of course ;
a high wind may shatter its foundation in the
latest toque and make it wobble like a grass-
blade.
Comforts dames are rolling up their sleeves
to tackle the refreshment-tent business at
the coming show again. Last year’s deluge
hasn’t damped their spirits.
The summer sale is making way for the
autumn hat. It is likely to be as high as
a stovepipe, and fashioned of silk plush,
mostly black or cream.
An exile in Egypt meets a bright girl
I was acting clerk at the Referendum voting, and
my first voter was a V.A.D. I asked her rank. She
said: “I wear no stars, but I bear a heavy cross.”
“Like a General,” said I ; “he wears crossed swords.”
“Yes,” said the lady, with a grin ; “I rank as
honorary general’—but I wear crossed brooms!” I
felt I’d like to see more of her.
h reak knitting is being tried by country
womenfolk just now. It is a revival of the
old way of knitting wool direct from the
greasy fleece. Judging by samples seen it
promises to be a great boon to the women out-
back who can neither buy the spun wool nor
afford a wheel. The gins of the Lachlan
tribe used to knit dilly- and other bags
straight from the ’possum fur, doing it with
two sticks, and these bags often figured as
wedding presents. One bush spinnerette
who owns a wheel has, by the way, made
very passable knitting “wool” from ’possum
fur. In the Scotch Highlands the old
wives used to collect bits of wool from
barbed-wire fences and briar-bushes and knit
it up into socks as a Christmas gift for the
meenester; but the work done locally direct
from the whole fleece is a great improvement
on theirs.
The local bobby and his pal the fireman are
throwing in their lot together for a huge
carnival for the hospitals. March 2 is the
date.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN

The elimination of waste, and conservation of every grain, should be the endeavor
of Dentists, Jewellers and other workers of precious metals, and the best method of doing
this is to have their sweepings treated at Harringtons’ Laboratories and Metallurgical
Works.
The latest appliances are used for this class' class of work, and every grain that

We specialise in Assaying, Ore Testing and Metallurgical Analysis, and buy materials

Alloys, in Plate, Wire or Granulated.

Is your Piano
“Cobwebbed”
with neglect?
Although the Piano has always been con-
sidered an essential part of the home and
represents the outlaying of a large sum of
money, still as years go by it is astonishing
how little the Piano is used.
May be your Piano has not been played
for months and the keyboard never touched
from one week's end to another. Your original
intention of infusing plenty of music into the
home life has proved a disappointment.
Why let this disappointment continue? Why
be witnout the great pleasure of good music?

Melbourne, February 18, 1918.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The Caulfield Autumn meeting began on
Saturday, and baked Melbournites flocked
out to the heath to revel in the first cool
breeze of the season. But the women seemed
too exhausted by the continuous heat to
parade in their autumn frocks. Some of
the most prominent feminine racegoers re-
fused to leave their I’ortsea retreats at all.
Leslie Stuart, wearing a blue band on his
grey hat, came alone and left his smart
missus dipping at Sorrento. The debonair
Stewart Brunton also left his frocky lady at
the seaside. Fan Dango Mackay was so
sure that their Headwind was going to win
th(it she couldn’t face the excitement.
Headwind failed, but hubby Sam Mackay
won a fortune over Desert Gold. While being
cooled off in the birdcage after the race, the
Maori-lady had a ring of overawed worship-
pers round her, gazing as if she were a
revealed goddess. Roger Thompson was there
with his Canadian wife, in a dress to match
Madame’s blue eyes. Mrs. Weston Pett was
handing round warm informal invitations to
friends to meet young soldier son Archie,
who has been finishing a long convalescence
at their country home. Squatter Wood was
down, and his lady (she was one of the
Creswick daughters) wore blue tailorings.
The Rajah wore very pale-grey clothes and
a pleased smile; his horse Ivilflinn romped
in on the last race. The Also Ranee re-
mained at the seaside.
Muriel Starr came out in a curious brown
checked flannel dress like a shirt, and topped
it with a hat of blue patchwork. Marion
Marcus Clarke wore her white linen mis-
sionary frock. Mrs. Neil Crowley was a
study in green, from her shoes to a waving
green tulle scarf. Steward Croker strolled
in solitary state with a lovely waist to his
pale-grey coat. Mrs. Clive Leonard had
sister Miss Reynolds with her, and this just-
returned girl was overwhelmed with greet-
ings. Her smart fawn London clothes were
eyed enviously. Mrs. Russell Gabb was out
in a gold-tissue hat and some mastic-grey
tailorings of cute design. Mrs. Barry
Thompson spread an acreage of dove-grey
crepe on the lawn. Some invalided Canadian
soldiers struck a new note in khaki. Their
hats were a queer shape, and behind them
they wore their battalion colors.
Melbourne and Sydney detectives went
over to Tassy to watch visiting crooks dur-
ing the Speck’s great fortnight. It was a
ripping idea and it worked out splendidly
until one Sherlock Holmes, after escorting
a suspect to the stewards’ room, found he
had collected a person of the very first
social importance.
The G.-G., Lady Helen and party are
touring the lovely North-West Coast of Tas-
mania. Their motor road runs in enchanted
semi-circles, following the outline of the
coast. The numerous bays have golden
sandy beaches below the vivid green of the
boobyallas—the native banyans—and the
rocks jut out into hundreds of little head-
lands where penguins sit and consider the
deep blue ocean. The inland side of the
wonderful road is mostly jungles of ferns
and occasional little townships nestling
below the mountain crests. Ragged red clay
cliffs and torn forests show where the tin,
silver and zinc are being hauled out by
inartistic Man.
There are murmurs and mutterings that
the Bigjohn Forrests are going “Home” —
not to the democratic West, as you might
imagine, but to the Home of Titles. The
gossips have also discovered that the Forrest
lady’s family tree has a peer somewhere in
the branches.
Niece Muriel North has arrived hurriedly
from the West to share in the glory of Uncle
Bigjohn’s peerage. Muriel is so much with
Auntie Forrest as to be practically an
adopted daughter. The Forrests have no
chick of their own.
A tall, slim goddess who appeared on the
Block one afternoon recently created a deal
of repressed excitement and drew a full
house into one of the largest afternoon-tea
depots. She had the most golden hair ever
seen on a woman’s head, and a lot of it. But
what caught the crowd was that she had
persuaded the large, ostentatious poodle she
was carrying to be exactly the same color.
Everybody wears her pearls under her
day-time blouses now. Most jewels are
stowed in safety vaults these dull days; but
pearls lose lustre and blacken if not worn,
so Paris has discovered that they look sweet
and discreet under black chiffon.
Melba and her pal Lady Susan Fitz-
Clarence spent Christmas as guests of the
G.-G. of Canada and his Duchess of Devon-
shire. Heavy falls of snow provided them
with plenty of tobogganing, sleiehing and
skiing. The time of Melba’s return to Aus-
tralia is indefinite, as she has thoughts of a
peep at Vladivostock.
The Edward Yencken girls hostessed a
young people’s frivol last week to farewell
the captain brother who has been home on
leave. The difficulty of getting suitable male
support for flapper frivols these times is
tremendous. But the modern Toorak miss
is so grateful for an invitation to any sort of
gaiety that she doesn’t ask impossibilities of
her hostess. In a social sense these are
chastening times.
Dr. McCarthy has been in Melbourne on
his way back from Tasmania to Sydney. On
Thursday they dragged him into a consulta-
tion with several Melbourne doctors, to dis-
cuss the best treatment for Basil Hart,
musician Fritz’s young son, who is still bat-
tling against the effects of his shooting
accident.
Dr. Ellis, our busy lady medico, is due
back from far places in March.
Black silk skirted gowns are the latest
splash for the water. Mrs. Buchanan (she
was Dr. Freda Gamble befoi’e she married) is
snorting the smartest of its kind at Portsea.
She can show it off, too, with acrobatic turns
on the springboard. That handsome Edin-
burgh woman, Mrs. Glenloth Peter, also dips
in black silk expensiveness; so does the
aesthetic Mrs. Robb —or did.
Mrs. Bridges came down from the Western
District last week to meet the only son of
the house, Lieut. Jack. He is over here to
help settle up his Admiral-father’s hand-
some estate before getting back to the Front.
The Parker knight, from Westralia, is shel-
tering in a South Yarra liasherie with daugh-
ters Ethel and Dolly. A flapper grand-
daughter has been deposited at a Geelong
school. The family is excited over the pros-
pective return of one of the soldier sons, who
is suffering from an overdose of shrapnel. A
previous thrill was supplied by his elder
brother Hubert, who, when convalescing in
Scotland, married his patriotic host’s younger
daughter before getting back to the trenches.
Last week a small mayoral dinner was
spread for a mixed party of guests in the
boudoir rooms reserved for the Mayoress, a: i
the pop-pop-pop reminded one of a machine-
gun at practice. Melody was laid on, for the
new city lady is intensely musical. Banana-
lander Cahill was at the piano, tenor Brookes
sang, and some naice girl amateurs also con-
tributed a warble or two. The Mayoress asked
Cahill for a dreamy nocturne, and Cahill
dreamed as requested through a length of
Chopin, pitting his music unsuccessfully
against the chattering women. The she-diners
were very much undressed and wore pints of
jewels. The loudness of their finery was
only matched by the noise that floated up
from the big hall below, where a Mannix
meeting was raging.
A somewhat damaged cat came back from
the wars the other day, and Jack Swanson,
city councillor and one of the jolly builder
brethren, parted with £lOO when it meowed
at him. When on recruiting work early in
the war he promised to each of the first
two men to volunteer a mascot, with the
sporting offer that he’d hand over £lOO on
their safe return with the mascot, which was
a cat in each case. No w r ord to date of the
fate of cat No. 2.
The managing director and shareholders
of the Gippsland and Northern Co-operative
Co. lately gave a champagne dinner to C. J.
Newton, recently their London representa-
tive, who is leaving the firm.
Cousin Adelaide’s gossip :
Some people haye freakish luck. At the end of
January one W. L. Jacques, who jumped off the
Semaphore jetty to save a drowning boy, lost a ring
while in the water. Last week a diver found the
trinket on the sea floor and returned it to its owner.
We have a new orchestra, 14 strong, composed
entirely of returned soldiers, all of them too badly
wounded to engage in heavy work. The baton will
be tapped for the first time in public at a concert in
the Queen’s Hall on Wednesday. A second appear-
ance is to be made at the Trench Comforts River
Continental on the following Saturday.
Monday night saw the launching of a Women’s
Representation League, with Mrs. T. Freebairn as
“chairman,” Mrs. J. F, Young and Miss Florence
Haycraft, B.Sc., as honorary secretaries, and Mrs.
Crawford Vaughan as a member of the committee.
The main object of the League is to encourage the
candidature of women for all elections, municipal
and Parliamentary. One of its first reps, at the
hustings will probably be Mrs. Jeanne F. Young. It
is threatened that she will be nominated for a subur-
ban (State) electorate.
The engagement is announced of Miss Crozier, only
daughter of the Elliott Croziers, of Brimbago station,
to L. Y. Langdon, second son of the E. W. Langdons,
of Millswood.
Captain F. T. Bruce, who has done excellent work
as organising secretary to the State Recruiting Com-
mittee, has resigned in favor of a returned soldier
suitable for the job. Incidentally, he has also made
three attempts to be passed for active service.
Commander Boddam Whettam, who was here with
the Challenger as a good-looking sub.-lieut. 'and since
the war has been on watch in the North Sea, has got
his D.S.O. He was married a few years ago to
pretty Mrs. Gordon Ayers.
The count of Friday’s Button Day takings showed
the usual fake coins—buttons, halfpennies wrapped in
silver paper to do duty as shillings, and bits of tin-
ware that had passed muster as half-crowns. Patriot-
ism runs in all misshapes.
Another South Australian to make his mark is
E. A. Kruss, whose aptitude for aeronautics has got
him the billet of senior flying-instructor at the San
Diego army school, the largest in the U.S.A. He is
due in France in the Spring in command of a flying
section.
Our Art Gallery has just received an astonishing
consignment of Old Masters belonging to the late
P. J. Cels, the Belgian who died in Adelaide lately
from the after-effects of gassing. He had decided to
settle in sunny S. Aus., and offered his pictures—
which he valued at £75,000 —to this State for
£40,000. The collection contains works, whose
genuineness has not so far been questioned, by Van
Dyck, Rubens, Teniers, Cuyp and the like. It would
be a marvellous acquisition Ur Adelaide Gallery,
which has a fine lot of modern work; but we can’t
think of £40,000 in war-time. Still, the pictures
are to be exhibited, so that we may have a new
thrill.
The Government has changed the location for the
Returned Soldiers’ Club. It was to be a central
building in Grenfell-street. It is to be the com-
modious old Ayers mansion on North Terrace, lately
known as Austral Gardens. The Gardens C'o, turned
one end of the land into an open-air theatre; at
the other end it built a dancing-hall. The war came
just in time to render this useless, and it is now
occupied by the Defence Department as a pay-office.
In between is the old mansion, which should make
a fine club. The Government is paying the “rent,
£5OO a year, for five years.
Mavis Thiel, one of the most decorative
girls in Melbourne and a daughter of the
striking-looking Fred Thiel pair, is to marry
Gordon Chirnside. The news of the engage-
ment has just been “released,” as the film
people say. Mavis is here to be congratu-
lated, but Gordon is in France acting as
A.D.C. to General Birdwood. The fiancS
went away with the first contingent, and is
a son of the late Robert Chirnside. His
mother is now wife of Justice Hodges. Mavis
has just received a wonderful ring, a wonder-
ful pendant and a diamond-studded watch
from her soldier.
Word came to the Harry Ilentys last week
that the son and heir, while holidaying from
the trenches, had booked himself for a wed-
ding. A postscript announced that the knot
had been tied. Letters with all particulars
about the bride will be in the next mail.
The New York papers are throwing head-
lines round the war romance of Jean Knox,
of Toorak, and Lieut. Eric Murray, who
sailed from England on the Andrania to meet
and marry his girl. A U-boat gave his ship a
knock-out blow, and the survivors travelled
almost round this troubled world before they
struck England again. Jean and her soldier
were re-united at last the other day, and
sentimental New York stood on tiptoe to see
them going to church.
Volatile Rita Kiddle changed her name to
McGeagh some weeks ago. The bridegroom
is a major in the Territorials and has a hor-
ribly rich father who hires a Kentish castle
called Hadlow. The bride is a daughter of
the one-time owner of Walbundrie station
(N.S.W.), most of which was sold some years
ago. The Kiddle estate had dwindled con-
siderably before the late owner passed out.
Brother Geoff.,.killed in action last year, left
half his share to his fiancee. This amounted
to a few hundreds a year. The other half
went to sister Rita.
Victoria must be full of young people in
quest of vocal training gratis. The latest
advt. of a scholarship offered by Theophilus
Roberts brought 211 entries, and nearly all
the aspirants came to have their voices put
to the test. Mr. Roberts found 29 of them
good enough for the final. Some were
strikingly good, and most of the singers
showed sufficient intelligence, if not the other
necessary qualifications, for a Career. The
scholarship went to a blonde, tall, youthful-
looking soprano of 16—Dorothy Home,
daughter of Rev. Stirling Home, vicar of
Buninyong.
Talking of scholarships, Adrian Holland,
who went to London on the R.A.M. award
for Australia, was lately required to turn
his dainty piano hands to military account.
But the medical test stopped him right
away. Three other doctors confirmed the
first verdict and decided that Adrian (son
of Edwin Holland, of Melbourne) was unfit
for any sort of military service whatever, on
account of an extraordinary heart —the onlv
heart of the sort, the doctors say, they e\ei
struck.
Walter Appendicitis Kirby, touring Tass.v,
put in last week “doing” Bayswater and
Sandy Bay.
Little Vera Carew has also flown over to
the Apple Island, and is singing and charm-
ing the Hobart people with her art.
Walter Kirby’s enthusiasms for rank and
fashion sometimes carry him off his feet and
on to other people’s corns. He was giving
a concert in Hobart, and though Lady Helen
was touring the little island she could not be
present. But —joy ! —the Monash lady arrived
with a party of friends. Walter rushed for-
ward, begged them to “walk this way,” and
gallantly made room for the new lady of title.
But the poor courtier could not have known
that in his exertions he half-obliterated
Lieut.-Governor Nicholls and his lady, who
were sitting in the front chairs without any
distinguishing marks of office.
“Snowy Baker” is to burst silently on
the public per medium of the screen. A
thrilling reel or two with a rescue of a
maiden from an ocean rock is one feature,
and there are also knock-out blows, I.W.W.’s
and other melodramatic items.
The Souter-Hill opera at the Princess’s
runs along on bare legs and feet. There, is
a salutary Carlyle-like revelation in seeing
chorus-girls’ extremities absolutely in the
nood. Legs and feet encased in fleshings
and silk slippers have some charm. Cut out
the slippers and stockings in the interests
of Art and you have just legs and feet again.
The ladies of “The Rajah of Shivapore”
cannot stand the severe test. One girl has
an unmistakable bunion. The fair heroine
wisely softens the bare effect with silk
anklets. Remember Nellie Stewart’s bare
feet? They were a picture—but only be-
cause Nellie had been as keen on pedicure
as on manicure for many years. For Trilby,
Nell Gwynn and a barefoot panto, boy, the
beauty had her well-kept feet massaged,
polished and henna-tipped regularly every
morning.
Little - red-thatched ex-mummer Malcolm
Laughton, of Tasmania, passed through Mel-
bourne lately, clad in khaki, to rejoin his unit
in Sydnev. Laughton took to the greasepaint
at the same time aB .Charlie Hammond, also
from Tassy, and played through the Tyrone
Power-Edith Crane season. He also did
sound work in George Musgrove’s Shaks-
pearean season, when pretty Norah Kerin
was the young star.
March 15 is the date of the big appeal by
the State War Council for money to help
wounded soldiers and their dependants. One
or' the lures of the day will be the C.T.A. sale
of surprise-packets. Herschell, of Pathe.
Frhres, has placed his services as organiser
at the disposal of the association, and 200,000
packets will be offered at a bob apiece. One
of them will be worth a block of land which
takes in a bit of Mount Dandenong. and
there are other prizes on a like magnificent
scale.
Miss de Lancy Forth is the new secretary
of the Old Newspaper Committee, at the Vic-
toria League, Collins-street, Melbourne. She
sorts out and sends newspapers to boys at the
Front, and (please note this!) she never has
too many. Send along what you can spare
and make a note of the address. It’s an easy
way of doing a great kindness to Billjim.
The stodgy old Athenaeum has so far for-
gotten itself as to build a reading-room for
our snubbed sex and appoint one of us
assistant librarian. The Athenaeum job was
secured against over 100 applicants ( mostly
males) by Alice Cooper, daughter of J. B.
Cooper, who made a hit a year or so ago
with his war novel, “Coo-00-ee,” and has just
received word that Hodder and Stoughton
have accepted a second. “Leg Bail: A Tale
of Bass Straits in the ’Thirties.”
Tours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

It’s Nourishment Your Nerves Need,
not Dangerous Drugs.
To use morphia to make you sleep, stimulants to drive your jaded brain and
body, or opiates to ease your headaches or other nerve pains, is about the worst
thing you can possibly do. At best they only afford temporary relief while their
reactions call for more and more drugging, till ultimately the patient becomes a
slave to the drug habit. The proper thing to do is to purify and enrich your blood
and nourish your nerves, for which purpose you cannot get anything better than
HeaN s T o nic Nerve Nuts
They are economical as well as being efficient. Boxes containing twelve days’
supply cost 3/-, or six boxes for 17/3. If not stocked by your chemist or store,
send direct to G. W. HEAN, Manufacturing Chemist, 178 Castlereagh Street’
Sydney; or Hean’s Pharmacy, Wanganui, New Zealand.

ALITIES
“F.N.”: Barcoo rot and spew are pos-
sibly due to flies and a mutton diet, in the opinion
ot “H.W.F.” (B. 24/l/’18), who asks for the experi-
ence of somebody who has been where beef is the
meat most eaten. I spent a year in the Far North,
above Cairns, where the flies are the limit. Apart
from an occasional goat, beef was the only meat
there. Vegetables were scarce. Dry-’ash (including
onions), bullock's ’eart stuffed (with onions), steak
(an’ onions) and roast or boiled beef (with boiled
onions) were the usual dishes at the leading ranches ;
but only one mild case of barcoo ever came under
my notice. Singularly enough, the party was a
non-beerer, though the beer of the Far North, unless
it comes out of a bottle, can’t, as a rule, be recom-
mended to anybody in the pursuit of health, wealth
and happiness. As for the idea of “Caveman” (B.
24/1/’]B), attributing the disorder to “the state of
the blood brought about by constant toil under a
burning sun,” 1 saw nothing in the blazing North to
bear out that opinion,
* * * *
“F.”: “I Say” touches me on a sensitive
spot when he advocates the taxation and abolition of
cats (B. 24/l/’18). I can hardly help bursting into
loud applause at the idea, though my natural fear
of ridicule prevents my joining an Anti-Cat League.
There are three large and three small cats in my
garden. They aren’t allowed in the house, for the
diphtheritic reasons stated by “I Say” ; but they
seem to be a useless crew, inside or outside. One
night last week I saw a rat sitting at my front
gate, silhouetted against the star-shiny wetness of
the ground after rain. The cats, however, were all
away in the back garden, entertaining. Mice inhabit
the place, too, as a matter of course. When a cat is
specially introduced into the house to make short
work of a sure thing, nothing happens. Puss simply
noses around, looking for an exit, so that she may
return to the pig-bucket; and every now and again
I find on the lawn the feathers of a thrush or black-
bird, which she seems to prefer to vermin.
“I Say” is a sensible bloke ; but even now I shudder
at the thought of murdering- the lot —six cats in
all! Will “I Say” act up to his principles and
come and do it for me?
“W.R.P.” : While droving from the head-
waters of the Burnett down to Dalby (Q.) some years
ago I got Barcoo rot so badly that the policeman
at one of the townships called me off because of
my .awful sores. He instructed me to burn every-
thing that I had used, so as not to pass the curse
on to others, and even offered to help me to make
arrangements with the Afghan storekeeper to get
my tucker sent to me if I would camp four miles
out in the scrub. For months before the attack
started I had lived on salt beef, pumpkin and sweet
potatoes, with occasional fresh beef, scrub or plain
turkey, and the inevitable damper, but no mutton.
I cured myself with an iron-quinine mixture internally
and a mixture of Venice turps, carbolic acid and
lard externally; stopped all meats, except an odd
pigeon, and lived on damper, fruits and vegetables.
I got rid of the scar marks by prolonged treatment
with cold cream. My theory—-simply impoverished
blood.
* * * *
“New Vagabond” : The alleged new fence
that “Dead Fence” (B. 24/l/’18) didn’t discover is
a “stud fence” in the bush glossary. The studs, of
course, are the rails. Twenty-five years ago the dad
and I put up 110 chains of this brand of barrier in
six weeks (winter time), and only used lewt. of No.
10 wire to tie the tops of the twin posts together,
the top rail resting on the wire near the head of
the posts. I cut the stringy and dad sunk the, holes
and built as fast as I could cut. Our panels ran
from four to five yards apart. The fence is still
there, and the delirious price of wire these days has
forced us on to the same kind on a new block.
Can t Australians make us decent fencing wire at a
reasonable figure?
* * * *
“Ligamudu”: For Barcoo rot try this.
Put a tablespoonful of bicarbonate of soda into a
beer bottle and fill it with water. At meal times
put on table alongside plate, and when the desire
to commune with nature manifests itself drink a
mouthful. A complaint similar to Barcoo and locally
known as nadi sickness was at one time very
prevalent during the hot months on N. and W.
coast of Viti Levu, Fiji, and the above was ai cure
for it. Am inclined to think that flies were the
cause of the trouble, helped probably by tinned meat
and scarcity of fresh vegetables. Years ago flies
were the most noticeable feature of the scenery, and
a flv-whisk was as necessary as boots, but since the
ant has added the fly egg to his menu the pest is not
so marked, and nadi has about disappeared, v ictims
to this sickness were usually attacked during or
shortly after meals, and very suddenfy.

“Wart”: There is a much-debated ques-
tion among- egg-collectors as to how many settings
a bird will lay if the fruit is repeatedly taken. I know
a blue crane’s nest on the banks of the Tamar (Tas.),
whence the eggs were taken four times in one season
ai fortnight intervals. On the first three occasions
there were three eggs, the last time only two, so that
chook laid 11 eggs in place of the usual three. I
know of a case, too, where a Tasmanian honey-eater’s
eggs were taken three times in one season. Curiously
enough, each time there was a pallid cuckoo’s egg
in the nest. This seems to bear out the theory that
the cuckoo does not lay an egg and leave the rest to
chance, but hangs about to see how its offspring gets
on.
* * ■# »
“Johanna”: I saw a white magpie this
year in the bush along the new road between the
Bern River and Mallacoota (Vic.). It was lily
white ; not the creamy white of the plentiful cockatoo.
This white magpie was a great dandy, excellently
groomed and of smooth, satiny plumage like his
piebald brother. Saw another example of white
Australia. A stockman at Mallacoota brought in a
baby kangaroo which was pure white. Mamma, who
disappeared in the chase, was quite white also.
Mother kangas throw baby from the pouch when
hunted. Probably they return and pick up the infant
when danger is past. Our little freak refused milk
and departed this iife. On the other hand, a young
grey ’roo, aged two weeks, flourished and became
a pet about the. house. It used to follow one of
the girls like a kitten, and was fed on bread
and milk, and presently it nibbled grass. Its chief
occupation was sucking his toe, as a human baby
sucks his thumb. He sucked all the hair off one big
toe, and when his goddess bandaged the member he
started on the other. Soon he was hopping about
with as many rags as a convalescent Billjim.
* * * *
“R.A.8.”: Re black twin lambs, I have
been 20 years Chasing jumbucks and have seen only
one pair. They are a pigeon pair, dropped last
month. I marked them last week, when they were
both well and hearty, and they can be seen with
their mamma till the end of next month, when they
will be weaned. Have only once seen an ordinary
black ewe (other than of a special black flock) with
a black lamb, and that was in 1916. In 1917 she
decided on a piebald one. Don’t know what her
intention is this year.
* * * *
“Engine”: Inform “Tom Tit,” who in-
quired for an authentic record of a pure white
magpie, that Mrs. Doyle, who kept the pub at
Girilambone (N.S.W.) thirty years ago, had one, a
splendid talker. At Cobar they had a white crow.
I think Father Curran became the owner.

OF MANY HORSES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
New, many sing- the thoroughbred, the steed of match-
less grace,
The hero horse who always sped to victory in the
race,
But few will sing the homely horse, the patient,
plodding hack
Who gives his quiet toil to man in lonely lands out
back;
By hidden ways,
Through weary days,
In lonely lands out back.
Fields yet uncleared will gleam and glow with golden
glint of grain,
And orchards as the seasons go will fade and bloom
again ;
Rich harvests on the bushland slopes, where fern
and scrub grow now,
Will ripen o’er the hoof-prints of the horse who pulls
the plough ;
Round stumps and stone
In lands unsown
lie pulls the creaking plough.
By leagues of barren button-grass, on rotting eordu-
roy,
Where sunning lizards see him pass, and stinging
flies annoy;
Along the silent bushland ways, the dark fern-
shadowed track,
And up the spurs of mountains goes the horse who
bears the pack;
Afar he tramps
To bark-built camps,
The horse who brings the pack.
The school-house in the out-back land is white and
plain and small,
Around the little playground stand the bush trees,
straight and tall;
From scattered farms the children come to heed the
teacher’s rule,
Their old cart jolts behind the horse who takes the
kids to school;
He jogs along
To childish song
(Vnd takes the kids to school.
The teamster cracks his curling whip, the waggon
wheels go round,
They start along the bush ward trip where railways
are not found;
The wheelers strain, the leaders stress, the sweating
bodies gleam,
And up the long red roadway move the horses of the
team ;
With mighty load
They tread the road,
The horses of the team.
And when the bushland heroes go to Europe’s fields
of blood,
Where Northern stars but palely glow beyond the
ocean flood,
They marshal in the ranks of men, they stay the
hate-filled Ilun,
A HERO.
SHE: “The bloke I marry will ’ave the
Victoria Cross.”
H E: “Oh, well, ’e'll deserve it.”
And near them still his hoof-beats fall, the horse
who pulls the gun ;
Where shot and shell
Make earth a hell,
He pulls the limbered gun.
Oh, many sing the thoroughbred, the steed of match-
less grace,
The hero horse who always sped to victory in the
race;
But worthier of a nobler verse, this patient, homely
hack
Who gives his faithful toil to man in lonely lands
out back ;
And great songs scrolled
On deathless gold
Should sing the horse out back.
TASMANIA.
C.C.D.

FOSTER’S LAGER
“Steve Hart”: If “Mahomet” (B.
10/1/’IS) wants hard droving let him try some cross-
bred hoggets just off the shears. Lincolns will lie
down; the hoggets gallop, and they’ll find every
slack wire on the road. But for the dizzy limit of
droving try goats. 1 was a goat myself once, and
took delivery of 600 others to escort down the Bogan.
The second day, dogs, horses, the cook and myself
were knocked up, while the goats, fresh as paint,
were careering over the landscape in nine different
directions. An indignant owner has been scouring
the West Bogan scrub for them ever since. He’s
looking for me too !
* * * *
“Harrison O.”: “Eddyson’s” yarn (B.
li/l/’l7) about country families whose members do
not play speaks reminds me of a middle-aged farm-
ing couple in the Bannockburn (Vic.) district, who,
although alone in the house, used frequently to
quarrel and refuse to exchange a word for weeks.
At one time whenever I c<juld escape from the roar
and bustle of Geelong I put in a few weeks with
them as a boarder, and more often than not a war
was in progress. Mrs. Tubb used to intone weird
dirges while going about her household jobs, and by
patient investigation I succeeded in learning the
inwardness of these concert items. When her Ben
had done something so dashed exasperating that it
was only by the exercise of superb self-control that
she was able to refrain from speaking to him, Mrs.
Tubb would relieve her feelings by putting her
opinion of Tubb into song. Sometimes she would
incorporate her opinion of Ben in the Old Hundredth,
at others she would besmirch his character through
the National Anthem. Tubb, too, would burst into
song in reply, but he lacked his wife’s facility. One
night, yarning with Tubb before going to sleep (we
both had stretchers on the verandah), I endorsed
his opinion that women were all fools and vam-
pires, but ventured to suggest that, as he was
apparently loaded with his particular vampire for
life, it was just as well to make the best of her.
During the next day I saw no sign that my hints
regarding the desirability of a reconciliation were
likely to be acted on, but in the evening, while Tubb
was putting some fly-proof netting on one of the
\\ indows, 1 heard him intoning, to the tune of
Shall We Gather at the River,” an edifying sermon,
the text of which had reference to the brevity of
human existence and the foolishness of prolonged
bitterness. Later I heard Mrs. Tubb at work on
the Old Hundredth, but was not able to catch the
diift of her remarks. Still, I think it had something
to do with olive branches, because that night Tubb's
stretcher was not occupied.
Cross-cut”: Can any Abo. give this its
correct name? it is a little fellow similar to a
’possum, body covered with dark brown fur, white
bars on back, bushy tail (sparsely-haired and like a
pipe cleaner), nose sharp like a rat. It is a nocturnal
forager and got bushed in my tent; consequently I
had a good view of it. Have travelled from
Mingenew in W.A. to Burketown in Q., but this, is
the first of the family that I’ve seen.

“Splodge”: After all, the Papuan native
is a nice kind old thing at times —at any rate, that
section of him that is put into a blue uniform and
made a Village Constable. I learned this when I
travelled several miles on foot with an official in
a coastal district, passing through various villages.
On entering each we were greeted by the local con-
stable, who solemnly saluted with one hand and
handed us a fresh cocoanut with the other. One
enterprising V.C. was good enough to send his aged
father two or three miles further ahead on the next
stage to present us with a newly-opened cocoanut
at a particularly exposed and hot part of the track,
where we were likely to be most thirsty. When we
were within one village of our destination heavy
rain set in. He were just considering whether to
wait for the storm to pass over or to push on and
put up with a drenching, when along came the
inevitable Hop with two umbrellas! Such unex-
pected hospitality was overwhelming, but we managed
to control our delight sufficiently to calmly, almost
disdainfully, accept the loan of the brollies as if
we were really expecting an oilskin coat and a pair
of goloshes apiece as well. The native owners of
the gamps (the V.C. had requisitioned them in the
King’s name) followed anxiously in our wake to the
next hamlet, where-,they repossessed themselves of
their treasures with visible relief. Umbrellas are
quite in vogue with civilised brown brother nowadays.
In fact, all the best people in dusky Papuan society
possess a gamp somewhere in the family.

Jim Carew”: Black twin lambs (B.
24/l/’18) are rare. Four years ago one of our ewes,
a merino-Border Leicester crossed to a Shropshire
ram, gave birth to three lambs, two black and one
white. One of the niggers died, but the other two
survived.

“R. 0.”: I was riding along the Toora-
weenah road (N.S.W.) the other day when I came
upon a ragged, thirsty kelpie shooing along a fair-
sized mob of jumbucks. At the township a couple
° f miles on I found the drover at the pub. After
the usual, I remarked that his first lieutenant, back
with the mob, looked as if he could hold one down.
“Yairs, I suppose he could,” he said; “Billy, go
•down and take the sheep and tell Jack to come up
and get a drink.” A big barb crawled out from
under the sulky, cocked an experienced eye at the
dust and heat-waves and trotted off. in a few
minutes Jack came up and had a refresher. They’re
what I reckon a good pair of dogs.

“Pick”; In the cherry seasons of 1917-18
a previously not introduced nark attacked the trees
in Southern Monaro (N.S.W.) orchards and ruined
many crops. The destroyer is a leaf-green leech-
like slug, half an inch long in maturity; it appears
in myriads among the foliage when the fruit begins
to ripen, and devours the leaves until they are but
a delicate network of ribs. The cherry trees polished
off, the pest deals with the pear trees also, and then
disappears. The vegetables, deprived of their natural
breathing apparatus, become sickly, their fruit
hardens and falls off, and the orchardist writes down
his profits to below zero. If any Abo. knows how
this infliction may be successfully strafed let him
have the floor.

“Eddyson”; Nobody at Pinpoint thought
“Hatter” McArthur really dipped till he began to
talk about his dear wife. He had always been con-
sidered eccentric, but his shrewdness, in driving a
bargain and his marked success in handling an
01 chard made it impossible for his neighbors to
believe that he was “fair up the pole.” Then came
the missing wife delusion, and Pinpoint finally made
up its mind “Hatter” was mad. There were those
at Pinpoint who had known him for 18 years without
discovering reason to believe he was anvthing but a
confirmed bachelor, with a furtive fondness for chil-
dren and a pronounced diffidence in the presence of
women, and here he was at 54 talking about his Lily,
and walking up the Quandong road every afternoon
at four to meet her on her delayed but inevitable
return. McArthur described the missing Lily as a
tall woman of unusual beauty, fair as a lily and
with tender eyes of the greyness of ashes. To
Prince he also talked affably of his daughter Jean.
Little Jean was like her mother, he said, but Bennie
took more after his family. “Bennie’s the baby,”
McArthur explained. “He’s just four and a half, a
beautiful child.” Describing little Bennie’s playful
baby ways, he was overcome, and sat on Prince’s
grindstone and cried into his hat. “But I’ll have
to be going now,” he said presently. “They’ll be
coming down the road presently. I’ve heard from
the wife, and she’s coming home.” McArthur walked
up the Quandong road to the top of Dinnie’s Hill,
and waited there for his wife and family. Their
non-appearance did not. distress him greatly. “Some
delay, he said. “They’ll come to-morrow all right,
Lil and the three kids. I don’t suppose you’d see
anywhere in the world three finer children. I’m
proud of them, and I’m proud of my wife.” After
nearly a year of this, during which time “Hatter’s”
tenderness and devotion to the absent wife and
family increased to a kind of rapture, the people of
Pinpoint were filled with amazement on hearing that
McArthur’s wife had come home, and brought two
children with her. Prince strolled through and met
latter at his door. He had a boy of about seven
by the hand. “This is my son James,” he said. “The
dead image of me, don’t you think?” “Hatter” gave
the youngster an affectionate hug. The bov was a
very ordinary, freckled youth, with thin, ginger hair,
he, girl's lovely. She’s her mother’s daughter, my

HYPOCHONDRIACAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
lhe silkworm is liable to 100 distinct diseases.'’
! oiks label me a hypochondriac;
To live my life I wonder how they’d care to !
f rom birth I’ve had the most unhappy knack
r 0f catching any ill that flesh is heir to!
To “feel cronk” is my hobby ; my pet wheeze is
In minor key to swank of my diseases.
I had the notion that of creatures doomed
On this mad, bad old world half-dead to hang on,
I, like a theatre-star, stood, billed and boomed—
Unique, and with a handy drum to bang on !
Rude is the shock—a crawling thing can meet me
On equal terms and at my own game beat me!
An easy mark! The thought I’d cherished long;
A prey to any epidemic going!
And now I And I’m hale and well and strong—
At ills an insect makes a better showing!
Soon worm’s-meat may I be, give up life’s riddle,'
Kor to no worm will I play second fiddle!

Business, Robbery, etc.
About John Brown’s father ;
In 1840 a young Scotchman namtkl Janies Brown
came to N. S. Wales, and, after looking round,
secured a lease of 60 acres of land at Pour Mile
Creek, near Maitland, from Arthur Charles Fitzroy
Dumaresq. At tin's time the A. A. Co. claimed
that it had from the Crown the sole right to mine
and sell coal in the colony, and could legally pre-
vent coal being mined by Brown or anyone else.
P. P. King, for the A. A. Co., laid an information
against Brown, on which the Attorney-General pro-
ceeded before Judge Dickinson and a jury of four.
August 25, 3845, was a great day for Costs. John
Hubert Plunkett (Attorney-General), W. M. Man-
ning and Mr. Broadhurst appeared for the Crown,
while Richard Windeyer (father of the late Judge)
and Robert Lowe appeared for Brown. The Crown
maintained that the grant reserved the coal to the
Crown and it could not be mined without permis-
sion. Windeyer argued that the proceedings were
instituted by the A. A. Co. for its sole benefit and
not for the Crown’s benefit. The verdict went to
the Crown with one shilling damages. Brown turned
to the Pull Court, and the appeal was heard on
June 24, 1846, with the same bar. In February,
1847-s they gave their reserved judgment against
Brown. This, however, did not stop that stubborn
Scot, who kept on mining in defiance of the judgment.
In 1850 a proclamation in the Government Gazette
cancelled the reservation of coal in all grants, and
thus ended the big threatened monopoly. From the
stormy beginning grew the wealthy J. and A. Brown
of to-day and the impressive coal contract that
needed such a lot of explanation.
* * * *
“Copra Bug” : Don’t believe those people who
talk about spontaneous combustion in copra.
How is it that fires in copra have been far
more frequent since the war began than
before? I guess that some hounds are getting
at the stacks with spontaneous matchboxes.
I’ve handled copra for 12 years, and my ex-
perience is backed up by men who have
juggled it for 20 to 30 years ; and never once
have 1 seen or heard of a case of spontane-
ous combustion. True enough, copra breeds
heat, particularly if it is bagged. But only
if it has been bagged wet or insufficiently
cooked or cured. Heat that is born of
dampness will not in itself cause fire, but
merely steam, until all the dampness is gone.
Damp bags alone (especially baled bags) will
do this.
* * * *
If whales persist in advertising their pre-
sence on the Speck’s coast, someone will be
getting after them. The 130 that recently
came ashore at Cloudy Bay by no means
exhausted the supply, for since then big
schools have been blowing off Eaglehawk
Neck and Forestier Peninsula. Long ago
there were a dozen whaling-stations in a
30-mile stretch on this part of the Speck’s
shores, but for many years the sardines have
been very scarce.
★ * * *
This picture shows the newest thing in
harvesting machines —an Australian inven-
tion, of course: it was thought out in
Westralia, and has been tested in N. S.
Wales this season. Its big feature is that
the side draught is abolished, the pull being
straight and direct from the back axle. The
inventors claim that with four horses, and
with a comb making a cut of 14ft., it will
do the work of two Bft. harvesters each
THE NEWEST THING IN HARVESTERS.
drawn by six. horses, and that, travelling 2-£
miles an hour, it can take off acres every
hour. From the beater-drum in front of the
machine the grain is conveyed by archi-
medean screws to the elevator behind the
driver’s seat; thence the system is as in
present harvesters. The steering gear is
independent of the horses, and is worked on
the same principle as on a motor-lorry. The
central idea of the invention was suggested
by F. D. Fogarty, of Westralia, and a local
co. took up the idea and, after making im-
provements suggested by recent tests, is going
to start manufacturing.
“Ivan P.”: Now that Randolph Bedford
has become a legislator he should study the
Merchant Shipping Act and the regulations
made thereunder for preventing collisions at
sea.. In his good yarn, “Predestination,
Faith and Works” (B. 81/1/’18), he writes:
“McAlister saw the masthead-light of the
Crest of the Wave.” Now, a masthead-light
is the distinguishing mark of a steamer;
and it is not to be shown by other craft.
A schooner, which he had told us the Crest
of the Wave was, must carry sidelights only.

“McTrochas”: Mention of Cooktown
sponges (B. 18/10/’l7) recalls the attempt
to introduce the culture of the animal in
Papua during 1903. 11. A. Wickham and
Dr. Allen considered the industry could be
founded in the Conflict Islands, coral atoll
some 70 miles north-east of Samarai. Wick-
ham got the scientist to come from England to
direct the enterprise, but after a few months
the.scheme failed, like so many other under-
takings in tropical development in Papua.
Allen was, nevertheless, convinced of the com-
mercial possibilities of sponge culture, and was
impressed with the varieties of the animal
that he found in the lagoon where he pur-
posed founding his beds. In 1895-6 Cam-
bridge University had Dr. Willey dredging
the waters of Eastern Papua to discover the
spat of the nautilus shell. He lifted a tre-
mendous number of sponges, and he, too, was
impressed with the
commercial possibili-
ties of the industry.
Papua holds a lot of
undeveloped products
on her reefs and cays.
Trochas shell, beche-
de-mer, pearls and
pearlshell and turtle-
shell are combed from
the coral-barriers by
the white and discol-
ored fishers. Such pro-
fitable industries as
sponge 1 gathering,
turtle-canning, fish-
preserving, the pre-
paration of dugong oil
and meat, fish-manures
and oils and the col-
lecting of gem-coral
could be established if
assistance was rendered the ventures by a
subsidy and free entry to Australian markets.

: A little meetin S has been held in
