any movement towards the perfecting of the
local industry and the securing of Protection,
even to the uttermost limit (which is prohi-
bition of foreign goods), counts as patriotic.
But the promoter of this meeting stated that
the new association would “require assist*
ance from the State and Federal Govern-
ments, and that is not good. Bottled scents
are a luxury ; and no Government would be
justified in giving the industry assistance
outside of the tariff, any more than it would
be entitled to devote public money to a busi-
ness of potting peacocks’ tongues or manu-
facturing mock pearls.
* * * *
The unprotected state of the oyster has at
last appealed to the chivalry of Melbourne
Board of Health, and Nicholas Condogianis
is 50s. 6d. the poorer in conseouence. Nicho-
las owns a restaurant in Elizabeth-street.
One day a health inspector dropped in and
ordered a dozen on the half-shell. As he
ate them he watched one of Nicholas’s aides-
de-camp combing his Hellenic locks over
plates of oysters which reposed uncovered
on the bar. The combination of hair-dress-
ing and the cuisine didn’t appeal to the
inspector, and Nicholas, in the District
Court, was fined 405., with costs. It is
probably unsafe even to hint at “the comb-
ing-out process” in Nicholas’s saloon now.
“D.F.”: Some of the country about the
Grampians (Vic.) is literally flowing with
honey just now. It is a great bee-country,
and lately the bees have been doing good
business with the tea-tree blossoms. There’s
a prejudice against the resulting honey, how-
ever, so the apiarists have been letting it out
of the frames to get a clear run for the
eucalyptus syrup.
* * * #
The tape-measure spieler is a war-time
product. Three of him successfully oper-
ated in a Melbourne hotel a few days ago.
Getting into conversation with an up-country
visitor one enthusiastically admired the
cocky’s physique and affirmed that he could
easily pass the enlistment tests. Another
of the push was doubtful and, producing
a tape-measure, proceeded to “run the rule”
over the victim —who, a little later, discov-
ered, of course, that his purse was missing.
A tough old sea-warrior is the barquentine
Ysabel, of M.L. The other day she lifted a
cargo of copra at New Caledonia or there-
abouts for the other side of the Pacific, the
longest voyage she has ever tackled, and
she’s well on in her forties now. Old man
Niccol. of Auckland, built her in the early
’seventies, of kauri, for the Melanesian Mis-
sion. For 30 years she hauled miss’onaries-
and skylarking Kanakas about the Western
Pacific, to and from the cannibal islands,
where now and again one of the reverends
went into the pot. Then Captain William
Ross bought the old waggon and made a pile
of money out of her in the Tonga trade. Now
she flies another house flag and her old feet
have still a turn of speed that may give much
trouble to a'ny of Von Buckner’s successors
in the Pacific pirate industry.
* * * »
Tasmania has shipped a cargo of native
blackwood, myrtle, lancewood, celery-top and
Huon pine to the British Air Board to be
tested in aeroplane construction. Thus far
Bananaland sillcwood is the only Australian
timber at all largely used in making aero-
’buses.
THE WHISTLING POSTMAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In Victoria (and elsewhere), the post-bird is silent.
The N. S. Wales variety, on arriving at the gate or
door, gives a shrill trill like that of the white
scooter or umpire bird. —Nature note.
Oh*,, the magpie’s joyous carol is the music 1 love best
b lien I’m holidaying gaily in the wide and gracious
West;
Then the kookaburra’s cackle, is the thing to make
you sing
When the Crow of Care is roosting and the Oof Bird's
on the wing.
But the note that sends me bounding like a Scotch-
man to a thistle
Is the pretty little post-bird’s whistle.
He is blue with thin red edges, and lie has a silver
bill,
And we love to hear him coming with his sudden
note and shrill,
For he brings us grubs called letters which we’re
mostly glad to get,
And he never, never fails us though the day be wild
and wet.
So I do a haggis canter like a Scotchman to a thistle
When I hear the little post-bird’s whistle.
N.S.W.
LOVER OF SONGBIRDS.
The Inky Way.
With Arthur Mason, the organist, in Lon-
don supplying the sort of thing the Fairfaxes
want, old Henry Lucy has dropped from
the S. M. Herald, after getting prosier
and more snobbish every Saturday for 25
years. In any case, the old man is 73 now.
* * * *
The Woodhouse and Wilson partnership
in the Country Press Agency has been dis-
solved by the death of Arthur Woodhouse,
whose flaxen moustache and dapper turn-
out were very familiar on Melbourne Inky
Way.. He looked amazingly young for his
57 years, 38 of which were spent in jour-
nalism. He began on the old Melbourne
Daily Telegraph, and turned in copy to
many another paper. Besides being a
seasoned Gallery hand, he wrote two books,
“Foundations of National Greatness” and
“The Man with the Annies.” The sixth
son of Frederick Woodhouse the elder, an
animal painter of note, whose brush was
taken up by son Herbert, Arthur was in
harness until the night before he died. He
leaves a young widow.
* * * *
The Melbourne Mechanics’ Institute, alias
the Athenaeum, has awakened, stretched
itself, yawned and turned itself inside out.
The library was the- creation of John Pas-
coe Fawkner, who trod the Inkv Way as
Melbourne’s first journalist. He also
swapped many beads, toothpicks and toma-
hawks for much land around Geelong.
Several worthy journalists have controlled
the Athenaeum, the best-known being Garnet
Walch, pal of Marcus Clarke and Patchett
Martin, the biographer of Viscount Lowe.
Then J. H. B. Curtis, litterateur, reigned
for some years. The present secretary,
R. W. E. Wilmot, is a best-known journal-
ist, too, being sports-editor of the Argus
and Australasian. His alias is “Bung”
because he doesn’t resemble a cork. The
library is not nearly such a nice place to
sleep in as it was before the alterations.
* * * *
Corporal Israel, reader’s assistant on the
Age before deciding to shoot full stops at
Fritz, has been awarded a D.C.M. for mak-
ing a German major and 12 lesser Huns yell
“Kamerad!” With the bunch he brought
back a machine-gun. When last heard from
Israel was returning to Australia with rup-
tured ear-drums.
* * * *
“T. 0.1.”: Apropos R. D. Adams (B.
7/2/T8), Sam Bennett, Henniker Heaton
and “Dictionary of Dates.” We old fogies
who knew all the parties firmly believed
(and those living still believe) that old
“surly” Sam Bennett was the compiler of
the MS. which produced “Men of the Time”
and “Dictionary of Dates,” Heaton being
merely the foster-father. Bennett pub-
lished his “Australian History,” carefully
prepared matter, in the Empire, and later
in book form. It is a very scarce book now,
worth (say) five guineas a copy. “Aus-
tralian Men of the Time” and “Dictionary
of Dates” was the by-product of that his-
tory. Sam Bennett died June, 1S78;
Heaton’s book was published late in the
year 1879.
* * * *
“Dal” : On November 13 last an organiser
of the A.W.U.. while addressing shearers and
shed-hands at a Mitchell (Q.) station,
asserted that one of the main reasons for the
deferred issue of the Sydney Labor daily.
The World, was that certain machine parts
were on board a German ship interned in
Johannesburg. When I suggested that
there seemed to be a geographical error
in this statement, he replied that he
got the information from Labor Dailies.
Ltd. As the novelty ought to have worn off
by this time, the people of Jo'berg might
oblige by sending the curiously-overdue cargo
on to its destination. Meantime, perhaps
J. C. Watson would oblige us bushwhackers
with some information on the point.

A. T. Saunders, Adelaide’s most prolific
press correspondent, won the Mixed Meta-
phor Championship on January 17 with a
spirited letter to the Advertiser. An
extract
But Owen Smyth upset the apple-cart; he couhl
see the hole in the ladder, and he hung on to
Bovkett like a bulldog and closed for £3B. The fat
was in the fire; the machine had slipped a cog.
Still, Saunders gets somewhere, while thou-
sands of stylists fail. If it hadn’t been for
him the chances are there would have been
no inquiry into the Vaughan Government’s
land transactions.
SYDNEY SCRIBES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They’re younger in Melbourne than we are in
Sydney. They have a Literary Society, and flower
in litcle papers like Birth and Fellowship; they
have a Socialist paper, and their I.AV.W. is not in
gaol.
Deeply respectable are we ;
Temptation’s wickedness we flee;
Enthusiasms stir no more;
Our lion’s grown too fine to roar !
We have our circles, sets and cliques,
Unbridgeable though genius speaks:
Indeed, we rather like to hear it knock,
As though less humbleness might give us shock.
We arc good copperplate, where once
Each was life’s well-beloved dunce;
Instead of olden, golden prank,
Mark of his youth and redness of his blood,
Each has a balance at the bank
To show his credit stands at “good’’!
... .Ah, w T ell!
Perhaps there’s judgment yet—and hell!
N.S.W.
M. GILMORE.
“B. Bowyang” : It looks as if Billjim in
Egypt and Palestine will soon have,to do
without his daily war news, for owing to the
rise in the price of paper the two English
rags are threatening to suspend. Paper
used to cost 14s. per ream, but owing to
high freights, insurance charges, etc., you
can’t land it in Egypt to-day for less than
about £3 5s. —an increase of nearly 500 per
cent. «

HOW TO NEUTRALISE DANGEROUS
STOMACH ACIDS.
. Few people besides physicians realise the
importance of keeping the food contents of
the stomach free from acid fermentation.
Healthy normal digestion cannot take place
while the delicate lining of the stomach is
being inflamed and distended by acid and
wind —the results of fermenting food in the
stomach. . To secure perfect digestion this
fermentation must be stopped and the acid
neutralised. For this purpose physicians
now rarely prescribe drugs or artificial diges-
tants: they rely instead upon a harmless
antacid and food corrective known as bisu-
rated magnesia, and obtainable of all high-
class chemists in the form of small com-
pressed tablets. Two of these tablets taken
after a meal are sufficient to quickly neu-
tralise all harmful acid, and in consequence
they prevent fermentation and wind, heart-
burn and flatulence, and all of the many dis-
agreeable symptoms which indicate indiges-
tion or dyspepsia. But only the tablets pre-
pared from bisurated magnesia will accom-
plish these happy results; and therefore
should you wish to try this remedy you
must ask your chemist very distinctly for
bisurated magnesia tablets. These are put
up in handy little flasks which you can
easily carry in your pocket, and they are
sold at a price which places them within the
easy reach of every sufferer.— Advt,

AND NO TARIFF WALL TO STOP

“Despite many protestations to the contrary there
are on every hand indications of colossal preparation
for the commercial struggle which will follow closely
on the heels of peace.”— W. M. Hughes.
Peace: “You might at least give me time to
get my heel out of the road. "
The Commission’s exposure of the appal-
ling misconduct of the Defence Department
was suppressed for nearly four months.
Thus presumably the Governor was in the
dark (though he shouldn’t have been) when
he recalled the responsible Ministry. And
the party was in the dark when it considered
what ought to happen to Hughes and his
colleagues. And Parliament was in the dark
when it discussed Tudor’s no-confidence
motion. And the party machine was in the
dark when it debated whether Hughes was
fit to. be its leader. Altogether it was a
most inconvenient report, and the Ministers
whom it scarified stuffed it into a
pigeon-hole until the game was over. In
a sporting club any member who introduces
loaded dice is called a spieler and kicked out.
In politics it apparently doesn’t matter.
If John Forrest was caught with a title,
what bait was used for Willie Watt?
The Governor-General ought to be asked a
few questions in connection with the scath-
ing report of the Commission on the Defence
Department handed in four months ago and
published only last week. That report,
which represents the administration of the
most important department as just about as
bad as it could be, was furnished before the
Referendum was taken, and therefore before
the resignation of the Ministry. As a reader
of the newspapers, if not otherwise, the G.-G.
must have known that a report had been pre-
sented. Did lie ask for it? If not, why
didn’t he, since it was the only guide he
could have as to the quality of the adminis-
tration in the Commonwealth’s most impor-
tant department? If he did see it, how could
he say that it was his duty to retain such an
administration and prevent an appeal to the
people? And if he didn’t see it, why did ho
neglect the one means he had of informing
hiujself? There is nobody in Australia to ask
him these questions. But beyond all doubt
the Secretary for tiie Colonies should do it.
His failure in his office at the one critical
moment he has met was as complete as that
of Pearce himself.
If the.N. S. Wales Arbitration Amend-
ment Bill gets through in its present
form the proprietor of a newspaper
which “advises, instigates, aids or abets
or _ publishes any report, advertisement,
article or paragraph encouraging” an illegal
strike will, be liable to a fine of £5OO,
If any common person by use of his mouth
aids or abets the same thing, the penalty for
him will be £2O or three months’ gaol at the
discretion of the beak. Clearly if there is to
be a fair deal all round the beak should have
the option of giving Mr. James, of the S. M.
Herald, 75 months in the cooler.
A woman’s work is never done. For the
matter of that, a politician never does his
either.
A telegram from Geneva was an impressive
item of last week’s war news. It affirmed
that th- burgomasters of the chief Rhine
towns had visited Berlin and urged the Gov-
eminent to come to an
arrangement with the
Allied Governments
before any “air re-
prisals” had come to
pass. “But the reply
was unsatisfactory
and the apprehensive
burgomasters returned
home.” Acceptingthe
telegram from Geneva
as a fair statement of
fact, the necessity for
air reprisals and
plenty of ’em is
strikingly apparent. If
the thought of what
may happen scares the
Rhine towns into
pleading for a mutual-
safety arrangement
with the enemy, the
actual happenings will
probably put them
out of conceit with
German military con-
quest. Military spirit
is in the homes of
the Huns because the
war has never been
carried over their
homes and dropped
thereon. How long
must we continue to
hear from' military and
naval authorities that
air raiding is confined
to cei'tain “important”
p o i n t s o r w o r k s,
selected by themselves?
All enemy places are important as marks for
psychological effect.
From the Federal Gallery:—
What were the “great services rendered to the
Empire” which induced the Phewsion to recommend
Bigjohn for a peerage? They ought to go on record.
When the Hughes-Pearce rump was hanging on as
a Federal Ministry by the grace of the Cookites,
proposals for a coalition were received by the
Liberals with cold disfavor. They liked neither
Hughes nor Pearce. “Let us keep them in till the
election. Then they will fall in a heap, for they
have no followers in the country,” was the view
generally expressed. It was Bigjohn who induced
the party to swallow the bolus. His argument was
this: “We can get in now and by doing so prevent
this Hughes-Pearce push from employing the
machinery of government to secure a following. If
we miss the chance it may not come again, for
three parties may spring up, and that would be plain
hell for us. The time is ripe.” Bigjohn had his
way, and the most immoral political deal in
the history of the Commonwealth was fixed
up. That was service to the Empire No.
1. When the Bendigo pledge had to be faced,
Bigjohn held the view that the time was ripe for
Hughes to commit hari-kari, and said so, leaving the
Prime Ministership open to a large man with a
red face - Most of the P art y thought so, too, and
reckoned John was the man to go up top. But Cook
MINED.
PRESIDENT WILSON: “Say, John, I guess we’d better keep to the warpath a lit longer.
This road ain't safe yet”
and Millen could not agree—and just when John
was beginning to think about smashing things, some-
one remembered his weakest spot, his vanity.
ould he be satisfied to be a Baron or something?
He would. Service to the Empire (and Wales)
No. 2.
There was another Ministerial crisis in
Sydney last week, following on the discovery
that certain safeguards, specified by Cabinet,
had been left out of a big contract let to
Teesdale Smith for building the terminal
station under the scheme for the bulk-
handling of wheat. The omissions having
been detected, the old contract was torn up ;
but it will take half a ton of iodoform to dis-
infect the premises.
Intrigue disguised as Socialism is now
endeavoring to break down Switzerland’s
home-defence system, in order that Germany
may have the run of the country for military
purposes. Calling themselves Bolsheviks,
the agitators demand the demobilisation of
the army, leaving only troops sufficient to
suppress contraband trade on the frontiers.
Doubtless a large number of dreamy Social-
ists will be carried away by the tide of agita-
tion, and Swiss trades-unions will sooner or
later go on strike, and the little country will
be ready for the executioner. Socialism is
the cat required to draw the chestnuts from
the fire for the Hunnish ape. It is Intrigue’s
pet tabby, even as Militarism is Autocracy’s
caged lion.
Prom the directors’ half-yearly report of
the Broken Hill South Mining Co.: —
Although the mine was laid idle for 11 weeks the
usual dividends have been paid, which have absorbed
£120,000. The liquid assets amount to £700,245.
After careful consideration our staff are of opinion
that it is doubtful whether the co. will be called
upon to pay anything under the War Time Profits
Tax.
As at June, 1916, the net liquid assets were
£475,124. So, notwithstanding strikes, this
co., besides paying its “usual dividend”—in
fact, it has been far more than the “usual
dividend”——has put by over £225,000 in 18
months. The Hughes Government created a
monopoly in the copper, lead, silver and zinc
trade. Now the news comes out that the cos.
will pay no war-time taxation, though they
have got richer out of the war than any other
group of capitalists in Australia. This
“Nationalist” Government has simply tossed
the public into the pig-trough to fatten the
al ready-fattest hogs in the sty.
During the discussion on the Arbitration
Bill in N. S. Wales Assembly Simon Hickey,
Labor member for Alexandria, put up a plea
for short-term apprenticeships. He pointed
out, rightly, that owing to present-day
machinery, specialisation and subdivision,
there was often barely a skilled trade left
as a residuum. To bind bright, technically-
trained boys for the usual five years to such
callings is tantamount to making the boss
a donation of two or three years’ work n t
about half wages. His leader," Storey, should
have seen a little thing like that; instead,
he burbled about the superiority of Austra-
lian mechanics, and the national necessity of
keeping them so by long-term indentures.
This is the effect of the Party crime on
the daily papers. Almost without exception
they profess to be horrified at the Defence
Department exposures. But, as a matter of
fact, they knew all about the bungling waste
two years ago, and (also almost without ex-
ception) they assisted Pearce and Hughes
back so that those two bright business
geniuses might do it some more.
Bowser proposes to give police magis-
trates power to deal with minor indictable
offences where the accused pleads guilty or
consents to jurisdiction, and to compel
accused persons who plead guilty in the
lower court to stick to their pleas when they
come before a judge and jury, unless it can
be shown that the pleas were made under an
honest misapprehension; to increase the
jurisdiction of County Court judges; to give
Courts of Petty Sessions jurisdiction in
actions for damages up to £5O (now they
can deal with cases up to that amount only
when they arise out of assault, cattle tres-
pass, offensive movements conducted by
hounds, and a few similar happenings) ; to
abolish the Full Court as an appeal tri-
bunal and permit appeals from the County
or Insolvency Courts to be made to a single
Supreme Court judge; and to enlarge the
jurisdiction of Courts of General Sessions.
If these last proposals are carried out a
reduction in the number of Supreme Court
judges should follow. At present various
crimes, such as murder, unnatural offences,
bigamy, arson and sexual offences, must be
heard by one of these expensive wigs, though
in most cases matters of fact and not of
law are involved. There is bound to be
ri against the proposal to abolish the
h ull Court as a court of appeal; but there
are too many Courts of Appeal. We have
the High Court, and that’s enough. The
multiplication of legal billets threatens to
be almost as scandalous as the multiplica-
tion of politicians.
THE POLITICIAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A curious thing is this we have evolved
From out our dearest hope and direst need,
This patriot from love and faith absolved,
A thing irrelevant, an alien breed
That wrecks our State to better our condition—
The politician 1
Among the stirring herd is he alone
Of duty free, untrammelled by a vow,
Nor called to earn his loaf and mutton-bone
By sweat of that bold, hemispheric brow:
Yet he avows a saviour’s godlike mission,
The politician.
The world, in throes of fury spun about
And tearing her own breast with wolfish fangs,
Needs men to cast her hell-born devils out;
Comes frothing with inveterate harangues,
To save us with his pothouse erudition,
The politician.
He beats the air with his poor paws and screams
His platitudes, while heroes cope with doom ;
lie sees the fierce effulgent sky—it seems
Celestial limelight flaming to illume
'1 he superman in his supreme position,
The politician.
The quiet people face their common need
IVith stern austerity, at grips with fate;
With high pretence he cloaks his petty greed,
And endless boons he gathers from the State,
And all the fripperies of low ambition,
The politician.
W ith cattish spleen for preference he claws,
Though skies are split and all God’s lightning
spills ;
In party battles champs his iron jaws,
While nations break and chaos shakes the hills.
Most bold in power, most craven in submission,
The politician.
He crowns himself with fruits of others’ zeal,
The soldier’s glory and the thinker’s fame,
Cries all the elixirs that nothing heal.
And you and I impose upon the lame,
Sick body politic its quack physician,
The politician !
VICTORIA.
EDWARD DYSON.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
VICTORY

MICK SIMMONS
GLOVES
The Oakleigh Plate day’s racing at Caul-
field has rarely been so interesting as it was last
Saturday. Blue Victor, lately from Tasmania, won
the Hurdle Race in smart style, and then the two
divisions of the Federal Stakes, for two-year-olds, fell
respectively to Palm Leaf, the grey filly from Sydney,
and Tragic Star, whose brilliant track form was; at
last put beyond all question. After which old Defence,
the son of Carbine, won the Bond Cup (12 furlongs)
by fairly 7 outstaying the opposition. All going well
with him, Defence will certainly make a good show in
the Australian Cup, despite a 71b. penalty. The
ancient gelding is at least a sticker.
Poitrina monopolised front place in the Oakieigh
sprint. The Welkin mare was there from start to
finish, and her performance was a handsome apology
for her failure last year, when Woorak was the only 7
one in it. Poitrina was the third first favorite to
win. Desert Gold was the fourth to do the trick, and
she also was ahead all the way. It had seemed more
than likely 7 that the great mare, not long arrived
from Maoriland via Sydney, might be unacclimatised
or short of condition. But her time for the nine
furlongs was a Caulfield record, and her appearance
after tire race indicated that she will do some more
things of the sort before leaving Melbourne. The
respectable effort that Cetigne made at the distance
went to confirm the general idea that Desert Gold
had shown her best form, or something very near it.
By a strange coincidence the victory of the M.L.
champion was followed by Kilfiinn’s reassertion of
her ability, Kiiflinn being credited with an unexpected
defeat of Desert Gold in their own country. She won
the concluding welter race pretty easily for the Rajah,
giving punters another reminder that now and the next
two months are the best time o’ year for mares and
fillies. Imported Elsdon started favorite for this event,
but he got badly away and never pushed near the front.
Now that she has proved herself in Melbourne the
star performer from M.L. will have a mob of fol-
lowers for the Futurity Stakes next Saturday. Her
weight (9st. 131 b.) is not prohibitive. Gladsome
carried 9st. 71b. and 9st. 131 b. on her two wirming
occasions, and Antonio and Soultline both had 9st.
81b. up. But this year there are some reputedly fast
things among the three-year-olds whose allowance
brings them down to something under 7st. Flash of
Steel, three years ago, was not a brilliant youngster,
but he was good enough to win. Yoorala and Colonel
Chutney will receive more than three stone from
Desert Gold, and either of them must beat the top
weight in order to have a good chance on paper, or
in fact, for the Newmarket flutter. The writer pre-
fers Yoorala, by Lingle out of Amata’s dam. The
filly did a great track gallop last week, on the
strength of which young Dempsey engaged to ride her
for the Futurity and Newmarket. As for Colonel
Chutney, the colt’s very outside price in the Oakleigh
Plate quotations was not consistent with his having
been one of the late entries for the Futurity.
* * * *
> Perhaps worth while remembering that
twice during the last four years the Australian Cup
has been won by horses that preceded their victories
by successes in jumpers’ flat races. Such considera-
tion will weigh in favor of Dalcross (Wallace —-Cross-
keys), who won in taking style the J.F. mile-and-a-
quarter event at Sandown Park (Vic.) the other day
with 9st. 61b. up. The gelding’s weight in the Aus-
tralian Cup is 6st. 71b.
*
Trainers from N. S. Wales in charge of
horses in work at Flemington for the V.R.C.
Autumn meeting were much disturbed by the playing
of military bands at the adjacent training-camp. So
they presented an ultimatum to someone that unless
the bands were stopped i they and the mokes would
leave for Caulfield. Whereupon the music was turned
off at the meter and the soldiers went about on
bare feet. This absurd old war can’t have everything
its own way, thank Heaven!
# * * *
Imported horses are charged with incon-
sistency, but some of them are brilliant exceptions.
The infirm Lavendo rarely ran a bad race when he
was fairly fit. Polvcrates was an honest battler, for
he beat Panacre and Colugo before he split them in
the Epsom last year, after which he ran second in the
Metropolitan. A few months later the handsome
brute won the Newmarket and ran second for the
Doncaster. Whitefield is another that has always
finished in the first half-dozen when expected to make
a show, although he certainly did get left at the
post in the Flying Stakes at the A.J.C. meeting last
April. The trouble with the imported nags is that
they are hard to train, as a rule, and apt to go off
very quickly when asked to do as much racing as an
ordinary Australian horse. But we are bound tq have
them in the boom again presently.
* * * *
Whitefield’s pretensions as a Newmarket
“fancy” are based on the theory that the bisr Erw-lish
horse is acclimatised, and therefore much improved,
since he ran fourth in the Newmarket.and third in
the Doncaster last year. Both at Flemington and
Randwiclc he has u r on a good race in commanding
style. Moreover, he is big and strong enough to carry
his weight—9st. 4lb. He had 9st. up when he made
his bold show in the mile race at Rand wick, so there
is no question of his ability to stay the course. Besides,
he is one that runs best when there isn’t a crowd to
shut out daylight ahead of him, which condition is
obtainable in the broad straight run for the New-
market. Apparently he is coming on with every
gallop, and should be forward enough by Saturday
week. The big chap is a promising place investment,
anyhow.
A colt that cost 1500 guineas last year was
a runner among- the juveniles at Warwick Farm
(Sydney) on Saturday. There were 24 of them,
including those which should have been at home,
and the 1500-guinea lot never had his tail away
from the wrong end of the procession. But that
doesn’t say he will not do better later on.
* * * *
Three years ago Scotch Artillery hopped
out in front of a Sydney Cup field, and, mainly
because the others didn’t care for the mud, stayed
there. It was one of the great flukes occasionally
heard of, and the more frequently the nag ran after-
wards the more impressive the fluke became, lor, with
the exception of a win up Newcastle way, he never
did anything worth noticing—till Saturday in the
Club Handicap at Warwick Farm. There was a
field of 11, and, starting at 12’s, the frequent disap-
pointment was soon in the lead and was there when
he struck the post, with Roman Queen and Medley
struggling to get their heads up, If the winnei
waits as iong before coming to light again he won’t
lie helping anyone to take a million in the war
loan.

“Alec” Nicholson, Adelaide raceliorse-
owner and sportsman in general, had a sportsman’s
fitting end. He was at Tattersall s Club on Saturday

quietly on the Sunday. In private life he was chiefly

Tasmanians had been expecting the Traquir
gelding Le Noir to put up a record ever since
Tom Lvons paid £l2OO for him a couple of years
ago. He did it in the Hobart Cup. Bumping
Aristaeus he somersaulted, broke his neck, and passed
without a kick. When Camelot broke his neck at the
last fence in a steeple at Adelaide in 1908 he
behaved better. He was remounted and completed
the course before turning into meat.

“Globe” : I was a sleeping partner in a nag
which was just beaten in an up-country race and I
always reckoned it hard luck —till after a yarn with
the owner of the winner the other day. He didn’t
know I had a share in the beaten prad. “Yes,” he
reminisced, “I remember the race well—had a good
win. But, Lord, on a dinkum go mine had no chance.
Strongarm, who was riding the other one, took three
fivers from me, and putting that with what he had
been given to dump on his own mount got away with
a hatful. He managed his part of it real well.” It
was like listening to a man explaining how he had
helped himself to my purse while I was in swimming.
By winning the Middle Park Plate at
Ch’church (M.L.) the other day with Almoner, who

man had a habit of doing. Between ’B5 and ’O6
G. G. Stead’s nomination for this race won 13 times.

* * * *
“Spreader”; What constitutes a champion
racehorse, anyhow ? I am open to put my Sunday
shirt on either Jack Rice or Greek Fire to beat
Desert Gold or Biplane over a mile provided I am
also allowed to nominate the respective weights, the
state of the weather and the track slung in. Carbine
put up a two-mile record with lOst. 51b. Machine
Gun carried a stone better and hoisted new figures
for five furlongs. These two performances proved
that the stamina was there as well as pace.. Now
without stamina racehorse breeding is foolishness.
With welter weights and nothing shorter than middle-
distance races for four-year-old horses (and over)
the selection of the fittest would be carried out on
far better lines than is at present the case.
* * * *
“Cherokee”: It is proposed in Victoria
to take, for the use of repatriated soldiers, the land
comprised in certain of the country racecourse
reserves. The sooner the better, in view of what
happened at the Drysdale course, when Lieut.
Maxwell, recruiting officer for the Corio electorate,
set out from Geelong in the forlorn hope of.finding
something that might fill a khaki suit. He and his
mate, who had already spent £1 in travelling to
the course, were refused admission unless they paid
for it. They were then told that if they attempted
to address those present the police would be in-
structed to remove them. At last Maxwell got Rave
to deliver a 10-minutes’ speech, throughout which he
was heckled by a large and mostly-eligible crowd.
And it is for lice like this that men are dying in
Palestine!
At Goulburn (N.S.W.) the weevils broke
out of the Government wheat stacks and swarmed
over the racecourse, and bit the punters, and nearly
ate up the “sport of kings.” “Eaten by weevils”
may yet be inscribed on the tomb of another
Carbine.
* * * *
“A Trooper Billjim”: The toast is, I
believe, the Australian and his Horse. Very w 7 ell.
The distances covered by our mounted troops (Aus-
tralian and M.L.) in their recent stunt in Palestine
constitute, I should think, for this war a bit of a
record. We rode from our starting point at
Marakeb (on the beach near Rafa) to Jaffa, via
Asluj and Beersheba, a distance by direct road
measurement of 160 miles; time 2-0 days. Add
another 75 to 100 miles at least for the innumer-
able scoots to and fro, and a rough idea may be
had of the actual distance covered. We left Marakeb
on October 26, and reached Asluj (40 miles) on the
night of the 28th, spelling 24 hours on the way.
On the night of the 30th we moved out against
Beersheba (80 miles), and the following night w 7 ere
in possession of the town. Two days later we went
15 miles up the Hebron-road, returning to Beersheba
on November s—a further 30 miles. We saw the
last of Beersheba on the evening of the 6th, and
moved off N.W. Midnight of the 10th found us on
the beach above Medjel, close to old Askalon, 30
miles from Beersheba and 30 miles up the coast from
our starting point at Marakeb, we having in the
interim encircled the entire Turkish position. Again
we moved on up the coast on the 13th, and at 11 a.m.
on November 16 entered Jaffa (32 miles). During
the whole of this period we were more or less
engaged with the enemy. So you get some idea of
the strenuousness of the proceedings. From the
outset our iellows Tarely had their gear off their
backs. When not engaged with the fleeing Turk we
were seeking for horse-feed and water. Times were
when the prads had no drink for periods up to 60
hours; the difficulties of obtaining it were quite
beyond any of our previous experiences. Most of the
village animals lay dead of thirst round the wells,
and the native population were in a bad way, too.
Between the Turks and ourselves they got a pretty
rough spin. The sights at some of the waterholes
beggar description. Scores of soldiers, natives (men,
women and children), horses in hundreds and dying
animals, all waiting for water which was being
raised a gallon a time in a canvas bucket. Gentle-
men, the Australian, Man and Horse 1
* * * *
There was loud laughter at daybreak when
rs ornan Ml Swamp, near Forbes
(N.S.W.), saw a girl, armed absurdly with a pea-
rifle, wander down to the water. The marksmen
with their doubie-barellcd lead-sprays thought the
girl with the little rifle the silliest sight they had
seen; but when the first big black duck tried to
whir past there \vas a light crack and a dead bird
tumbled from mid-air. The second shot missed ; but
the third, fourth, fifth, sixth and seventh stopped a
flying duck apiece, while the eighth pellet drew
blank. The lady gathered up her six birds, tucked
her rifle under her arm and went home to breakfast
surrounded by respectful silence. She is Miss Reid,
daughter oi a farmer at Oumbijowa, near Forbes,
and scorns to shoot a bird sitting.
* * * *
“Brigalow” : It might have been added by
“Mat” (B. 14/2/’I8), when making remarks about
the 28 alleged “sports” who bagged 1137 ducks near
Lockhart at the opening of the season for slaughter
that, as a general thing, more than half the birds
killed in this outrageous, wholesale fashion are wasted.
Some years ago about 1800 wild ducks were butchered
on the Doodle Gooma Swamp in one day, and a very
large percentage of them were left to rot or were
afterwards thrown away in a state of decomposition.
It is quite common, too, for the slaughter of ducks to
begin before the termination of the close-season. In
fact, shooting goes on practically all the year round in
some parts of the State. The writer has known of
ducks not more than eight weeks old being served up
at station homesteads for dinner long before Christmas.
In some country districts the bird and game protec-
tion law is a jest. If anyone is caught in the act it is
usually regarded as “rotten luck.” And it won’t be
anything else until the law is altered so that the
guilty person is forbidden to use firearms for one
year or three or seven, as the case may be —imprison-
ment to be the punishment for that offence. These
fellows must be branded somehow 7.
“Tupara”: “Centurion” (B. 24/1/T8)
forgot one magazine-rifle of the pre-1870 era.
Besides the original Winchester repeater, which he
mentioned, there was the Spencer. In 1869 or
thereabouts my father brought to Maoriland a fire-
arm which he reckoned w'ould be a daisy of a gun
for the defence of his home on the King Country
frontier. It was a Spencer carbine, I think eight-
shot, the latest thing out. The magazine was
in the butt; it was filled through the heel of the
butt, which was closed with a flange, and a feed-
spring contraption sent each cartridge in turn up
into the breach. The only time it ever saw “active
service” was on the butts at Te Awamutu. An old
Imperial soldier picked it up to examine its mechan-
ism, and accidentally put a bullet into a trooper.
No harm done, beyond making the recipient of the
lead do dismounted work for some weeks. But
there was a court-martial, and someone was repri-
manded or given a medal or something.
“Ranger” : While“G.L.H.”(B. 7/2/TBicon-
fines himself to his experience of wallaby-shooters
as mound shots, I listen to him. But I’ll swear that
experience doesn’t teach him that the abolition of
“sighters” and of marking between shots would be a
step towards greater efficiency. Under that theory
naval range-finding and aeroplane spotting during a
bombardment are similar to the farce that he alleges
the present system of marking to be. At long ranges,
shooters cannot manage without assistance—that is,
they must have help in determining just where then-
shots are landing. Point-blank shooting is a different
thing altogether. I w 7 ant to hammer in the fact that
rifle-clubs and mound-shooting are assets that it is
criminal for Australia to go short of. I speak from
positive experience when I say that a live rifle-club in
a district means that practically all the men in that
district learn to shoot, a large percentage of them
expertly.

“Adelaide”: Hardly correet to say that
J. N. Crawford “stayed here” on coming first to
Australia with A. 0. Jones’s XI (B. 14/2/’18). He
went back to England and to his position in the
Surrey team; but later had an argument with the
County executive over captaincy and team-selection,
which somehow grew into a bitter personal squabble.
Just then St. Peter’s College, Adelaide, cabled him
that it had a vacancy on its staff, and he came back
to Australia as a schoolmaster, helping S. Aus. (as a
side-line) to win the Sheffield Shield, which it had
not seen for years.
* * * *
A small regatta on the Torrens, in the
middle of Adelaide, was enlivened by the gradual
sinking of a pontoon used by the officials and as a
diving-platform. There were boats all about, and
the last person to leave stepped off at the final
moment, without wetting his feet. It seemed a
humorous incident to everybody but the local Maxi,,
which preferred to regard it as averted tragedy.
* * * »
“Smokehouse”: This for the close-shave
competition. I was out after whitefish off Erub, a
Torres Strait island some 120 miles from Thirsty, in
a small sailing-dinghy. I had one line out and made
fast and was standing up to heave the second when
the first one tautened suddenly. The dinghy lurched
over and I fell into the Pacific. As I was struggling
back aboard, a 15ft. shark made a pass at my right
foot and got away with the sole of my sandshoe. The
upper was still hanging round my ankle when I
collected myself from the bottom of the dinghv and
made an inventory of the spoil—one sft. barracoota
and two badly-barked shins.

War-time Nervousness.
In these war times many people are liv-
ing in an unnatural condition of nervous
excitement. Unless something is done to
correct it the inevitable result will be a
nervous breakdown, an exhaustion of ner-
vous energy that may take a very serious
and unforeseen turn.
Neurasthenia is one such result, with its
accompaniment of worry, irritation, over-
sensitiveness and severe headache. It means
that the patient has lost the power to re-
cover and store up energy. The condition
will grow worse if not corrected.
The treatment is one of nutrition of the
nerve cells, requiring a non-alcoholic tonic.
As the nerves get their nourishment from
the blood the treatment must be directed
towards building up the blood. Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills act directly on the blood,
and with proper regulation of the diet have
proved of the greatest benefit in many cases
of neurasthenia. A tendency to anaunia,
or bloodlessness, shown by most neuras-
thenic patients, is also corrected by these
tonic pills.
Two useful books, “Diseases of the Ner-
vous System” and “What to Eat and How
to will be sent free by the Dr. Wil-
liams’ Medicine Co., Box 594 G.P.0., Sydney.
Your own chemist or storekeeper sells Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills at 2/9 per box; six
boxes 15/3. — Advt.

GENERAL MANAGER, CENTRAL
SUGAR MILLS.
STATE OF QUEENSLAND.
A PI’LICATIONS are invited for the
position of General Manager of the
Government, Central Sugar Mills of Queens-
land.
Applicants must state their age, and fur-
nish satisfactory evidence of their technical
and administrative experience and general
qualifications.
A salary of £l2OO per annum is offered,
with an engagement extending over a period
of one year from Ist April, 1918, with an
extension of a further four years if services
are satisfactory.
The successful applicant will be respon-
sible for the good and efficient management
of the Government Central Mills now estab-
lished or to be established, and will super-
vise for the Treasurer of Queensland five
other Central Mills which have also been
established out of State funds.
Applications will be received up to 7th
March, 1918, by the Under-Secretary, the
Treasury, Brisbane, and must be endorsed
“Application for Position of General Man-
ager of Central Sugar Mills.”
G. L. BEAL, Under-Secretary.
The Treasury, Brisbane,
30th January, 1918.

As usual after the September balance the
available profit is put by for the annual dis-
tribution in may. Nothing; much is to be
gained by the half-yearly balance; but some-
thing is gained by a half-yearly meeting
This is a movable affair; once a year there
is a roll-up-on the North Coast—at Lismore
or Grafton—and once on the South Coast,
generally at Bega. This time, however, the
meeting was at Nowra, where the baby was
horn 1< years ago. After talcing a store in
Sussex-street the infant was three weeks
before it received a butter consignment • the
turnover last year was £2,348,000. A grain
section has been added ; and now a fruit and
vegetable section. Of the latter the manager
reports:—•
We have had a very uphill fight, but the corner
has been turned. In the last three months the turn-
over has been upwards of £30,000 and the business
is now an a profit-earning 1 basis.
As bit by bit has been added to the business
bit by bit has been added to the premises ;
and premises secured like that are not gener-
ally very satisfactory. Moreover, as expan-
sion was blocked ; so the directors, reckoning
that co-operation was now well established
took a 50 years’ lease of about half an acre
of land between the Central Railway Station
and Darling Harbor. This is a move of some
consequence. Sussex-street has had a
monopoly of the produce trade. It was time
it was broken up. The people’s food ought to
be handled in the sweetest smelling and most
sanitary part of the city. Sussex-street is
in that connection most things that it
shouldn’t be. Presumably to supply funds or
part of the funds to build the big, open, two-
storey warehouse the co. has secured author-

t his little milling co. goes along at a steady
pace, even if the track is a bit bumpy. For
years profits have averaged
£•>800 p.a., and co-operators liave received in
divs. exactly 14 times the original capital
fhoy put into the concern. And though,
since 1910, when capital was doubled by the
tiansfer of £15.000 of reserves, the surplus
of liquid assets over liabilities has decreased
by about £4400. £9400 more has been put
into premises and plant. Owing to the Gov-
ernment restriction on stocks, the cash
position at present is particularly strong •
against liabilities of £1484 there are liquid
and semi-liquid assets totalling £22,310.
1 lie co. is a fine little example of the value
of co-operation to the primary producer;
and the retention of the milling'in the coun-
tiy. where the “offal” is badly wanted for
return to the soil, is a national service, too.
The small Launceston co. which for some:
ime has been prospecting an ochre deposit
tor paint manufacture at Beaconsfield fTas.)
is almost ready to introduce its product to
the market. A large body of pigment eon-;
raining all the primary colors has been
opened up, as well as a body of pipeclay.
lie co. is installing the necessary plant,
and reckons it can supply all the paint the
Commonwealth requires, and of a quality
equal to the best imported.

BOILER REPAIRS and OXY-ACETYLENE
WELDING REPAIRS
We desire to announce that we have started a Boiler Repair Department in con-
junction with our Oxy-Acetylene Welding Business, and are now in a position
to undertake all classes of BOILER REPAIRS whatsoever. We also buy and
sell second-hand boilers, and undertake inspections on behalf of intending
buyers. We carry out A«jL CLASSES OF OXY-ACETYLENE WELDING
REPAIRS, so do not discard ANY BROKEN MACHINERY, WHETHER
BRASS, COPPER, CAST-IRON, CAST-STEEL, MILD-STEEL, IRON OR
ALUMINIUM, as we can repair the broken parts for a small cost compared
with the cost of buying new parts, and the repairs will then be absolutely satis-
factory. NOTHING TOO SMALL OR TOO LARGE. Portable plants and
experienced workmen sent anywhere in Australia.

SIXTH WAR LOAN (1927)
SUBSCRIPTION LIST CLOSES 10TH APRIL. 1918.
The Loan is for WAR purposes only.
CHARLES OCTAVIUS TATLOCIv, of
Sydney, last heard of in 1892, is re-
quested to communicate with ASHLEY,
TEE & SONS, Solicitors, London. England.
If dead, information as to his children will
be welcomed.
4 y 2 per Cent.
Interest on the 4A per cent. Loan will be free
of Commonwealth and State Income Tax,
and will be exempt from the operation of Wealth
Levy, if any.
On applications under the instalment system
interest amounting to £3 12s 6d on each £lOO
subscribed will be paid on 15th December, 1918.
If deposit and all instalments are paid in full on
or before 10th April. 1918, interest amounting to
£3 15s on each £lOO subscribed will be paid
on 15th December, 1918. Thereafter interest will
be paid halfyearly at the rate of 4$ per cent per
annum.
5 per Cent.
Interest on the 5 per cent. Loan will be
subject to Commonwealth Taxation, but will
be Free from State Income Tax.
On applications under the instalment system
interest amounting to £3 17s 6d on each £lOO
subscribed will be paid on 15th December 1918.
If deposit and all instalments are paid In full on
or before 10th April, 1918, interest amounting to
£4 on each £lOO subscribed will be paid
on 15th December, 1918. Thereafter interest will
be paid half-yearly at the rate of 5 per cent, per
annum.
80N0S will be issued in amounts of £lO, £5O, £lOO, £5OO and £lOOO, payable to bearer,
STOCK will be inscribed in amounts of £lOO and multiples of £lO above £lOO, and transfers will
be FREE of all STAMP DUTY.
80NDS and INSCRIBED STOCK will be accepted at par in payment of Probate and Succession
Duty due to the Commonwealth. INTEREST is payahle Half-yearly on 15th June and 15th
December free of exchange at any Bank or State Savings Bank.
THE COMMONWEALTH BANK OF AUSTRALIA is authorised on behalf of the TREASURER
OF THE COMMONWEALTH OF AUSTRALIA to receive applications for the above loan.
INSTALMENTS Payable as under:—
£lO per cent, on application £25 per cent. Ist July, 1918
£l5 „ „ Ist May, 1918 £25 „ „ Ist August, 1918
£25 „ ~ 3rd June, 1918
Any or ail instalments may be prepaid.
* CONVERSION PRIVILEGES. Holders of Stock or Bonds in the above Issues will have the
right to convert them into the 5 per cent Stock or Bonds of the present Issue (the Sixth War Loan)
provided that the amount so converted in any case shall not exceed the amount of cash subscribed to
the present Issue. Such conversion will be effected as on 15th June, 1918, at face value. free~of charge,
and will be contingent on the relative cash application being duly completed.
The holders of new 5 per cent. Bonds or Stock issued in exchange for 4i per cent. Bonds or Stock
will, on 15th December, 1918, receive £2 10s. interest on each £lOO and will receive the same amount
on every subsequent half-yearly interest date
WAR BAVINGS CERTIFICATES will be accepted at purchase price plus interest at 4£ per cent, to
10th April, 1918. in payment of Stock or Bonds in this Loan up to the full amount of 4J per cent,
loan applied for, or up to 50 per cent of 5 per cent, loan applied for.
APPLICATIONS accompanied by deposit of £lO percent., will be received at any Branch of the
Commonwealth Bank of Australia or at any Bank, State Savings Bank, Money Order Post Office, or
through any member of a recognised Stock Exchange. Instalments payable similarly.
Prospectus, Application forms, and all particulars at any Bank, State Savings
Bank or Money Order Foil Office.

ANSWERS TO CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character ) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on the MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.

For Our Kino and Nation.
Now that our King has continued sitting of Parlia-
ment,
May the Civilised Nations be against unscrupulous
aggression ;
So the World will know that we are not penitent,
And to show our enemies that they are no compari-
son.
So that they will know we are not amaranthine,
That we’ll fight with all our might for country and
King;
To show we are not made of bombazine,
That we will be glorious and show our breeding.
Now may the Allies show their high constancy,
And prove to their King their determination,
That we may know no more contingency—
That may our King live to act Discrimination.

TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC)
CORRESPONDENTS.
Jas. P.: Merely cheap smartness. .. .G.M.M.H.:
Tinny little figures. Why don’t the two society
sneerers say something clever about the extraordinary
left leg of the underdressed lady ?... .8.J.D.: It’s
wrong—-but not brilliantly wrong... .Clear as Mud:

idea is original there might be a copyright postcard
in it. Not suitable for us... .S.J.W.: Sketch too
crude. Study, if you’re interested.... 5.0'8.: One of
the old, obvious ideas... .D.W.M., Jr.: Where did

“Momokani”: While you’re talking of
scurvy treatment of returned wounded sol-
diers, what do you think of this? Here in
Papua, which is under Australian adminis-
tration, if a police officer gets injured on
duty, by spear or otherwise, he has to pay
his own hospital fees and doctor’s bill! If
a man in the service is killed, and any ex-
pense is incurred over his funeral, it is
charged against his estate —vide the Cura-
tor’s account of the estate of a patrol officer
who was speared to death a year or so ago.
If any further evidence were needed that
a ladical change is urgently called for in
the administration of the Defence Depart-
ment, it could be got from the relations of
John Wren, pony racer, with the ruling
powers. Wren has fought against war-time
reductions in his racing dates in more than
one of the States, and seems to be able to
win anywhere. A while ago the Department
brazenly informed the Q.T.C. that the Min-
ister had agreed to give John Wren three
/ rrwt extra . 1-at ‘i n g at Albion Park. The
Q.T.C. (which gives its profits to war funds,
whereas Wren gives his profits to himself)
objected to this new idea in war-winning,
and applied for extra dates on its own
account. And what is the reply? In the
words of The Argus :
It is surmised that it will be found that the
Uetence Department has agreed to give the Q.T.C.
two extra days if it will consent to Mr. Wren also
having two days.
That is to say, the Defence Department,
under Pearce’s control, will let the Q.T.C.
have something provided it won’t kick up a
row if the Minister lets John Wren have
something. Now. assuming that the Argus’s
surmise is true, how does John Wren, who
eaters for the slackers who won’t-win-the-
j:vvar. get such a pull over a Win-the-War
-^Ministry?

Government Notices.
Department of the Nary,
Melbourne, January 31, 1918.
Examination for Appointment as Naval
Staff Clerk, Class V., under the
Naval Defence Act 1910-1912.
ATTENTION is drawn to an advertise-
ment appearing in the Commonwealth
of Australia Gazette, inviting applica-
tions from persons to sit for examination for
appointment as Naval Staff Clerk, Class V.,
in the above Department. The examination
will be held toward the end of April, 1918.
2. The following are eligible to sit for
this examination:—
(a) Males who are members of, or have
Served with satisfactory record in any
Expeditionary Force raised under the
provisions of the Defence Act 1903-
1917, and members of the Naval
Forces who have been on active service
outside Australia, or who have served
on one of H.M.A. ships since the out-
break of war, and who will not have
attained tlpsir 51st birthday on Ist
May, 1918/'
(6) Other males who will not be less
than fifteen years or age, nor have
l’eached their 26th birthday on Ist
May, 1918.
3. AH applications must be on the proper
printed forms, and must reach the Naval
Secretary, Department of the Navy, not
later than 7th March, 1918.
4. Full particulars as to subjects of
examination, rates of pay, etc., can be
obtained from the Commonwealth of Aus-
tralia Gazette, or from the Naval Secre-
tary, Department of the Navy, Lonsdale
Street, Melbourne.
JOSEPH COOK,
Minister of State for the Navy.

“8.J.: Bob Greig, who for some time has
counted his embonpoint as a histrjionie
asset, lias been made an associate-director of
H. Rickards’ Tivoli Theatres, Ltd., and in
future will loom in the only way he can
loom in the management of vaudeville
affairs. Many bright lads will feel sorry if
Robert’s promotion means the withdrawal
from the stage of Mrs. Robert, who behind
the footlights is winsome Beatrice Hollo-
way.
MELBOURNE TIVOLI.
“WINNIE BROOKE, WIDOW.
Charles Albert as Hercules, and Harry
Jacobs as Reggie.
Peter Dawson, whose songs are on a
million gramophones, is going to be Aus-
tralia’s singing soldier. He enlisted last
week in Brisbane, but will fulfil brief Bris-
bane and Melbourne engagements before
going into Enoggera camp. A manly singer
of manly songs, he will delight brother Bill-
jims ashore or afloat, wherever he may hap-
pen to be. Home music-lovers will miss his
ringing, rousing voice; but Billjim has more
right to Peter than the mild civilian has.
Ted Tait, now homeward bound from New
York, has secured “De Luxe Annie,” an
alleged novelty which resembles “On Trial”
in structure and contains as many surprises
as “The Seven Keys to Baldpate.” Stowed
deeper in Tait’s bag are “The Firefly,” a
new musical comedy; “Old Lady 31,” a
quaint romance of middle-aged people; and
the sticky “Mother Carey’s Chickens,” which
is another poach from the preserves of Kate
Douglas Wiggin, who wrote “Mrs. Wiggs of
the Cabbage Patch.” Those pieces, with Guy
Bates Post and “The Invisible Foe.” an H.
B. Irving production which was bought in
London, will keep the young firm busy in
1918.
“Fax”: The first night of the Japanese
fantasy in Sydney was not the only occasion
of Kathleen Macdonell’s abrupt departure
from a stage. One night in Brisbane, when
she was playing the heroine of “Outcast,”
somebody slipped over a stair in the darkness
and a gallery girl tittered. It was just as
Miss Mac was refusing the hero’s offer of
marriage and volunteering to follow him to
the uttermost end of the earth without a
ring. The titter broke the spell of illusion
for the leading lady. She shrugged her shoul-
ders and left the stage. Curtain. Then a
man put his head out from the side and
announced: “Miss Macdonell says that unless
the noise discontinues she refuses to go on
with the play.” By that time .the man had
left off slipping and the woman had ceased
tittering, so the play went on.
John Hugo, one-time of Hugo’s Minstrels,
saw the various States from behind the foot-
lights in the late ’eighties ; and then he went
back to the boot trade. After that came a
strike. John was a union official in those
days, and when the trouble was over he
found himself unpopular with every boss in
the business. So he took service with the
A. A. Co., which controls most of Mel-
bourne’s hoardings, and he has held his billet
ever since—2l years of slapping beauty’s
face with a pastebrush and watching the
wind out of the tail of his eye. Now he has
established a record as long-distance member
of the Bill-posters’ Union, and the brethmn
gave him a gold medal for it the other day.
“B. J.” : The list of operas made in Aus-
tralia which have reached the footlights is
longer than the casual rememberer might ex-
pect. Alfred Hill has two previous efforts to
his credit, “The Moorish Maid” (book b$
John Birch, a Maoriland journalist) and
“Tapu” (book by Arthur Adams). At Syd
ney Cri., in 1902, tenor Clarence Leumane
and Ernest Truman offered, under the title
of “Mathias,” an operatic version of “The
Bells,” Clarence singing the title-role. A
pioneer was Luscombe Searelle, whose
“Estrella” was presented by Nellie Stewart
and the Royal Comics, and whose “Black
Rover” (later staged in London) and “Boba-
dil” were produced by Majeroni and Wilson.
Auguste Juncker, a J.C.W. conductor, had
to be content with amateur productions of
“Zelma,” “A Romance of Corsica” and “The
Queen’s Page.” Three operas which have
been produced in Maoriland are Carmini,
Morley’s “The Two Brides,” Fred W. Jones’s
“The. Monarch of Utopia,” and Squarise’s
“Fabian.” The most ambitious of all was
Marshall Hall’s “Stella,” which was pro-
duced for two matinees at Melbourne
Majesty’s in 1912, with Rosina Buckman
in the name part.
“Harrison O.”: The trouble with “The
Rajah of Shivapore” is that David Souter
has been too busy constructing little master-
pieces round black cats and bold hussies to
have conquered the art of the librettist. The
staging of the work will do good if only it
makes composers realise that to be successful
a comic opera must have something more
than melody. Local musicians with comic-
opera aspirations mostly suffer from the delu-
sion that the “book” is a matter of secondary
importance, which it certainly is not. Operas
like the Savoy series, in which music and
libretto dovetail perfectly, happen once in
centuries, but most of the big successes of
minor artistic importance, although now
best remembered by their tunes, have been
helped by smart writing. Instances are “The
Cingalee” and “Florodora” : one had Chedda
Lhoy and the little Babu lawyer, with his
quaintly mixed metaphors; the other reached
the ribs of audiences with a Tweedlepunch
and the . crisp epigrams of the gay widow.
Our Miss Gibbs,” although its tunes were
ground out bv every hurdy-gurdy in the city,
probably made its strongest appeal by means
ot its book. ’ Hughie Pierpoint, the ama-
teur crook,” was a delightfully humorous
ft oak. and Timothy Gibbs had dozens of
lines that could not fail to win a laugh.
Although some deplorable English lyrics
were provided for “The Merry YT mow,” the
opera’s well-sustained plot gave it interest,
and its jokes attracted playgoers to whom
all music is much of a mushiness. The Aus-
tralian composer who wishes to produce a
successful comic opera must secure a well-
euuipped librettist, and regard him not as an
incidental contributor but as an equal
partner;
“0.P.”: I got “the bird” years ago at
Charters Towers (Q.). We were a bellow-
drama co. trying to earn our board with a
sacred concert on Good Friday night. The
theatre contained a gallery full of loose
wooden forms capable of accommodating
2000 miners—if they arrived. While I
waited in the wings “The Holy City,” “Ora
Pro Nobis” and other solemnities were passed
out in ominous silence. I flattered myself
on a reasonably good bass voice and felt con-
fident I could wake ’em up with the popular
Fisk Jubilee hymn, “Steal Away.” I got
safely to the third line, “Steal Away Home,”
when a beery listener from aloft rumbled
“By , I will!” and then proceeded to
keep his word, clambering over those wooden
forms with his No. 12 bluchers. It finished
the sacred concert.
“Norbell”: Touching “Bowyang’s” request
for a concert party of Australian artists to
entertain Billjim in Palestine (B. 14/2/’18),
I quote a letter just to hand from Ted Scott,
who writes from “In the Desert” (Edward
is a stranger to me) :
I am a nursing orderly attached to No. 2 Stationary
Hospital, El Arish. Well, I gave your Bulletin
poem, “W’en are yer goin’ away?” one evening, and
ever since that, every time I appear, I am called on
to do it again. Seeing that it has been done solely
for the benefit of the boys that are doing their bit
out here, I knew you wouldn’t mind, and The
Bulletin's a cobber of ours. Everywhere the piece
takes the roof off. It is difficult "to put on new
pieces; but I’m kept pretty well supplied from The
Bulletins that float into camp.”

“Jwosp”: If big-voiced De Wolf Hopper
goes to Australia in Gilbert and Sullivan
stuff, it will be on a sort of trial run, Lon-
don being the goal. Hopper had a fling on
the London stage in ’99 with Sousa’s “El
Capitan” and “The Charlatan,” and was
kindly treated ; but he has never shown them
his G. and S. tricks. He is now 60 and his
stage jobs go back to “Our Boys” in ’7B.
His chief hits have been in comic opera, but
he has also' shown something beyond the
ordinary as Falstaff in “The Merry Wives.”
David in “The Rivals,” and “Mr. Pick-
wick.”

THE FILM FANATIC.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One time my Jane was very s.veet,
A gentle soul with smiling lips,
Who walked demurely on the street
Without a hint of ragtime hips.
She wished but for the simple life,
A quiet lot
In ivied cot
As Simon Simple’s simple wife.
My Jane for two distressing years
Has felt the living-picture lure.
Her wagging walk provokes the jeers
Of vulgar people, and I’m sure
They wouldn’t like her cigarette.
Her cheeks of rose
And powdered nose
I hate —I loathe the name Jeanette!
I must rain gold upon the poor,
And lift the fallen creature up,
As heroes do, the sick must cure,
And carry home the stranded pup.
And Jeanette when she wakes or dozes,
Or runs, or rides,
With skill provides
A choice array of picture poses.
I’m sending Jane to U.S.A.
On managers to try her schemes.
It is my pious prayer she may
Succeed beyond her wildest dreams
And be detained by fame and pelf.
’Twill best please me
Henceforth to see
The shadow of her former self!
VICTORIA.
SIMON SIMPLE.
THE CRITIC.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
VICTORIA.
He goes in on the free-list,
And sits in the D.C.;
The face of an idealist
Some people say has he.
Before him in arraignment.
As ’twere, those on the boards;
Never the entertainment
This blast* judge applauds.
His pose is of a critic
Omnipotent and stern ;
With gaze cute, analytic,
He’ll to the programme turn.
The hero moves him never,
The heroine in vain
Appeals. They must be clever
Who his approval gain!
At all the funny wheezes
He’ll sadly smile, and if
The house the comic pleases,
Quite audibly he’ll sniff.
He thinks the whole crowd duddy;
And surely he must know—
The young juve. understudy
Of the opposition show!
E. T.

car is involved.
It is obviously good judg-
ment to consider only
those cars whose real
value has been proved be-
yond question by every
known trial that could
have been given in every
country the world over,
through several seasons.
That over 300,000 Stude-
bakers are now in daily
use explains eloquently
how the public has ap-
proved Studebaker qual-
ity mechanism.

o

quantities.

Timken roller bearings

London's Beauty Writers.
Weekly Selections from Noted Beauty
Experts Writing tor the English Press.
Simple and Effective iMethods .
By Olga Amsdell, Special Correspondent,
London, Eng.
To Kill Roots of Superfluous Hair.
Women annoyed with disfiguring growths
of superfluous hair wish to know not merely
how to temporarily remove the hair, but how
to kill the hair roots permanently. For this
purpose pure powdered pheminol may he
applied directly to the objectionable hair-
growth. The recommended treatment is
designed not only to instantly remove the
hair, hut to actually kill the roots so that
the growth will not return. About an ounce
of pheminol. obtainable from the chemist,
should be sufficient.
Removing Her Skin.
The quickest and surest way to make a
good complexion out of a bad one is to
actually remove the thin veil of faded or dis-
colored outer skin from the face. This can
he done easily, safely and privately by any
woman. The process is one of gentle absorp-
tion.
Get some pure inercqlised wax front your
chemist and apply it at niglit like cold cream,
washing it off in the morning. In a few
days tiie inercolide in the- wax will have
absorbed the disfiguring cuticle, revealing
the fresh young skin underneath; you will
have a beautiful, clear, natural complexion.
-The process is pleasant and harmless, making
the face look brilliantly attractive and youth-
ful. It also effectually removes such blem-
ishes as freckles, tan, moth patches, liver
spots, pimples, etc. Every woman should
keep a little mercolised wax on hand, as this
simple, old-fashioned remedy is the best coin-
plexioned beautifier and preserver known.
Blackheads Instantly Go.
A very simple, harmless and pleasant pro-
cess is now used to remove blackheads and
correct greasiness and large pores in the
skin. You have only to drop a tablet of
stymol, obtained from the chemist, into a
glass of hot water and bathe the face with
the liquid after the effervescence has sub-
sided. The blackheads will then come right
off on the towel. The enlarged pores immedi-
ately contract to normal and the greasiness
disappears, leaving the skin smooth, soft and
cool and free from blemish. But to make sure
that this desirable result is permanent it is
advisable to repeat, the treatment several
times at intervals of say about four or five
days.
Grey Hair Unnecessary.
One need not resort to the very question-
able expedient of hair dye in order not to
have grey hair. The grey hair can easily he
changed back to a natural color in a few
days’ time merely by the application of a
simple, old-fashioned and perfectly harmless
home-made lotion. Procure from your chem-
ist two ounces of tammalite concentrate and
mix it with three ounces of bay rum. Apply
this to the hair a few times with a small
sponge, and you will soon have the pleasure
of seeing your grey hair gradually darkening
to the desired shade. The lotion is pleasant,
not sticky or greasy, and does not injure the
hair in any way.
Naturally Wavy Hair.
Good stallax not only makes the best possi-
ble shampoo, but has the peculiar property of
leaving the hair with a pronounced natural
“wave,” an effect presumably desired by many
ladies. A teaspoonful of the stallax granules,
dissolved in a cup of hot water, is ample for
a luxurious head wash, and leaves the hair
with a peculiar lustre and fluffiness obtainable
from nothing else as far as known. It is per-
fectly harmless, and can be obtained from
most chemists, although since it has been but
little used for this purpose it comes only in
lib. sealed original packages, enough to make
up 25 or 30 individual shampoos. [2

“T. 0.1.”: The grandfather of “Reggy”
Black (B. 7/2/TS) was not the only notable
shipmaster who sailed out of Sydney Harbor
for the China Seas and lost the numoer of
his mess. In the first ’forties Captain John
Bibbs, of Upper Fort-street, father of the
notable brothers J.C., Tom and George,
sailed in command of a ship owned by
Campbell of the Wharf for China, and was
not heard of again.
Young backblock Australia won’t have to
go into a camp to learn the ways of the
humped beast. The other day, for instance,
Chris Smith, a veteran of 17, arrived in
Quilpil (Q.) in charge of eight camels to
load with stores for Nocatunga. They found
the 100-mile journey in bone-dry ; but going
back the team won’t perish of thirst, if it’s
water they want.
“Myola” on art-teaching in Victoria: —
Max Meldrum is objected to by “8.J.” (B.
24/1 /’IS) as a candidate for the late Fred McCubbin’s
Melbourne National Gallery billet. He quotes
Meldruni’s coo-scientific attitude towards Art as a bar
tc his appointment. Let it be remembered that the
vacant position is that of drawing master. Much
sloppy drawing would be averted if perspective were
always taught to students as an exact science. Tf
ability to impart his enthusiasm and energy and
his instinctive feeling for mass and form count for
anything, Meldrum is the obvious drawing master,
liis portrait of his mother in the Gallery speaks
with more effect than his detractors. Although
McCubbin was the drawing instructor, lie ranked
much higher as an interpretive colorist than as a
draftsman. Conversely, Director Bernard Hall, who
takes the color classes himself, excels in the technique
of line, but usually fails in the medium of paint.
It is to be hoped that whoever is appointed will
try to obtain more comely female models than were
used last year. Some of the women depicted in
the recent exhibition of students’ work were deplor-
able. A couple of the emaciated creatures looked
fitter subjects for the Melbourne Hospital next door.
Thus is youth’s aesthetic eye offended when there are
thousands of young goddesses who might be given
some permanence in paint. Droves of them flock to
display their lovely limbs as chorus girls; I wish a
few would rush the model’s throne.
“0.K.” : What is the age of the ironical
cockneyism, “I don’t think”? It seemed quite
new to Australia when Bert Gilbert used it
in a panto, a few years ago. He and a bro-
ther low-comedian were discussing a Mrs.
Drinkwater. “Mrs. Drinkwater!” he said
sniffingly—“l don’t think.” The expression
was then coming into vogue again, and, being
a popular revival, it has stayed in. You find
it in “Pickwick Papers,” published more than
80 years ago. Sam Weller, being deputed by
Mr. Pickwick to recover the fugitive Mr.
Winkle, upbraids the nervous runaway:
“You’re a’ amiably-disposed young man, sir, I
don’t think.” Probably it was a common
form of humorous expression in the age of
Weller. Dean Swift’s “Polite Conversa-
tion,which dates back to 1736, satirised the
phrasing of the small-talk in upper-middle-
class London sassiety of that period, and
hundreds of the household proverbs and
similes in Swift’s satire are stock phrases
to-day. All things considered, it seems pro-
bable that when Eve had a few words with
-vdam at the close of the honeymoon, she
tossed her golden mane and snorted .
“Everything in the Garden’s lovelv—l don’t
think.” •
“64-Pounder”: Squatting on a shoulder of
Mt. Victoria, Wellington (M.L.), 600 ft.
above the sea, there is an ancient cannon of
the Crimean War type—a big iron muzzle-
loader weighing a couple of tons or so. The
weapon was man-hauled up there in 1574 or
1876 by a battery of artillery under Major
McCready. They took a week at the precipi-
tous job, working with block and tackle and
ways. For several years the blunderbuss
went bang at noon on Saturdays; now it.sits
dumb, and the youth of Wellington cuddles
his tartlet on its well-polished breech.
The chemist’s yarn :
Industrial depression re-acts very quickly on our
business ; when things get tight people curtail adver-
tised mixtures and stick to Epsom salts and other
cheap remedies. The same thing applies to the
luxury of having the doctor. Victorian lodge doctors
were rushed all day on January 31, and we had to
put on extra hands ; on February 1 dispensaries and
consulting-rooms were alike empty. One of the most
humorous things in the whole doctor-lodge controversv
is the burthen of the circular which most of the
friendly societies are sending out. For example:—
“Any visits to doctors except for reasons of great
urgency will not be recognised by the branch.’’
“You are. requested for the time being to seek
medical advice only in cases where it is absolutely
imperative to do so. The greater the number of visits
you pay to the doctor the more you may be levied at
the end of the quarter.”
Where you feel in doubt it is advisable that you
nrst consult your secretary.”
“Do not seek medical advice for minor and unim-
portant ailments, as each visit to the doctor will now
cost your branch 10s. fid.”
It looks as though it will be necessary for lodges
to appoint a vigilante, who will decide whether the
sick member is sick enough to risk 10s. 6d. A local
doctor whose average number of lodge patients in his
afternoon consulting hours had been 28 up to January
31 now finds he has to attend 7J. And he swears
that that is about a fair average number in any real
need of attention.
“J.C.” : About time Maoriland authorities
followed S. Australia’s example and strafed
the Hun names off the map. In the Southern
Alps there are such names as the TJnser
Iritz Waterfall, the Moltke Range and the
Ivron Prinz Rudolf Glacier, all handy to the
big 4 ranz Josef Glacier. Not far away are
the Bismarck Peaks. It would be' easy
enough to find fitting substitutes in English
or Maori. The original abo. name of the
4 ranz Josef iceflow is Waiau, which means
swirling waters,” applied to the mad little
liver that issues from a cave in the glacier
ace.. . There might be reasonable excuse for
letaimng such names as Ilochstetter and
Ilaast, and. the Mueller glacier, the last-
named, for instance, having been christened
a..ter a Government surveyor of the pioneer
rays, an old pal of Dick Seddon’s, who
escaped from Hunland early. He’s still
above ground, up Auckland way.

Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort and good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Post free. 1/3. Free sample posted.
