The Boothby ballot and the Torrens Title
Acts, the two reforms which lifted South
Aus. into world-notice, were, by a coinci-
dence, assented to by Governor Macdonnell
on the same day—January 27, ISSB.
There’s one corner of the N. S. Wales
State Electoral Division of King that has
no further use for the recruiting-sergeant.
Lord Howe Island —400 miles east from
Sydney; population 100 —has sent along all
its eligibles. Nine; and one h-as made the
Big Sacrifice.
Sliih-Yuan Lin, who recently arrived at
Wellington (M.L.) as Consul, is a great-
grandson of the famous Commissioner Lin
Tsze-Su, who in 1839 spilled some ship-
loads of British opium into Canton Harbor.
Out of the racket that followed England got
Hongkong.
Half the trouble that has struck 8010
Pasha, whose cash and paper transactions
are now being investigated in France, would
have been avoided had he paid more atten-
tion to the hymn he ought to have learned
at his mother’s knee: “Man wants but little
here, 8010, nor wants that little long.”
From a Sydney morning daily:—
Working Man wants Lodgings, no meals, with
Englishman's or widow’s family, of laboring class,
handy to city, single room, or share only with son
or relative or landlady. Particulars and terms
(including soft washing) to . William-st.,
D’hurst.
An accommodating young man.
“Centurion”: The ban on lighting three
cigarettes at one match is much older than
the present war. I ran across it in South
Africa years ago. The superstition may
have arisen from the old one concerning
three lights in a room —in Scotland regarded
as certainly presaging sudden death to one
of those present.
A person who gets on one’s nerves is the
would-be fashionable female with a new
baby, who advertises herself as “fjee Eliza-
beth Fitzherbert.” Any woman may be
born Fitzherbert or Briggs or Sloper, but
she becomes Gladys or Gwendolen afterwards.
In the interests of the British language the
dailies should refuse Elizabeth Fitzherbert’s
ad.
“Ibsenshaw”: Russia has (or had) a
Union of Dramatic Authors, which associa-
tion is (or was) in the habit of holding an
annual open play-competition. The year
before the war the result of the contest
seemed to show that mighty Russia was
suffering badly from the blues. Comedy was
almost completely lacking in the pieces sent
in. With hardly an exception the subjects
chosen were gloomy to the last degree. The
embryo Dostoieffskys fairly out-Dostcieff-
skyed the master. Murder, suicide or mortal
disease figured in all but five of the plays.
Twenty per cent, of the writings, by the way,
were characterised by absolute illiteracy. One
wonders what a similar competition held in
this year of grace would bring forth !
From the Office Archdeacon: —
Anglicanism at ’Ome has been in convulsions —all
over the nomination of Dean Hensley Henson to bish
at Hereford (Eng.). Those Anglicans who like to
regard themselves as members of a church, not of a
State department, have squirmed for years under the
system by which bishops in the Cold Country are
appointed by the Prime Minister. One theory by
which they try to comfort themselves is that such
appointments are made, not by the Prime Minister,
but by “our most religious King,” who is Supreme
Governor of the Church and an “ecclesiastical per-
son” by virtue of the sanctified grease dabbed on him
at his coronation. This argument doesn’t convince,
because the King always “chooses” the man “recom-
mended” by the Government. The next wriggle in
which refuge is sought is that the King doesn't
“appoint” the bishops, but conveys to the “chapter”
(i.e., the dean and canons of the diocese) his “per-
mission to elect” a certain person ; and the chapter
then plays the solemn farce of “electing” his nominee.
But as the chapter never dreams of electing anyone
else, and would be subject to unknown penalties
under the Statute of Praemunire if it did, this won’t
wash either.
The two English archgaiters appointed a committee
some time ago to see how this and similar grievances
could be avoided ; and certain recommendations were
made for seeking from Parliament greater freedom
for the Church in managing its own affairs. But
these recommendations didn’t go far enough for the
Bolshevik Anglicans, and last year they formed a
revolutionary movement, with the slogan “Life and
Liberty for the Church.’:’ William Temple, who made
a lecturing tour of Australian Universities before
the war, gave up his cushy job at St. James’s, Picca-
dilly, to organise the revolt. Temple is a son of a
' former Canterbury starchbishop, and a leader of the
Anglican “intellectuals.” Official Anglicanism is
always afraid that if too much fuss about liberty is
made, Parliament will get tired and break the fetters
by disestablishing the Church and taking away its
endowments; but the Bolshevik Anglicans loudly
clamored: “If establishment stands in the way of
liberty, give us disestablishment.” “Prave words,”
thought Lloyd George, the Welsh hardshell Baptist
who as Prime Minister now appoints Anglican Bishops,
and lately disestablished the Church in Wales: “I
wonder if there’s anything in them.” So he “called”
their bluff by nominating Hensley Henson, Dean of
Durham, to wear the vacant gaiters at Hereford.
Of all ecclesiastics in England, Henson is perhaps
the most obnoxious to “Highs” and “Lows” alike;
ind so the fun began. The first move was a frenzied
appeal to the Hereford chapter to be sports and
chance the penalties of Praemunire by refusing to
elect him. But of course the docile chapter accepted
George V.’s “permission” and elected Henson. Organ-
ised labor in the vineyard then took a hand by
declaring Henson black; and the Parsons’ Trade
Protection Society (it calls itself the English Church
Union) lodged a protest against the election. The
protest was not received, probably on the ground
that the Union was bogus as not including a majority
of workers in the industry. Dr. Gore, who bishes
at Oxford, next appealed to the Canterbury arch-
gaiters to veto Henson’s election on the ground of
heresy ; but the Canterbury Primate made the vague
remarks usual with Anglican administrators in such
matters, and declined to act. So Henson is to wear
his mitre, and the “Life and Liberty” crowd will
have to show whether they meant it when they
shrieked for freedom even at the price of disestablish-
ment. The “establishment” is the only thing that
keeps the perpetually squabbling “Highs” and “Lows”
together as nominally members of the same church;,
and once disestablishment comes, Anglicanism will
inevitably split up into at least two, and perhaps
three, sects.

it be an old one which has been troubling you for many months or years, or whether it is one
of only recent date, which refuses to heal, you should give it prompt and careful attention.

The Holy Piper.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
T\LACK snakes, green snakes, hear the
i J pipes and understand!
7J Long snakes, small snakes, all the
snakes in Ireland.
Holy water, holy sod,
Shamrock leaf and the cross of God
Padraig pipes below his hand
To all the snakes in Ireland.
Loudly on the summer air
Rang the chapel’s call to prayer,
Brazen notes that shook the bees
In white blossoms under trees,
While along the river bank
Danced the rushes rank on rank,
As if fairy troops rode by,
Nodding cap and winking eye-
So the bell’s vibrations went,
Making holy discontent.
Rose the people two and two,
Walking soft the green world through
To the chapel where this day
Padraig Saint would come to pray.
Rose the herdsman grey and old,
Rose th.e prince with chains of gold,
Flashed the sail across the water,
Beat the hooves on turf and grass;
And O’Brien’s fairest daughter
Heard the quiet people pass.
“All is well, all is well,”
Sang the rolling chapel bell.
“Come to prayer, come to prayer,”
Echo whispered on the air.
Would she hear or would she heed
Saint or bell or book or bead?
Surely she was very fair
With a red rose in her hair!
Down the way a man would walk
Glowed the woodbine on its stalk,
AVliere a twisted apple tree
Stood in brambles to the knee—
It was there that she would go,
Ankle-white and pink of toe.
“All is well, all is well,”
Sang the rolling chapel bell.
Surely she was very fair
With a red rose in her hair!
Bees about the briers hum
Down the way a man would come;
And a viper coiling there,
Angered, heard the call to prayer.
Ankle-white and pink of toe,
Would O'Brien’s daughter know—
She with lips a man should kiss —-
Fang and crest and warning hiss?
Death below the briers green
Struck and slipped away unseen.
Down the road a man would come
Bees were still and death was dumb;
And the bell upon the air
Ceased its rolling call to prayer.
Came the people two and two
Walking back the green world through.
Padraig Saint, with beard of snow,
Blessed them ere they turned to go.
Pale and cold and very still
Lay Aideen and slept her fill.
Curse the worm and all his brood
Over grass or under wood,
Venom creeping through the day
While the people bend to pray !
Padraig, holy saint, came by,
Saw the folk and heard their cry.
Padraig Saint below his hand
Played a pipe along the land;
Up and up the hill he went
In a holy discontent.
Snake in wood and grass and wall
Heard afar the music call.
Night fell black on tree and sod,
Shamrock leaf and cross of God.
Snake and adder must obey
words the pipes will say.
Wriggle, wriggle, twine and twist —
Adders in the mountain mist!
Fang and scale beneath the moon —
The holy piper makes the tune;
And all from Moyle to Kerry neater
'Tis wriggle and craicl to the western bay,
For the snake that bit O'Brien’s daughter
Must answer the call of Judgment Day.
Two white horns were on the moon
Looking light to aid the tune.
Up and up the hill he went
In a holy discontent.
Wriggle, wriggle, writhe and twist,
Climbed the snakes through mountain mist;
Wriggle, wriggle, twist ‘and twine
They were there at morning-shine.
Padraig Saint, upon the hill,
Stayed his hand and laughed his fill —
“Now, by holy crook and cross,
Ireland’s gain’s the Devil’s loss!
I have tunes and tunes to spare—
Make your will and say your prayer—
There’s a sup of merry sea
Calling salt and blue to me.”
Black snakes, green snakes, hear the pipes
and understand !
Long snakes, small snakes, all the snakes in
Ireland !
Holy water, holy sod,
Shamrock leaf and the cross of God —
Padraig pipes below his hand
To all the snakes in Ireland.
Looked the saint to sea and sand,
Took the road the snakes must go.
Down the hill across the land
Rocks and bushes heard him blow.
Och, the pipes were summer-sweet,
Blossoms opened round his feet,
Vipers wriggled, adders cradled.
And the music called and called
Down the land across the sea,
“Follow, follow, follow me!”
On the dancing waves afloat
Padraig found a holy boat.
In they went, and on they went,
Through the waves they coiled and bent,
Took the water, left the sand,
All the snakes of Ireland.
Wriggle, wriggle, twist and twine —
When was music made so fine?
Sure, the sea was kind and wide—
Padraig's piping turned the tide.
Now, sirs, swim—God bless deep water —
You that nipped O’Brien’s daughter!
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
A Person of duality.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MACQUARIE left “his Excellency the
Captain-General and Governor-in-
Chief in and over His Majesty’s
Territory of New South Wales” outside the
door of Mrs. Macquarie’s bedroom, ana
became the very human Scottish gentleman
whom that dear lady found so much ditii
culty in dissociating from the stern and un-
bending autocrat ruling the Colony in a
fashion that no king has ever been permitted
to rule England. Not a little -to his embar-
rassment, the good woman frequently made
the mistake of assuming that his Excellency
was the same person as her dear Lachlan.
He was tired, weary and infinitely bored.
When he flung himself into a straight-backed
armchair of the fashion of the last Louis
but one, his wife turned a smiling, mischiev-
ous face to him, as she sat before her mirror
twisting her shining hair into two thick
braids, one of which, hanging over her shoul-
der, stood out darkly against the whiteness
of her nightdress. There was a sympathetic
twinkle in her deep brown eyes as she spoke
to him.
“My dear” she said, with a little laugh,
“what a man! What a terrible creature!
Dear me, what have we done to Lord
Bathurst?”
Macquarie groaned.
“Elizabeth —the fellow will be the death
of me,” he sighed so mournfully that her
laughter rippled unrestrained, and her
pretty shoulders shook with it. “He is pre-
posterous—pre-posterous!”
“ ‘The aim and object of the embryonic
state,’ ” quoted Mrs. Macquarie maliciously,
“is— ah — the — ah—improvement of its—ah—
citizens, my dc-ah Governor.’ Oh, Lachlan,
what a droll creature he is!”
“Droll, my dear! Droll? Deplorable,
unendurable —exasperating ! Hardly droll.
I find little that is amusing in Mr. Main-
waring-Coutts. Very little, I assure you.”
“And will he make a long stay, do you
think?”
“He announced his intention this evening
of remaining here until he has acquired sutti
cient material to write a book about ‘Our
Felon Dependency.’ A book, my love!
Imagine that dreary fellow’s book! Every-
one in Sydney and the several settlements
will fill him up with outrageous stories and
personal grievances. Already he has been
presented with seven or eight memorials to
Government, and at least two petitions to
the House of Commons. It is the first time
that we have been visited by a member of
Parliament, and the inhabitants, both bond
and free, positively revel in bringing the
strangest and most singular affairs beneath
his notice. They trade upon his vanity and
his insatiable curiosity. They sell him
things. Rubbish. They fill him up with the
most amazing lies.”
Mrs. Macquarie laughed merrily.
“Oh, yes, indeed, my dear. Do you know,
he showed me to-day a snuffbox he had pur-
chased from that old rogue Danny Myle-
charane, our gardener. Danny was at work
on it in the evenings all last summer, and
he often showed it me. It is certainly a
pretty little piece of carving—some of these
people are extraordinarily ingenious. Quite
artistic. And he has inlaid it with what he
calls ivory, but which are really fragments
of chicken bones that he begged from the
cook. And, do you know, the old villain sold
it to Mr. Mainwaring-Coutts as having
belonged to Bonaparte! Dannv told him
that he had himself picked it up upon the
field of Waterloo, where he was with the
Guards. The idea! Why, Danny was trans-
ported here in Mr. King’s time. Positively
—and I know I did wrong—l had not the
heart to give Danny away. Seven guineas,
no less, he asked for it. Have you ever
heard of such a thing, my dear Macquarie?”
The Governor smiled grimly. He could
bear to hear such a tale told against the
British M.P. who was perforce his guest at
Government House —though it would have
gone ill with Mr. Daniel Mylecharane had
ho been guilty of such an impudent imposition
upon one of Macquarie’s personal friends.
“Well, well,” he said, “the fool deserves
such treatment. Why, Captain Piper told
me that he had caught one of his men—one
of the crew of the Naval Officers’ gig—in a
similar transaction. The fellow had cut four
feet off the boat’s painter, and had sold it to
our guest as part of the rope used in the
first execution that ever took place in the
Territory. Two guineas. Piper made the
man hand the money over to the Orphan
Fund, and sent him back to the gaol-gang,
with an order for twenty-five lashes, but.
like yourself, he could not bring himself to
inform Mr. Mainwaring-Coutts as to the
real nature of the transaction. ‘A fool and
his folly,’ Piper said, and I entirely agreed
with him.”
“Do you think he will stay with us for all
those months? My dear Macquarie, the
prospect is simply appalling ! How can we
endure the man for so long—Lord Bathurst
or no Lord Bathurst?”
“Well, well, my love,” said Macquarie
after a little hesitation. “I have—er—taken
means this evening—hardly justifiable
means, perhaps—to discourage the admira-
tion and interest which Mr. Mainwaring-
Coutts evinces in the social life of our com-
munity. I almost fear for the results —but I
was goaded to it. You know young Calder,
of the 46th?”
“Oh, the bad Mr. Calder? Yes. Lachlan.
I know him. from rumor, as a very wicked
and depraved young man—but one for whom
I confess I cannot help feeling a little
liking. You know, your rogues always
appeal to a woman. Yes—what of him? I
was rather, glad that you had him here to
dinner to-night. I believe that there is good
in that young man. my love. I do really ”
“ '7 s ? , . most skilfully hidden. then ”
growled his Excellency. “Well, you must
know I took Colonel Molle into my confi-
dence. He is quite as well satiated with
(Continued on next pane.)

this noodle as I am. Mainwaring-Coutts has
the audacity to lecture him on the interior
economy of the regiment, if you please. Molle
is furious—simply fuming. You know how
he broods. Had the man not been a guest
here, he would have kicked him out of
barracks, he says.”
“Yes, yes—and what have you done? I
am all impatience, Mr. Macquarie.”
“Mr. Mainwaring-Coutts has professed
himself eager to see something of the shady
side of Sydney. As though it were not all
shade—with here and there a gleam and
promise of the light to come! He spoke of
it as we sat over our wine, after you and
the ladies had withdrawn. I noticed a
gleam of interest in the Colonel’s glance at
him. He waited until the great man had
finished—you know his insufferable verbosity
—and then he turned to young Calder. ‘Mr.
Calder,’ says he—and I distinctly saw him
wink —‘do you not think you could show
Mr. Mainwaring-Coutts something of the
night-life of the town ?’ ‘I don’t know very
much of it myself, sir,’ says that shameless
young prevaricator, ‘but I daresay I could
put Mr. Mainwaring-Coutts in the way of
seeing something of it. I know people who
are really intimately acquainted with it—
and of course I would accompany Mr. Main-
waring-Coutts to see that no harm should
come to him. Delighted, sir,’ he says, and
he had the infernal audacity to wink at
Molle. Molle remarked to me as he said
good-night, and our guest walked off to Mr.
Calder’s quarters with him: ‘I have every
confidence in Mr. Calder, your Excellency—
in this kind of thing, at any rate. He will
astonish the statesman.’ So there you are,
my _ love. It is just possible that, after
to-night, Mr. Mainwaring-Coutts, M.P., may
desire to return home at an early oppor-
tunity. The Lady Charlotte sails on the
15th. May we dare to hope?”
“Oh, I trust no hann will come to the
poor man,” laughed the Governor’s lady.
“Come to bed, Mr. Macquarie—’tis nigh to
midnight.”
,No harm will come to him, my dear
Elizabeth. We will see to that. After all,
he has his Lordship’s introduction, and he is
a Person of Quality.”
* * * *
The Person of Quality expounded his
views regarding the disreputable recreations
of Sydney by night to Ensign Patrick Calder
as the two of them walked down the hill and
across the Tank Stream on their way to Mr.
Calder’s quarters at the Barracks. He
found a polite and deferential audience in
the subaltern officer.
, A s you are doubtless aware, my dear
sir,” said Mr. Mainwaring-Coutts, “I—ah
have the honor, the distinguished honor, of
representing Great Miggleton in his Majes-
ty s—ah—faithful Commons, and I have—
ah—made it my business to become thor-
oughly intimate with social conditions in
that important borough.” (Actually, there
were 31 persons in Great Miggleton entitled
to exercise the franchise.)
“Most interesting, no doubt, sir,” mur-
mured civil Mr. Calder, who was busily
passing in review the various forms in which
enlightenment might come to the member for
Great Miggleton concerning the depravity of
Sidney. lie took it, in his simple way,
that depravity was what Mr. Mainwaring-

“Doubtless; doubtless. With so hetero-

• —ah—desire to investigate.”
“Oh, yes. My friend Gregory Wicks, you
know, sir. A lieutenant in the Navy. He’s
pretty well up in all this business. Sailors
get to know these places and people, don’t
they ? Not quite such slaves of respecta-
bility as we are in the Army, eh?”
If Patrick Calder was a slave of respecta-
bility, his C. 0., for one, would have been
keenly interested in studying a person who
was freely and frankly a thrall of vice.
I think we’ll find Mr. Wicks in my quar-
ters, sir,” went on Mr. Calder. “He some-
times drops in on his way down to the Cove,
for a for a dish of tea, and a little improv-
ing conversation. Seafaring men have so
few opportunities for that, haven’t they?”
‘Quite so. . Quite so. I —ah —rejoice
that you find it congenial, my dear sir, con-
genial—ah—to endeavor to uplift your young
contemporaries in the—ah—sistah service.
I hey have not your opportunities, as you so
justly remark. Ah —-here we are. The
Barrack Gate, I take it.”
Mr. Gregory Wicks—him they called
“Pansy” after the Gosport girl—was shuf-
fling a pack of cards in Mr. Calder's room,
as that gentleman and his important charge
entered it from the verandah. It was
obvious that Mr. Wicks had dined and
wined without any stint. His blue eyes
were bright and merry and the deep tan of
his complexion was a little deeper for the
rosy flush of alcohol.
“Damme, Patsy, I thought ye were never
coming. Who’s y’r pot-teflied little friend?”
The small but important figure of Mr.
Mainwaring-Coutts stiffened, and he glow-
ered at the naval officer.
“Insolent!” he said. “I would have you
know, sir, that I am a member of—ah—the
House of Commons. Member for Great
Miggleton, sir.”
“Glad to meet ye, Mister Miggles—put it
there. But I don’t care to-night if ye was
a Port-Admiral. I’m feelin’ brave to-
night, Patrick.”
Mr. Wicks took the fat paw of the mem-
ber for Miggleton in a hard and vice-like
grip, and wrung it aifectionately.
“What’s it to be, Patsy? Did ye bring
him along for a little flutter with the paste-
boards—or shall we make it the dice?”
“Not to-night, Pansy my blood. Mr. Main-
waring-Coutts, Mr. Gregory Wicks. No,
this gentleman is writing a book on the
colony, and he wants you to show him Syd-
ney by night. Where do you think we ought
to go first?”
“Oh, thaPs it, is it!” Mr. Wicks con-
sidered a moment. “Grog, or girls—or cock-
fightin’?” he inquired.
“Everything. What do you say to The
Spotted Dog for a start? Things should be
moving down there by this time.”
“Well, Tim Naylan’s is a quiet house—
to some we know of. But we might go
there first. No harm in that. There’ll be
one or two people Mr. Miggleton-Scott might
like to meet —the lovely Mrs. Devine and
the sprightly Kitty Levi, eh? We could
go on, if that’s too slow, up to Black Tom
Nutting’s—The Good Intent on the Rocks.
I’ll warrant the company leaves nothing to
be desired at Black Tom’s place. How d’ye
say, Patrick?”
“I’m with you, my boy. Give Mr. Main-
wanng-Coutts a drink, Pansy, me lad, while
I change out of these regimentals. You’ll
excuse me a moment, sir? Shan’t be a jiffy.”
“Right you are, Patrick. What’s your
tipple, Mr. Scootleton? Pat Calder lias a
catholic taste in liquor. Here’s rum—the
best Navy, same as they pickled Lord Nelson
in—brandy, bottled beer and cheap Oporto.
Cheap but effective. Name your fancy, my
buck. We’ll make a night of it. I like your
face. Not so handsome as my own, perhaps
but not a bad face at all. Here’s luck to
your countenance, sir.”
* * * *
Of the events of that most torrid night in
Mr. Mainwaring-Coutts’s career it would be
interesting and edifying to tell the tale. Mr.
Calder, however, remembers nothing that
happened after 2 a.m., whilst Mr. Wicks
only has an impression of having spent an
intensely amusing early morning, and of
having rendered a great personal service to
Governor Macquarie, Colonel Molle and the
community of Sydney. Mr. Mainwaring-
Coutts has a clear and painful recollection
ot the night s proceedings, but a shuddering
distaste for their recital.

vant, Private Michael Muldoon.

realise the importance of EFFICIENTLY insulating

can be effected in this direction, in some cases the coal

Carbonate of Magnesia is to-day recognised as the best
non-conductor of heat. NewaU s Coverings are com-
posed of 85% of this material, and 15% Asbestos—the
Asbestos being used for binding purposes only—and in
addition possess innumerable advantages over all other
forms of lagging. Newall’s Coverings are used, practi-
cally exclusively, by the British Admiralty, N.S.Yv.
nnd Victorian Governments, and have been used
throughout the Ultimo. White Bay, Melbourne, I erth
and Canberra Power Stations.
